The Hadith of Seeing Allah and the Prophet's Response
Maturidi presents a hadith and defends questioning about vision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Prophet's gentle response to the question about seeing Allah to argue that asking about divine vision is permissible and that vision is affirmed.

Core Idea
The Prophet's gentle answer shows the question was not about literal sight but about knowledge.

Maturidi presents a key hadith that believers will see Allah on the Day of Resurrection. He then addresses a potential objection: the Prophet was asked if he had seen his Lord and replied 'with my heart.' Some claimed the question itself was disbelief. Maturidi argues that the Prophet's calm, non-rebuking response proves the question was not about literal vision and that inquiring about divine vision is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the objection
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the hadith about seeing Allah like the moon and the question directed to the Prophet.
Pay attention to the phrase 'with my heart'.
2
Identify the objection
Understand that some argued the question about seeing Allah amounts to disbelief, citing Quranic warnings against asking certain things.
The objection is based on the assumption that the question concerned literal physical sight.
3
Follow Maturidi's reasoning
Notice how Maturidi contrasts the expected rebuke with the Prophet's gentle reply, concluding that the question was not about literal sight but about the concept of vision.
The key point: the Prophet's behavior shows the question was not forbidden.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that this passage affirms the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) and permits discussing it, contrary to those who deem it disbelief.
This is a central argument in Maturidi's theology on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا ما جاء عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في غير خبر أنه قال : سترون ربكم يوم القيامة كما ترون القمر لا تضامون . وسئل : هل رأيت ربك ؟ فقال : بقلبي . قيل : فلم ينكر على السائل السؤال . وقد علم السائل أن العلم ، وأنه قد علمه ، وأنه لم يسأل عن ذلك ، وقد حذر الله عز وجل المؤمنين عن السؤال عن أشياء قد كفوا عنها بقوله : يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لا تَسْأَلُوا عَنْ أَشْيَاءَ ، فكيف يحتمل أن يكون السؤال عن مثله بحى، وذلك كفر في الحقيقة عند قوم ، ثم لا ينهاهم عن ذلك . ولا يوبخهم في ذلك. بل يلين القول في ذلك ، ويرى أن ذلك لبس ببديع ، والله الموفق
And also what has come from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in a report other [than the above] that he said: 'You will see your Lord on the Day of Resurrection as you see the moon, you will not be harmed [by crowding].' And he was asked: 'Have you seen your Lord?' He replied: 'With my heart.' It was said [by others]: 'Then why did he not rebuke the questioner for the question? Indeed, the questioner knew that knowledge [of Allah is by the heart], and that he [the Prophet] had knowledge of Him, and that he did not ask about that [i.e., literal sight]. And Allah, Exalted and Mighty, has warned the believers against asking about things they have been forbidden concerning His saying: 'O you who believe, do not ask about things' (5:101). So how could it be possible that the question about something like this is considered disbelief in reality according to some people, and then he [the Prophet] did not forbid them from that? And he did not rebuke them for that. Rather, he spoke gently in that matter and considered that it was not something strange. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known hadith that believers will see Allah on the Day of Resurrection as clearly as they see the moon, without crowding. Then it addresses a potential objection: the Prophet was asked if he had seen his Lord, and he replied 'with my heart'. The questioner (the person asking) knew that the heart's knowledge is the true knowledge, and the Prophet had that knowledge, so the question was not about literal physical sight. Why then did the Prophet not rebuke the questioner, especially since Allah warned against asking about certain things? Some argued that asking about vision is equivalent to disbelief, but the Prophet did not rebuke or forbid them; rather, he answered gently and considered the question not outlandish. This indicates that the question about the possibility of vision is permissible, and that vision itself is affirmed.

القمر
لا تضامون
بقلبي
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet's answer 'with my heart' denies the possibility of physical vision of Allah.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the question was not about literal sight but about the heart's knowledge, and the Prophet's gentle response shows that asking about vision is not disbelief.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi infer from the Prophet's gentle response to the question about seeing Allah?
A) The question was about literal vision and should be rejected
B) The question was not about literal vision, so the Prophet did not rebuke
C) The Prophet was too kind to rebuke
D) The question was forbidden but the Prophet ignored it

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's refusal to rebuke the questioner about seeing Allah proves that asking about divine vision is permissible and that vision itself is affirmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you ever judge others for asking 'dangerous' questions about Allah? After reading this lesson, will you reconsider your reaction and seek to understand the intent behind the question, as the Prophet did?
?
Have you ever avoided discussing the vision of Allah because you assumed it was too controversial or beyond your understanding? What would it take for you to engage with such a topic with the same openness that Maturidi defends?

Vision as a Higher Reward: Intellectual Goodness vs. Sensory Pleasure
Maturidi argues that vision of Allah is a greater reward than sensory pleasures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the beatific vision is a reward superior to physical pleasures because it is an intellectual and spiritual honor.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is the ultimate intellectual reward, surpassing all sensory delights.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a rational argument for why the vision of Allah must be the greatest reward in the afterlife. Maturidi distinguishes between goodness by nature (physical pleasure) and goodness by intellect, showing that tawhid is the highest good. He concludes that since the reward must be greater than deeds, and sensory pleasures are limited to certain natures, the honor of vision is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: التوحيد, حسن العقل, حسن الطبع, الرؤية.
The Arabic has some OCR uncertainties, but the argument is clear.
2
Understand the two kinds of goodness
Maturidi distinguishes between goodness by intellect (حسن العقل) and goodness by nature (حسن الطبع). Tawhid is good by intellect, while sensory pleasures are good by nature.
Intellectual goodness is universal and higher.
3
Follow the argument against sensory pleasures
Sensory pleasures are not universal—angels, for example, do not have physical desires. Therefore, if the reward were only sensory, it would not honor all beings equally.
This shows the need for a higher reward.
4
See the conclusion: vision as the necessary reward
The vision of Allah turns the unseen object of worship into a witnessed one, providing an honor that is intellectual and universal, surpassing any physical pleasure.
This is the core of Maturidi's argument for ru'ya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله تعالى وعد أن يجزى أحسن مما عملوا به في الدنيا، ولا شي أحسن من التوحيد وأرفع قدرا من الايمان بنه ؛ إذ هو المستحسن بالعقول والثواب الموعود من جوهر الجنة حسنه حسن الطبع . وذلك دون حسن الطبع : وذلك دون حسن العقل إذ لا يجوز أن يكون شيء حسنا في العقول لا يستحسنه ذو عقل ، وجائز ما استحسنه الطبع أن يكون طبع لا يتلذذ به كطبع الملائكة . ومثله في العتموية ؛ لذلك لزم القول بالرؤية لتكون كرامة تبلغ في الجلالة ما أكرموا به ، وهو يحير لهم المعبيد بالغيب شهودا كما خيار المطلوب من الثواب حضورا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that Allah, Exalted is He, promised to reward with something better than what they did in this world. And nothing is better than tawḥīd (monotheism) and higher in rank than faith in Him, for it is what is deemed good by intellects. And the promised reward from the substance of Paradise – its goodness is the goodness of nature (ṭab‘). And that is below the goodness of nature: that is below the goodness of intellect, because it is not possible that a thing is deemed good by intellects while no person of intellect deems it good. And what is deemed good by nature may be a nature that does not take pleasure in it, like the nature of angels. And likewise in al-‘atamawiyya [؟]; therefore the doctrine of vision is necessary so that it be an honor reaching in majesty what they are honored with, and it transforms for them the object of worship known by the unseen into something witnessed, just as the best of what is sought from reward is attendance. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the superiority of intellectual goodness over physical pleasure. Maturidi states that Allah promised to reward the believers with something better than their deeds. The best deed is tawḥīd, which is deemed good by intellect. The sensory pleasures of Paradise are good by physical nature (ṭab‘), but intellectual goodness is higher. Moreover, sensory pleasure may not appeal to all natures (e.g., angels do not have physical desires). Therefore, a higher reward must be something that all rational beings can appreciate, and that is the vision of Allah, which turns the unseen object of worship into a witnessed one. This vision is a greater honor than any sensory pleasure.

Key Terms
التوحيد
حسن العقل
حسن الطبع
الرؤية
كرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the vision of Allah is merely another sensory pleasure, like seeing with physical eyes.
Correction: Maturidi argues that vision is an intellectual and spiritual honor (karāma) that transcends sensory pleasures, since it is accessible to all rational beings, including angels, and it is good by intellect, not by nature.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that vision is necessary as a reward?
A) Because it is the best sensory pleasure
B) Because it is an intellectual honor surpassing physical pleasures, suitable for all beings
C) Because the Prophet said so
D) Because the Mu'tazilites denied it

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the vision of Allah is necessary as the highest intellectual reward, surpassing all sensory pleasures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Paradise, do you find yourself longing more for its sensory pleasures or for the vision of Allah? What does your longing reveal about the depth of your faith?
?
The angels do not take pleasure in physical delights, yet they worship. In what ways are you prioritizing sensory gratification over the intellectual honor of knowing and seeing your Lord? Name one change you will make today.
?
If you truly believed that seeing Allah is the greatest possible reward, how would your daily priorities shift? What specific action can you take this week to increase your desire for that vision?

Direct Sight vs. Inferential Knowledge in the Afterlife
Maturidi argues that vision provides knowledge of certainty unlike inferential knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that direct vision provides a higher certainty than inferential knowledge, and why this necessitates the beatific vision in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Direct sight of Allah gives a certainty that no inference can reach.

Maturidi here addresses why believers need to see Allah in the afterlife. He argues that knowledge through inference (istidlāl) always carries a degree of doubt, whereas direct vision (ʿilm al-ʿiyān) is utterly certain. The present passage lays out this contrast and grounds it in Quranic evidence about the persistence of denial even when evidence is overwhelming.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note that the Arabic has some OCR errors but the meaning is preserved.
The passage argues that afterlife knowledge is of direct sight, not inference.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Look for the terms 'علم العيان' (direct sight) and 'علم الاستدلال' (inferential knowledge). Understand how Maturidi contrasts them.
Inferential knowledge relies on signs; direct sight is immediate.
3
Examine the evidence and conclusion
Maturidi cites the verse about sending angels (6:111) and the disbelievers' denial after death to show that even strong signs do not produce certainty. Then concludes that vision is necessary.
The logical flow: inference does not yield the same certainty as direct sight, so direct sight is needed for ultimate knowledge of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن كلا تجمع على العلم بالله في الآخرة ، العلم الذي لا يعتريه الوسواس، وذلك علم العيان لا على الاستدلال ، وكثرة الآيات لا تحقة الاستدلال ، وكثرة الآيات لا تحقق عالم الطبق الذي لا يعني ذلك ، دليله قوله : وَلَوْ أَنَّنا نزَّلْنَا إِلَيْهم المَلائِكَةَ ، وما ذكر من استعانة الكفرة بالتكذيب في الآخرة وانكار الرسل وقولهم : لم تكث إلا ساعة من النهار وغير ذلك . يحسين علم العيان نحو علم الاستدلال لم لم يجز أن علم العيان ، فثبت أن الرؤية توجب ذلك . علم العيان و بعد ، فإنه إذ لا يجوز أن يصير علم الاستدلال نحو علم العيان، فثبت أن الرؤية توجب ذلك.
And also that everyone agrees on the knowledge of Allah in the afterlife – the knowledge that is untouched by doubt. And that is knowledge of direct sight (ʿilm al-ʿiyān), not by inference (istidlāl). And the abundance of signs does not produce [the same certainty]? And the abundance of signs does not verify the state of? that is not meant. Its evidence is His saying: 'And even if We sent down angels to them' (6:111), and what is mentioned of the disbelievers' persistence in denial in the afterlife and their rejection of messengers and their saying: 'You stayed only an hour of the day' (46:35), and other than that. Thus if [it is that] direct sight is superior to inferential knowledge, then why is it not permissible that? so it is established that vision necessitates that. And after that, since it is not possible that inferential knowledge becomes like direct sight, it is established that vision necessitates that.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the knowledge of Allah in the afterlife is of the type of direct vision (ʿilm al-ʿiyān), not inferential knowledge (istidlāl). Inferential knowledge, even with many signs, does not reach the same level of certainty as direct sight. Maturidi cites the verse about sending down angels: even if angels came, the disbelievers would not believe. Similarly, in the afterlife, the disbelievers still deny and claim they stayed only a short time. This shows that inferential knowledge does not bring the same conviction as direct sight. Therefore, the believers need direct vision of Allah to attain complete certainty. The passage concludes that since inferential knowledge cannot become like direct sight, vision is necessary for that ultimate certainty.

علم الاستدلال
اليقين
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that inferential knowledge is sufficient for certainty about Allah.
Correction: Maturidi argues that inferential knowledge, even with many signs, does not produce the same certainty as direct sight; only direct vision can remove all doubt.

Checkpoint

A) Inferential knowledge (istidlāl)
B) Direct sight (ʿilm al-ʿiyān)
C) Rational deduction
D) Speculative knowledge

Key Takeaway
Direct vision (ru'yat Allah) is necessary for the ultimate certainty in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If inferential knowledge of Allah is insufficient for full certainty, how does that challenge the way you currently seek closeness to Him? Are you satisfied with just learning about Allah, or do you genuinely long for the direct vision of Him in the hereafter?
?
The disbelievers saw clear signs yet persisted in denial. What signs has Allah shown you in your life that you have yet to fully accept or act upon? What would change if you sought certainty beyond intellectual agreement?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and realizing that your knowledge of Him was only inferential. Would that thought alarm you or motivate you to seek a deeper, more experiential faith now? What step can you take today to move from mere inference to a heart certain of Allah?

The Vision of Allah: The Supreme Reward for Tawhid
Maturidi proves vision of Allah as ultimate reward for tawhid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the vision of Allah is the greatest reward for believers, based on the principle that the reward must surpass the deed.

Core Idea
The best deed deserves the best reward—the vision of Allah.

Maturidi presents a rational argument for the vision of Allah. He starts with the premise that Allah promises a reward better than the believer's deeds. Since the best deed is tawhid (monotheism), the reward must be something even greater. Maturidi then shows that the only thing greater than tawhid is direct vision of Allah, which is the ultimate honor and presence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The passage contains an unclear phrase marked with [?]; focus on the overall logic.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Maturidi starts with the premise that Allah rewards with something better than the deed. Then he identifies tawhid as the best deed. Ask yourself: what could be better than tawhid?
3
Understand the need for vision
Maturidi argues that natural pleasures (like those of Paradise) are inferior to intellectual goodness. Since tawhid is intellectually good, its reward must be intellectual and spiritual—culminating in the vision of Allah, which is the highest honor.
This vision transforms unseen worship into direct witnessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله تعالى وعد أن يجزى أحسن مما عملوا به في الدنيا، ولا شيء أحسن من التوحيد وأرفع قدرا من الإيمان به؛ إذ هو المستحسن بالعقول والثواب الموعود من جوهر الجنة حسنه حسن الطبع. وذلك دون حسن الطبع: وذلك دون حسن العقل إذ لا يجوز أن يكون شيء حسنا في العقول لا يستحسنه ذو عقل، وجائز ما استحسنه الطبع أن يكون طبع لا يتلذذ به كطبع الملائكة. ومثله في العتموية [؟]؛ أن لذلك لزم القول بالرؤية لتكون كرامة تبلغ في الجلالة ما أكرموا به، وهو يحير لهم المعبود بالغيب شهودا كما خيار المطلوب من الثواب حضورا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also that Allah Almighty promised to reward with something better than what they did in the world, and there is nothing better than tawhid and higher in rank than faith in Him; for it is deemed good by the intellects, and the promised reward from the substance of Paradise its goodness is the goodness of nature. And that is below the goodness of the intellect: for it is not permissible that something be good in the intellects that no intelligent person deems good, and it is possible that what nature deems good be a nature that does not delight in it, like the nature of angels. And similar to it in 'al-utamawiyya' [؟]; therefore the statement about vision is necessary so that it be an honor reaching in majesty what they have been honored with, and it transforms for them the worshipped by unseen into witnessed, just as the best of what is sought of reward is present, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the vision of Allah. The author argues that Allah promises to reward believers with something better than their deeds in this world. The best deed is tawhid (monotheism) and faith. Its goodness is both intellectual and natural, but intellectual goodness surpasses natural goodness. The promised reward must be of the highest order. Since the best deed deserves the best reward, and the vision of Allah is the ultimate honor and presence, it must be granted to believers. The author draws an analogy: just as the best reward is not merely a natural pleasure but a higher spiritual presence, so the vision transforms the unseen worship into direct witnessing, which is the greatest honor.

D) Because all deeds are equal in reward.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that the vision of Allah is the greatest honor because it is the only reward that surpasses the intellectual and spiritual value of tawhid.

?

?
The greatest deed is tawhid, yet many of us practice it without it transforming our desire for the afterlife. Are you living as if the vision of Allah is the supreme goal, or are you settling for lesser rewards? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that the best reward is to see your Creator?

Direct Vision as the Basis of Certain Knowledge
Maturidi argues direct vision is necessary for certain knowledge in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the certain knowledge of Allah in the afterlife can only be attained through direct vision, not inference.

Core Idea
Certainty in the afterlife requires direct vision, not inference.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a proof for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife based on the nature of knowledge itself. He argues that the knowledge of Allah in the hereafter is certain and doubt-free—this can only be 'ilm al-ʿiyān (knowledge of direct sight), not inferential knowledge. The passage uses a Qur'anic verse to illustrate that even overwhelming signs cannot compel belief through inference alone.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish two types of knowledge
Identify the two kinds of knowledge mentioned: ʿilm al-ʿiyān (direct sight) and ʿilm al-istidlāl (inferential knowledge). Notice that Maturidi says the afterlife knowledge is of the former type.
2
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
Read the reference to the verse about sending down angels (Qur'an 6:111). Understand that even direct angelic visitation would not produce belief through inference alone, highlighting the limitation of inferential knowledge.
The precise wording of the verse is not quoted fully, but the allusion is clear.
3
Connect to the necessity of vision

The OCR garbles some logical steps, but the core argument remains.

Denying Comprehension but Affirming Vision
Maturidi states the Ash'ari position: deny idrak, affirm ru'ya using Quranic verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi distinguishes between denying comprehensive perception (idrāk) of Allah while affirming the possibility of seeing Him (ru'ya) in the afterlife, based on Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah does not require comprehending Him.

This short but dense passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a key theological dispute: does the Quranic verse "Sight does not comprehend Him" rule out seeing Allah entirely? Maturidi argues that it only negates comprehensive perception, not vision itself, drawing a powerful analogy with knowledge and encompassment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: idrāk, ru'ya, ihāṭa, 'ilm.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two Quranic verses
Find the verse about sight (6:103) and the verse about knowledge (20:110). Notice how Maturidi uses each.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi compares the negation of encompassment (ihāṭa) with knowledge to the negation of comprehension (idrāk) with vision. In both cases, the thing itself (knowledge/vision) is affirmed, while the encompassing/comprehending is denied.
This preserves the possibility of seeing Allah.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it allows the Ash'ari position to affirm ru'ya of Allah in Paradise while upholding the Quranic text.
This is a key point in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولا نقول بالإدراك لقوله تعالى: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ، فقد امتدح به بنفي الإدراك لا بنفي الرؤية، وهو كقوله: وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ عِلْمًا، كان في ذلك إثبات العلم ونفي الإحاطة، فمثله في حق الإدراك، وبالله التوفيق.
The jurist Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And we do not affirm comprehension (idrāk) because of His saying: 'Sight does not comprehend Him.' He praised Himself by negating comprehension, not by negating vision. And it is like His saying: 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge.' In that was affirmation of knowledge and negation of encompassment, so likewise regarding comprehension. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement by Imam Maturidi. He clarifies that the Ash'ari position (and his own) is to deny idrāk (comprehension) of Allah, while affirming ru'ya (vision) of Allah in the afterlife. He cites the Quranic verse 'Sight does not comprehend Him' (6:103) and argues that Allah praises Himself by negating idrāk, not by negating ru'ya. He draws an analogy with the verse 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge' (20:110), where knowledge is affirmed but encompassment is negated. Similarly, vision (ru'ya) is affirmed but comprehension (idrāk) is negated. This subtle distinction allows for the possibility of seeing Allah without fully encompassing Him.

Correction: Maturidi explains that the verse only negates comprehensive perception (idrāk), not vision (ru'ya), thus affirming the possibility of seeing Allah without fully encompassing Him.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi affirms the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) while denying comprehensive perception (idrāk), using Quranic verses and analogy.

Ru'ya and Idrak: A Crucial Distinction
Maturidi distinguishes ru'ya from idrak using Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Quranic negation of idrak (comprehension) does not negate the possibility of ru'ya (vision) of Allah.

Core Idea
Negating comprehension does not negate vision.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key theological nuance: the Quran praises Allah by stating that sight does not comprehend Him, but this does not deny that believers will see Him in the afterlife. In this passage, he clarifies the distinction between ru'ya (vision) and idrak (comprehension) using a parallel from another Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the argument: he first distinguishes between general knowledge and the specific glad tidings of vision for believers.
2
Identify the key Quranic citation
Find the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not comprehend Him). Maturidi uses this to argue that what is negated is idrak, not ru'ya.
3
Analyze the analogy with knowledge
Maturidi draws an analogy: just as 'لا يحيطون علما' negates encompassment of knowledge but not knowledge itself, so 'لا تدركه الأبصار' negates comprehension but not vision. Reflect on how this analogy strengthens his argument.
The analogy shows that negation of a specific aspect does not negate the broader category.
4
Consider the misconception
Some confuse ru'ya with idrak. Maturidi's argument guards against this error by showing that the two are distinct. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for Islamic theology.
This distinction preserves the possibility of seeing Allah while maintaining His transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن في ذلك العلم يستوى الكافر والمؤمن، والبشارة بالرؤية خص بها المؤمن، ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولا نقول بالإدراك لقوله: لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ، فقد امتدح به بنفى الإدراك لا بنفى الرؤية، وهو كقوله: ولا يُحِيطُونَ عِلْمًا، كان في ذلك إنجاب العلم ونفى الإحاطة، فمثله في حق الإدراك، وبالله التوفيق.
And after, indeed in that knowledge the disbeliever and the believer are equal, but the glad tidings of vision (ru'ya) are specifically for the believer. There is no power except with Allah. Al-Faqih Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And we do not affirm comprehension (idrak) because of His statement: 'Sight does not comprehend Him' (la tudrikuhu al-absar); He praised Himself by negating comprehension, not by negating vision. And it is like His statement: 'And they do not encompass Him in knowledge' (wa la yuhituna 'ilman); in that was a granting of knowledge and a negation of encompassment. So it is similar regarding comprehension. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This passage begins by establishing that both believer and disbeliever share knowledge of certain matters, but the promise of seeing Allah (ru'ya) is exclusive to believers. Then Maturidi states his position: he does not affirm idrak (comprehension) of Allah because the Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not comprehend Him) praises Allah by negating idrak, not by negating ru'ya. He draws an analogy with another verse 'ولا يحيطون علما' (they do not encompass Him in knowledge), which negates encompassment (ihata) of knowledge but not knowledge itself. Thus, just as knowledge is affirmed while encompassment is negated, vision (ru'ya) is affirmed while comprehension is negated. The argument relies on a precise reading of the Quranic text, distinguishing between the act of seeing and the act of encompassing the seen object. Maturidi concludes by acknowledging divine success.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think that negating idrak (comprehension) automatically negates ru'ya (vision).
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that idrak and ru'ya are distinct; the Quran negates only idrak, not ru'ya, just as it negates encompassment of knowledge but not knowledge itself.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya; they are distinct, preserving the possibility of seeing Allah while affirming His transcendence.

Further Refutations and the Case of Musa
Maturidi provides additional refutations and clarifies the story of Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's additional arguments against conditioning vision of Allah, including the variability of veils and the correct interpretation of Moses' request.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah is not bound by physical conditions; veils are relative and can be removed.

Maturidi continues his refutation of al-Ka'bi's conditions for vision by presenting two more arguments. He shows that things like shadow and light are seen without distance or facing, and that even if all conditions are met, vision can still be absent due to veils. He then extends the argument by noting that what veils one observer may not veil another, using the Angel of Death as an example. Finally, he addresses the incident of Moses (peace be upon him) to clarify that the request was for knowledge of signs, not literal physical sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Pay attention to the numbers (third, fourth) and the shift to Musa.
The passage is dense; you may need to read it twice.
2
Identify the third and fourth arguments
Locate where Maturidi presents the third and fourth refutations. Notice how he uses the example of seeing shadow, darkness, and light without conditions, and the point that conditions can be present yet vision absent.
3
Understand the relativity of veils
Maturidi argues that distance and smallness are not absolute barriers; they vary between observers. The Angel of Death sees everything from all parts of the earth. This shows that veils are not intrinsic to vision itself.
This is a key insight: vision is not limited by the properties of the object but by the observer's capacity.
4
Analyze the conclusion about bodies and accidents
Maturidi concludes that only accidents are not seen by their essence; every body is visible. Therefore, denying vision of what is not a body (Allah) does not follow from the visibility of bodies.
He uses this to counter the opponent's assumption that vision requires a body.
5
Examine the interpretation of Musa's request
Maturidi explains that Moses asked for 'knowledge encompassing the signs' (i.e., understanding of divine signs), not for physical sight. This refutes the objection that even a prophet asked to see Allah.
This shows his commitment to interpreting ambiguous verses in light of theological principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث ما بينا من رؤية الظل والظلمة والنور من غير شيء من تلك. والرابع أنه قد يجوز وجود تلك المعانى كلها مع عدم الرؤية إما بحجب أو فجاز تحقيق الرؤية على نفى تلك المعانى، نحو ما أجيب القائل بالجسم جوهر عند معارضته بالفاعل والعالم أنه جسم لا كذلك، فيجوز وجود ذلك ولا جسم، فمثله في الرؤية. على أن البعد الذي يحجبنا والدقة يجوز أن يبانه بصر غيرنا، فصار ارتفاع الرؤية بالحجاب، فإذا ارتفع جاز، ولا قوة إلا بالله وبعد، فإن الذي يقوله تقدير برؤية الأجسام، ولم يمتحن بصره بغير الأجسام والأعراض أن كيف سبيل الرؤية له. وبعد، فإن كل جسم يرى وإن كان الدقة والبعد يخجبان، فيجوز ارتفاعها عن بصر غيره فيرى على ما يرى ملك الموت من بأطراف الأرض ووسطها مما لو اعتبر ذلك يبصر البشر لما احتمل الإدراك، فثبت أن الذي قدر به ليس هو سبب تعريف ما يبصر، ولكن سبب تعريف ما يحجب به البصر فإذا ارتفع رأى، صبع ما كان المنفى رؤيته لذاته عرض، وإلا فكل جسم يرى، فإن لزم إنكار الرؤية لما ليس بجسم أو لما لا يرى إلا بما ذكر ليلزم الإقرار به؛ لأن الذي لا يرى لذاته هو العرض، وإلا فكل عين يرى، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعارض بأمر الدنيا. ولا يحال ذلك، ولكن يسقط المحنة ويرفع. والدنيا لما خلقت الخافية. ثم ذكر في أمر موسى عليه السلام أن ذلك على علم الإحاطة بالآيات. وقد بينا فساد ذلك. وما ذلك بلغ العلم بالذى يسأل، وهو رسول يبعث إلى به نجاة الخلق، وذلك لا يكون بغير الممتحن؛ إذ هو تبليغ الرسالة والدعاء.
And the third: what we have explained concerning the seeing of shadow, darkness, and light without any of those [conditions]. And the fourth: that it is possible for all those meanings to exist with absence of vision, either due to veils, so it is possible for vision to be realized upon the negation of those meanings, like the answer given to the one who said that a body is a substance when he was opposed by the agent [creator] and the knowing that He is a body, not like that, so it is possible for that to exist without being a body, and similarly for vision. Indeed, the distance that veils us and the smallness may be penetrated by the sight of another, so the denial of vision is due to the veil; when it is removed, it is possible. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, what he says is a measurement by the vision of bodies, and he has not tested his sight with other than bodies and accidents as to what the way of vision is for it. Moreover, every body is seen even if smallness and distance veil it, so it is possible for those veils to be removed from the sight of another, and it sees as the Angel of Death sees those at the ends and middle of the earth, such that if human sight were considered with that, it would not have capacity for perception. So it is established that what he measured with is not the cause of defining what is seen, but the cause of defining what veils the sight; when it is removed, it sees. [Then] what was negated in vision due to its essence is the accident; otherwise every body is seen. For if the denial of vision is necessary for what is not a body or for what is not seen except by what was mentioned, then acknowledgment of it would be necessary. Because that which is not seen by itself is the accident; otherwise every eye is seen. And there is no power except with Allah. And he objected by the matter of the world. That is not possible, but it removes the trial and raises [it]. And the world was created hidden. Then he mentioned concerning the matter of Moses (peace be upon him) that that was based on knowledge encompassing the signs. We have already explained the corruption of that. And that does not reach the knowledge of the one who asks, and he is a messenger sent for the salvation of creation, and that cannot be without the trial, for it is the delivery of the message and the call.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of al-Ka'bi's conditions for vision, presenting the third and fourth errors, and then additional arguments leading to a discussion of the request of Moses (Musa) to see Allah. The third error is that we see things like shadow, darkness, and light without the conditions of distance, facing, etc., proving that vision can occur without these. The fourth error is that even if all those conditions exist, vision may still be absent due to veils, so the conditions are not sufficient; conversely, vision can be realized without them, just as we answer the anthropomorphist who says that a body is a substance but the Creator is not a body like other bodies. Maturidi further argues that distance and smallness are veils that vary between observers; what veils one being may not veil another (e.g., the Angel of Death sees all). Thus, the conditions al-Ka'bi cited are not intrinsic to vision but are veils. If veils are removed, vision occurs. He then makes a positive point: anything that is seen is either a body or an accident; the only thing not seen by its essence is the accident. Therefore, affirming vision of what is not a body (i.e., Allah) does not contradict the principle that every body is seen, because the vision of Allah is different. Finally, he addresses the story of Moses (Quran 7:143), where Moses asked to see Allah. Maturidi argues that this request was for knowledge encompassing the signs (i.e., a special knowledge), not for literal physical vision. He criticizes the interpretation that Moses was asking for physical sight, saying that Moses is a messenger and his request must be understood in the context of his mission and trial. This passage reinforces the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that vision of Allah is possible in the afterlife, even though it is not subject to physical conditions.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الحجاب
الجسم
العرض
موسى
الآيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Moses' request to see Allah in this world proves that vision of Allah is impossible or that even prophets cannot ask for it.
Correction: Maturidi explains that Moses was asking for knowledge of divine signs (ayat), not for physical sight; the request was a means to strengthen his message and was granted in a different form.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the example of seeing shadow, darkness, and light demonstrate?
A) That vision requires light.
B) That vision can occur without conditions like distance and facing.
C) That shadow and darkness are not real.
D) That the opponent's conditions are all necessary.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that vision of Allah is not subject to physical conditions; veils are relative, and Moses' request was for knowledge, not literal sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi argues that what we think of as necessary conditions for vision are actually veils. Think of one area in your life where you have mistaken a temporary hardship or limitation as an absolute barrier to experiencing Allah’s mercy or guidance. What would it mean for you to see that barrier as a veil that could be lifted, and how can you actively seek its removal today?
?
The Angel of Death sees all creation without the constraints of distance or smallness. How does this example expose your own tendency to limit Allah’s power based on your human limitations? In what specific situation are you currently acting as if Allah cannot reach or help you because of the 'distance' or 'smallness' of your circumstances?
?
Maturidi clarifies that Moses’ request was for knowledge, not literal sight. Are you, like some of the people Maturidi refutes, demanding a tangible sign from Allah before you commit to obedience? What 'sign' are you waiting for that actually keeps you from responding to the clear guidance He has already given you?

Refuting Anthropomorphic Analogies
Maturidi refutes anthropomorphism by exposing the flaw in analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the anthropomorphist's analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is invalid and how the Quranic verse negating comprehensive sight supports divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Comparing God to bodies is a flawed analogy—God is not like His creation.

Imam Maturidi begins by critiquing the anthropomorphists who claim that since every agent and knower in the world is a body, God must also be a body. He then cites the Quranic verse 'Sight does not comprehend Him' to establish that vision of God is not like bodily vision. This lesson examines the logical failure of anthropomorphic reasoning and the scriptural foundation for transcending it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice that Maturidi first presents the anthropomorphist's claim, then refutes it.
2
Identify the anthropomorphist's fallacy
The anthropomorphist argues that every agent in the witnessed world is a body, so God must be a body. Maturidi points out that this analogy only considers bodies; it does not prove anything about seeing a non-body.
3
Understand the Quranic proof
Maturidi cites 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' and argues that just as creation and omnipotence are eternal, so is this negation of comprehension.
This parallels the eternal nature of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن من تأمل الذى ذكر عرف أنه مشتهى النحلة، لأنه المعنى الذى له يجب أن تكون الرؤية بتلك الشرائط، إنما أخبر أنه كذلك وجد، وهو قول المشبهة إنه وجد كل فاعل في الشاهد جسما، وكذا كل عالم، فيجب مثله في الغائب. ثم ذكر معنى رؤية الجسم ولم يذكر معنى رؤية غير الجسم حتى يكون له دليلا وبعد الله فإنه نفى بالدقة والبعد، وهما زائلان من سبحانه، ثم احتج بامتداح الله بقوله: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ، وقال: لا يجوز أن يزول، فمثله عليه في قوله: خَالِقٌ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وقوله: وهو على كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ، فلا يجوز أن يزول. ثم قد وصف الله بالرؤية على ذكر، فثبت أن ذلك طريق لا يؤدى على كنه ما به الرؤية إسقا ما ذكر فإن قيل : كيف يرى ؟ قيل : بلا كيف؛ إذ الكيفية تكون لذى صورة، بل يرى بلا وصف قيام واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة، وقصير وطويل، وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل، ولا يعنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل التعاليه عن ذلك
Then the principle is that whoever reflects on what was mentioned knows that it is the desired doctrine, because it is the meaning for which vision must be with those conditions. He only informed that it is thus found, and it is the saying of the anthropomorphists that he found every agent in the witnessed world to be a body, and likewise every knower, so the like must be in the unseen. Then he mentioned the meaning of vision of a body and did not mention the meaning of vision of a non-body, so that it would be a proof for him. And after Allah, he negated by subtlety and remoteness, and both are removed from Him, glory be to Him. Then he adduced Allah's praise by His saying: 'Sight does not comprehend Him, and He comprehends sight', and said: it is not permissible that it cease. So the like is established upon His saying: 'Creator of everything' and His saying: 'He is over all things Omnipotent', so it is not permissible that it cease. Then He described Allah with vision upon mention, so it is established that that path does not lead to the core of what vision is. Then if it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without modality; for modality belongs to one with form. Rather, He is seen without description of standing, connection, separation, facing, being behind, short, long, sitting, leaning, attachment, light, darkness, stationary, moving, touching, separate, outside, inside; and nothing that imagination takes or the intellect estimates applies to Him, exalted is He above that.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage refutes the anthropomorphists (المشبهة) who analogize God to created things. Maturidi argues that the anthropomorphist finds that every agent in the sensible world is a body, and every knower is a body, so he concludes the same for the unseen (God). However, this analogy fails because it only mentions the modality of seeing a body, not the modality of seeing a non-body. Then he cites the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not comprehend Him) and argues that since God is eternal and His attributes like creating and omnipotence are necessary, the negation of comprehension is also necessary. Finally, he answers the question 'How is He seen?' with 'بلا كيف' (without modality), because modality applies to things with form. He then negates all bodily attributes: standing, connection, separation, facing, behind, short, long, sitting, leaning, attachment, light, darkness, motion, rest, contact, separation, inside, outside—nothing that the imagination or intellect can conceive applies to Allah.

Key Terms
رؤية
تشبيه
المشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since God is seen in the afterlife, He must have a body or be like physical objects.
Correction: Maturidi shows that vision of Allah is affirmed by revelation but its modality is unknown; the Quran explicitly denies comprehensive sight, proving it is not like bodily vision.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Maturidi use to support the negation of comprehensive sight?
A) إِنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَىٰ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ
B) لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ
C) خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ
D) وَلَا يَزَالُونَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphic analogies are invalid because they assume God is like created bodies, whereas the Quran denies that sight can comprehend Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often compare God to what you know from creation when you think about His attributes. After reading this passage, how will you guard against the subtle anthropomorphism in your own heart? What specific action will you take when you catch yourself imagining God in human terms?
?
This lesson says that every analogy from the witnessed to the unseen regarding God's body is flawed. Have you ever justified a wrong belief about God by saying 'But everything in creation works this way'? What is one belief you need to abandon right now after this lesson?
?

Seeing Allah Without Modality
Maturidi explains the modality of vision by denying all bodily attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means to say Allah is seen 'without modality' and how this affirms vision while negating all bodily attributes.

Core Idea
Allah is seen truly, but not in a way that resembles any created thing.

After establishing that vision of Allah is not like bodily vision, Maturidi answers the inevitable question: 'How is He seen?' His response, 'Without modality' (bila kayf), is a cornerstone of Sunni theology. He then lists numerous attributes that do not apply to Allah, emphasizing that nothing imagined or conceived can describe Him. This lesson unpacks that answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the direct question and answer format.
The phrase 'بلا كيف' is the core response.
2
Understand 'bila kayf'
Realize that 'bila kayf' means vision is affirmed without describing how. It is not a denial of vision but a denial of any modality that can be compared to creation.
This is a standard Ash'ari/Maturidi position.
3
Review the negated attributes
Maturidi lists many physical and spatial attributes (standing, connection, etc.) and denies them. This shows that vision of Allah is unlike any bodily experience.

The Vision of God: Seeing without How
Maturidi refutes anthropomorphism and answers how God is seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God is seen in the afterlife without any modality or physical comparison, and how he refutes anthropomorphic interpretations.

Core Idea
God is seen, but without how—no physical attributes or limitations apply.

In this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam Maturidi tackles one of the most debated topics in Islamic theology: the vision of Allah (ru'ya). He first refutes anthropomorphists who liken God to created bodies, then answers the key question 'How is He seen?' with the classical formula 'bila kayf' (without how). This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the negations that safeguard divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argument and how it shifts from Quranic verses to theological reasoning.
2
Understand the linguistic distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between 'seeing' a person's essence and 'seeing' their actions. This is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses about God's 'seeing'.
3
Identify the anthropomorphism argument
Find where Maturidi presents the anthropomorphist (mushabbiha) claim that God must be a body because all agents in the world are bodies, and then see how he refutes it by pointing out the missing link in their analogy.
4
Examine the 'bila kayf' answer
The passage culminates in the answer to 'How is He seen?'—the answer is 'bila kayf' (without how). Notice the long list of attributes that are negated, ensuring that God is beyond all physical and mental categories.
This is the core of the doctrine: vision without modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَلَمْ تَرَ إِلَى رَبِّكَ كَيْفَ مَدَّ الظِّلَّ وَأَلَمْ تَرَ كَيْفَ فَعَلَ رَبُّكَ بِأَصْحَابِ الْفِيلِ، وأصله أن من قال: رأيت فلانا أو نظرت إلى فلان لم يحتمل غير ذاته، وإذا قال: رأيته يقول كذا ويفعل كذا أنه لا يريد به رؤية ذاته، قتل أمر قصة موسى عليه السلام وهذه الآية. ثم الأصل أن من تأمل الذي ذكر عرف أنه مشتهى النحلة [؟]، لأنه المعنى الذي له يجب أن تكون الرؤية بتلك الشرائط، إنما أخبر أنه كذلك وجد، وهو قول المشبهة أنه وجد كل فاعل في الشاهد جسما، وكذا كل عالم، فيجب مثله في الغائب. ثم ذكر معنى رؤية الجسم ولم يذكر معنى رؤية غير الجسم حتى يكون له دليلا. وبعد الله فإنه نفى بالدقة والبعد، وهما زائلان من سبحانه، ثم احتج بامتداح الله بقوله: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ، وقال: لا يجوز أن يزول، فمثله عليه في قوله: خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وقوله: وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ، فلا يجوز أن يزول. ثم قد وصف الله بالرؤية على ذكر، فثبت أن ذلك طريق لا يؤدي على كنه ما به الرؤية إسقاط ما ذكر. فإن قيل: كيف يرى؟ قيل: بلا كيف؛ إذ الكيفية تكون لذي صورة، بل يرى بلا وصف قيام واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة، وقصير وطويل، وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل، ولا يعنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل تعالى عن ذلك.
Did you not see how your Lord spread the shade and did you not see how your Lord dealt with the companions of the elephant? The principle is that if someone says: I saw so-and-so or I looked at so-and-so, it does not imply other than his essence; and if he says: I saw him saying such-and-such and doing such-and-such, he does not intend the vision of his essence. The matter of the story of Moses, peace be upon him, and this verse killed [that]. Then the principle is that whoever contemplates what was mentioned knows that it is desired by the bee [lit. uncertain], because it is the meaning for which vision must be under those conditions; He only informed that it is found thus. This is the saying of the anthropomorphists: they found every agent in the witness to be a body, and likewise every knower, so the same must be in the unseen. Then He mentioned the meaning of vision of the body and did not mention the meaning of vision of other than the body so that it could be a proof for Him. And after God, He negated by precision and distance, both of which are removed from Him, Exalted is He. Then He argued by God's praise with His saying: 'Sights do not perceive Him, and He perceives sights' (6:103), and said: It is not permissible that it should cease; so liken it to His saying: 'Creator of everything' and His saying: 'He is over all things powerful' (11:4), so it is not permissible that it should cease. Then He described God with vision as mentioned, so it is established that this path does not lead to the core of that by which vision is dropping what was mentioned. Then it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without how; for modality belongs to that which has form; rather, He is seen without description of standing, connection, separation, facing, turning away, short, long, sitting, reclining, clinging, light, darkness, stationary, moving, touching, separate, outside, inside; and it does not mean that illusion grasps it or intellect estimates it—He is exalted above that.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the vision of God (ru'ya). It begins with Quranic verses that mention God's actions, then discusses the meaning of 'seeing' in ordinary language: when one says 'I saw someone,' it means the physical vision of their essence, but when one says 'I saw him speaking,' it refers to witnessing actions, not essence. This distinction is applied to Moses' story and the verses. The passage then identifies the anthropomorphist (mushabbiha) argument: since all agents and knowers in the created world are bodies, God must be a body. Maturidi refutes this by noting that they only mentioned the vision of a body, not vision of a non-body, so their analogy fails. He then cites the verse 'Sights do not perceive Him' to negate any physical perception, and uses the other verses to show that God's attributes of creating and power are eternal and unchanging, so His description as being seen must also be eternal but not like physical vision. The core of the passage is the objection-and-answer: 'How is He seen?' The answer is the classic aqeeda formula: 'Bila kayf' (without how). Modality (kayfiyya) applies only to things with form. God is seen without any physical or mental attributes like standing, connection, separation, facing, distance, size, posture, light, darkness, motion, touch, inside/outside. The passage concludes that God is beyond being grasped by imagination or intellect in this regard.

Key Terms
رؤية
بلا كيف
تشبيه
مشبهة
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'seeing God without how' means He is seen with physical eyes like seeing created beings.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that God is seen in a real but incomparable way, without any physical modality or mental image.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is God seen?
A) With physical eyes like seeing created beings
B) Without any modality or comparison
C) Through intellectual perception alone
D) Only in the afterlife and not in this world

Key Takeaway
The vision of God is real but incomparable; it is seen without any physical or mental modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you unconsciously assign forms or limitations to Him? How does the teaching of 'bila kayf' challenge your assumptions about how you relate to the unseen?
?
What does it mean for you personally that your Lord is seen but not comprehended? How does this truth affect your longing to meet Him?
?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila on God's Attribute of Creation
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim and compares them to eternalists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God was not creator before creation, exposing their inconsistency in denying eternal divine attributes.

Core Idea
Denying God's eternal attributes leads to self-contradiction about creation's dependence on God.

The Mu'tazila argued that God existed alone and then created the world without any eternal attribute, such as will, being the cause of creation. Maturidi points out the logical flaw: if the world came to be without a cause from God, then it is not truly from God, and using the world as proof of God becomes problematic. He compares their position to that of those who believe the world is eternal, showing the Mu'tazila to be inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the key terms like إرادة, تكوين, دليل, قدم, حدوث.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim and its implications
See how Maturidi summarizes their view: God was by Himself, the world did not exist, then it came to be without a 'meaning' (attribute) from God. Understand why this leads to contradiction when they also use the world as proof of God.
3
Analyze the comparison with eternalists
Maturidi compares them to those who hold the world eternal. Both deny the world is from another. But eternalists are more consistent because they make the world eternal, while Mu'tazila make it temporal but still not from God. Ask yourself: why is this a refutation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم إن الله كان بذاته ولم يكن العالم ولا شيء منه، ثم كان العالم من غير أن كان منه إليه معنى به كان؛ لأن الإرادة عندهم هي العالم، وكذلك التكوين، فكان العالم لا معنى منه إليه به كان، ثم صيروه دليلا عليه على القول بما ذكرت. فأما إن كذبوا بجعله دليلا، وبقولهم أن لم يكن منه غير كونه بعد لم يكن، ومذهبهم أن العلم بكون شيء لا يوجب تكوينه، والقدم لا يوجب كونه عندهم إلا هذين، لا يوجب واحد منها كونه، فأوجبوا كون به، وليس من الله العالم لا بأحد، على ما قال القائلون بقدم العالم إذ كان لا بغيره، فضاهوا بقولهم هذا قول أولئك، إلا أن أولئك ألزم للقياس؛ إذ لما كان العالم لا بغيره جعلوه أزليا، وهؤلاء جعلوه من الوجه الذي بينا لا بغيره حادثا.
And their saying that God was in His essence and the world and anything from it did not exist, then the world came to be without there being from Him a meaning by which it came to be; because will according to them is the world, and likewise causing-to-be (takwin), so the world came to be without a meaning from Him by which it came to be, then they made it a proof for Him according to what I mentioned. As for if they deny making it a proof, and their saying that there was not from Him other than its being after it was not, and their doctrine that knowledge of a thing's being does not necessitate its coming-to-be, and pre-eternity does not necessitate its being according to them except these two, neither one of them necessitates its being, so they necessitated being from it, and the world is not from God by anyone, as those who say the world is eternal said since it is not from another, so they resemble by their saying the saying of those, except that those are more consistent in analogy; since when the world was not from another they made it eternal, and these made it from the aspect we explained not from another but temporal.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazilite claim that God existed alone and then created the world without any attribute or cause from Him, because they deny eternal attributes like will. Maturidi points out that they then use the world as a proof of God, but if they deny that proof, they must admit the world came from nothing and is not from God. He compares them to those who hold the world eternal: both deny that the world is from another, but the eternalists are more consistent because they make the world eternal, while the Mu'tazila make it temporal but without a cause from God, which is contradictory. The core flaw is that they separate the world from any divine causation.

Key Terms
إرادة
تكوين
دليل
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila affirm divine causality, but Maturidi shows they effectively deny it by making the world self-caused.
Correction: The Mu'tazila deny eternal attributes, so they cannot attribute creation to a divine cause; thus the world is not truly from God.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi compare the Mu'tazila to those who hold the world eternal?
A) Both affirm creation ex nihilo
B) Both deny that the world is from another
C) Both affirm God's will
D) Both deny God's existence

Key Takeaway
Denying God's eternal attributes leads to an inconsistent view of creation, where the world is temporal yet not from God, which is illogical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that denying God's attributes leads to a view of creation nearly indistinguishable from atheism, do you ever catch yourself treating God's attributes as merely abstract ideas rather than real and effective? What would change in your reliance on God if you truly believed His will and power are constantly bringing everything into existence?
?
Maturidi here points out the logical inconsistency of claiming the world is temporal but not from God. Have you ever held a belief about God or religion that, if you followed its implications, would lead to a contradiction? Are you willing to examine your own theological assumptions with the same rigor Maturidi applies?
?
The Mu'tazila wanted to protect God's oneness, but ended up making the world self-existing in a way. Are there areas in your life where you try so hard to protect something good that you inadvertently undermine the very thing you love? What would it look like to entrust that area fully to God without slipping into extremes?

Maturidi's Critique of the Mu'tazila Proof for the World's Temporality
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazila proof for world's temporality and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique that the Mu'tazila argument for the world's temporality logically implies the temporality of God.

Core Idea
If the world's temporality requires a temporal maker, then God would be temporal too.

The Mu'tazila argued that since the world contains events, it must be temporal and have a creator. Maturidi exposes the flaw in this reasoning by showing it leads to a contradiction regarding God's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi first states the Mu'tazila argument, then presents his two objections.
The passage is the first half of the source chunk.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
What is the core claim? The Mu'tazila say the world is temporal because it is not free of temporal events, and therefore has a temporally originating agent.
3
Understand Maturidi's two objections
Maturidi presents two logical consequences: (1) if the whole world is temporal, then the same reasoning would make the Creator temporal; (2) if God's essence is eternal, why not also grant eternity to the world?
These show the argument proves too much or too little.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلت المعتزلة وغيرهم على حدث العالم بما لا يخلو عن محدث، ولم يكن دليلهم على ذلك سوى وجود العالم فأوجبوا حدثه، وقالوا: ذلك بمحدث. ثم قالت المعتزلة في الله سبحانه: إنه كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم، وهو اليوم كذلك، فصيروه في أول أحواله ما وقع للخلق به العلم، غير خال عن الحوادث كالعالم الذي وجدوه بالجس غير خال عنها، قصار السبب عرفوا حدث العالم به هو الذي به عرفوا حدث الخالق الرحمن، والله قديم لم يزل. ثم بعد هذا وجهان: أحدهما أنه إذا لزم القول في جملة العالم بالحدث وإن لم نشهده بوجودنا ما شهدنا منه غير خال من الإحداث للزم ذلك في الصانع الخالق وجودنا له ما به نسميه حدثا. والثاني أنه قد وجب قدم ذاته، مع ما لا يعلم وجوده إلا بحوادث ليم لا وجب القول بقدم جملة العالم وإن كان غير خال عن الحوادث؟ و بعد، فإن معرفة احتمال الحوادث فيما لا يجس من العالم بما أضيف إليه من الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون، فالله على قولهم يضاف إليه الرحمة والصنع والإبداع والإعادة، وكل هذا عندهم حوادث، فيجب القول فيه بما يجب في العالم، ويكون لمن هو بهذا الوصف خالق صانع خارج من ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقالت المعتزلة: كان الله سبحانه، ثم حدث منه إرادة كان بها العالم من غير أن كان منه إياها إحداث أو إرادة أو لها اختيار؛ إذ لا غير لها سوى ذاته وقد كان ذاك قبلها. وكذلك قالت المجوس: إن كان الله سبحانه، فحدثت فكرة رديئة كان منها الشيطان، وهو الذي كان به كل شر. قسمت المجوس تلك فكرة، وسمتها المعتزلة إرادة واختيارا. وكان حدوثها لا باختيار وإرادة، فهي بالفكرة أشبه، ثم لم تكن هي غير الشر. ولا الإرادة عند المعتزلة غير العالم. فهذا والله أعلم.
And the Mu'tazila and others argued for the temporal origination of the world by that which is not free of a temporally originating agent, and their proof for that was only the existence of the world, so they necessitated its temporal origination, and they said: that is by a temporally originating agent. Then the Mu'tazila said concerning God, exalted: He was not a creator nor merciful nor compassionate, and He is today like that, so they made Him in the first of His states what befell creation by knowledge, not free of temporal events like the world which they found by sense not free of them, the extent of the reason they knew the temporal origination of the world is that by which they knew the temporal origination of the Creator, the Merciful, while God is eternal, never ceasing. Then after this there are two aspects: one is that if the statement of temporal origination is necessary for the entirety of the world even if we do not witness it by our existence, what we witnessed from it is not free of bringing into existence, then that is necessary for the Maker, the Creator, our existence for Him by which we call Him temporal. And the second is that the pre-eternity of His essence is necessitated, along with what is not known except by temporal events, why then is the statement of pre-eternity not necessary for the entirety of the world even though it is not free of temporal events? And after that, the knowledge of the possibility of temporal events in that which is not sensed from the world by what is attributed to it of conjunction and separation and motion and stillness, so God according to their saying is attributed mercy, making, origination, and returning, and all of these according to them are temporal events, so it is necessary to say concerning Him what is necessary concerning the world, and one who is with this description is a creator maker outside of that. And there is no power except with Allah. And the Mu'tazila said: God, exalted, was, then an will occurred from Him by which the world was, without His having from it a bringing into existence or will or choice; since there is no other for it besides His essence, and that was before it. And likewise the Magians said: If God, exalted, was, then an evil thought occurred from which came Iblis, and he is the one by whom every evil was. The Magians divided that thought, and the Mu'tazila called it will and choice. And its occurrence was not by choice and will, so it is more similar to thought, and it was not other than evil. And will according to the Mu'tazila is not other than the world. So this, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazila argument for the temporal origination (حدث) of the world and their problematic view of God's attributes. Maturidi first states that the Mu'tazila and others argue that whatever is not free of temporal events requires a temporally originating agent, but their only proof is the world's existence itself. They conclude the world is temporal and has a maker. Then Maturidi points out their inconsistency: they claim God was not creator or merciful in the beginning, and is the same today, thus making God subject to temporal events like the world. They derived the temporal nature of the world by sense, and used that to argue for a temporal creator. Maturidi objects that God is eternal, so this reasoning is flawed. He then presents two possibilities: (1) If the whole world is temporal, then the same logic would apply to the Creator, making Him temporal. (2) If God's essence is pre-eternal, why not also grant pre-eternity to the world? Maturidi notes that knowledge of temporal events in the world comes from sensed attributes like conjunction and motion. But they attribute similar temporal events to God (mercy, making, etc.), so by their own logic, God would be temporal like the world. However, the true Creator transcends such. Then Maturidi compares the Mu'tazila view of will to the Magian concept of an evil thought: both posit an uncreated event from God that leads to evil or creation. The Mu'tazila say God had a will that occurred without previous will or choice, which is like the Magian thought. That will is identified with evil or the world. Maturidi concludes that this is confused, and God knows best.

Key Terms
حدث
محدث
قديم
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the temporality of the world proves a temporal creator without contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to the absurd conclusion that God would be temporal, or that the world could be eternal.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of Maturidi's two objections to the Mu'tazila argument?
A) The world is eternal because it contains events.
B) If the world is temporal, then the Creator must also be temporal.
C) God cannot be known through His attributes.
D) The Mu'tazila rely on revelation, not reason.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila argument for the world's temporality either implies God's temporality or fails to prove the world's temporality uniquely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like mercy or creation, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as events that happen 'in time' for God? How might that be similar to the Mu'tazila's mistake? Pause and ask yourself: Do I truly believe God's attributes are eternal, or do I sometimes think of God changing? Commit to correcting that thought with the statement 'God is eternal, never ceasing.'
?
Have you ever held a standard for God's existence or actions that you wouldn't apply to anything else, like requiring a 'cause' for God? The Mu'tazila demanded a temporal agent for the world, but then struggled to keep God eternal. Where in your own reasoning about God might you be inconsistent? Resolve to submit your logic to revelation, not the other way around.
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God 'was not creator nor merciful' in the beginning—implying God changed. Is there any area of your life where you treat God's attributes as if they can increase or decrease, like He becomes more merciful after you repent? Repent of any thought that limits God's perfection.

Maturidi's Critique of Mu'tazila View of Divine Will and Comparison to Magians
Maturidi draws parallel between Mu'tazila will and Magian evil thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's criticism that the Mu'tazila concept of divine will resembles the Magian idea of an evil thought, both positing an unoriginated event from God.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila will is like the Magian 'evil thought' — both claim an uncreated event from God.

After critiquing their proof for the world's temporality, Maturidi attacks the Mu'tazila notion of will. He draws a striking parallel to the Persian Magians, showing that both introduce a temporal event in God without prior will or choice, leading to absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the source text and its translation. Focus on the Mu'tazila view of will and the Magian comparison.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim about will
The Mu'tazila say God existed, then a will occurred from Him by which the world was created, without that will itself being willed or chosen.
This means the will is an unoriginated event in God.
3
Understand the Magian parallel
The Magians say God existed, then an evil thought occurred from which came Iblis (Satan) and all evil. Maturidi notes the structural similarity: both posit a spontaneous event in God without prior will.
The Mu'tazila call this 'will', the Magians call it 'thought'.

?

In what specific areas of your life do you need to submit to the truth that His will is perfectly intentional and wise? What would change in your trust if you truly believed that every event from Him is chosen?
?
The Mu'tazila and Magians both introduced an unplanned origin for evil in God. When you face trials, do you ever blame God or question His justice? How can you fight the temptation to see His decrees as thoughtless or arbitrary, and instead see them as part of a deliberate, wise plan for your good?
?
Maturidi points out that using a different word (will vs. thought) does not change the flawed reality. Are there areas in your faith where you use pious language to cover an underlying belief that does not align with the Quran and Sunnah? Ask Allah to purify your heart and your understanding of His attributes.
