The Error of Denying Divine Attributes
Maturidi presents the argument of those who deny divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning of those who deny that God has essential names and attributes, and why Maturidi considers this a mistaken approach.

Core Idea
Fear of anthropomorphism can lead to denying God's attributes, which is a deeper error.

Maturidi begins by presenting the view of a group who, out of excessive caution, rejected the idea that God has essential attributes or names. They argued that since names in the created world imply similarity, applying names to God would compromise His uniqueness. This lesson examines their argument and its logical pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' argument
Read the Arabic passage that describes the opponents' denial of essential attributes and names. Note their core claim: that having a name implies similarity.
This passage is a refutation; Maturidi sets up their position before correcting it.
2
Identify the analogy with place
The opponents draw an analogy: just as saying God is in a place leads to anthropomorphism, so does naming Him. Find this analogy and understand its role.
They extend the argument to deny calling God 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful'.
3
Reflect on the flaw
Consider why Maturidi ends this section with 'With God is success.' He implies their reasoning is flawed and will now provide the correct position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أنكر قوم أن يكون صفة الله ذاتية يوصف [بها]، أو اسم ذاتى يعرف به، وظنوا أن ذلك يوجب التشابه؛ إذ له اسم كما كان لغيره، وقالوا: وإذ لم يجز أن يكون له موافقة في شيء من جملة الخلق على الإشارة كان أدلة تحقيق الموافقة بالاسم الذى لكل شيء أحرى؛ ولهذا أنكروا إليه القول بالشيء والعاليم والقادر، وضربوا له المثل – بأن القول له وفيه بالمكان؛ إذ يوجب التشبيه والحد، فهو بكل مكان، كذلك إذ للامكنة نهاية، فالوصف بها وبالواحد منها واحد، فمثله الأول، وبالله التوفيق. وأما الأصل عندنا أن لله أسماء ذاتية يُسمّى بها نحو قوله الرحمن، لكن الوصف له وصفات ذاتية بها يوصف نحو العلم بالأشياء والقدرة عليها، والاسم إنما هو بما يحتمله وسعنا وتبلغه عبارتنا بالضرورة، إذ سبيل ذلك إنما هو عن المعروف فى الشاهد، وذلك يوجب التشابه فى القول؛ إذ عن معروف به في الشاهد قدر، ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفى المفهوم من الشاهد لينفى به الشبه، ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمى به غيرنا نسميه، لكنه إذ كان الشاهد دليله و به يجب معرفته / فمنه قدر اسمه على ما يقرب من الفهم بما يريد به، وإن كان الله يتعالى عن أن يكون له مثال أو شبه. ألا ترى أن العبارة التى بها نسميه عالما قادرا في الألسن مختلفة من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف، فيدلك أن الاسماء التي نسميه بها عبارات عما
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Some people denied that God has an essential attribute by which He is described, or an essential name by which He is known, and they thought that necessitates similarity; for He has a name just as others have. They said: Since it is not permissible for Him to have likeness in anything of creation in terms of reference, then the evidence for verifying likeness by the name that everything has is more appropriate. Hence they denied the statement about Him of 'thing', 'knower', and 'powerful', and they drew an analogy – that the statement about Him and in Him is like place; for it necessitates anthropomorphism and delimitation, so He is in every place, likewise since places have an end, describing with them and with one of them is one, so His likeness is the first. And with God is success. As for the principle with us: that God has essential names by which He is named, like His saying 'the Merciful', but the description of Him is by essential attributes by which He is described, like knowledge of things and power over them. And the name is only according to what our capacity can bear and our expression reaches by necessity, for the way of that is only from what is known in the witnessed world, and that necessitates similarity in speech; for it is from what is known in the witnessed world in some measure, but necessity has released us to negate the concept from the witnessed world in order to negate similarity, and we name Him with what I mentioned by necessity. And if our capacity could bear naming Him with what none other is named, we would name Him so, but since the witnessed world is His proof and by it He must be known, from it His name is measured to what is close to understanding by what He intends, even though God is exalted above having any likeness or similar. Do you not see that the expressions by which we name Him 'knower', 'powerful' are different in languages without there being any difference? This indicates that the names by which we name Him are expressions of what...
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation and positive exposition. Maturidi begins by presenting the view of those who deny that God has essential attributes or names, arguing that having a name like others implies similarity. They extend this to deny that God can be called 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful', and they make an analogy with place, which leads to anthropomorphism and delimitation. Maturidi then states the correct Sunni principle: God indeed has essential names (e.g., al-Rahman) and essential attributes (e.g., knowledge, power). The name is only what our capacity and expression can attain by necessity, derived from what is known in the witnessed world. This might seem to imply similarity, but necessity allows us to use these terms while negating any concept of similarity from the witnessed world. We name God by necessity, and if our capacity allowed, we would use unique names. However, because the witnessed world is the means to know God, we use terms close to understanding while affirming that God transcends any likeness. He gives the example of different languages for 'knower' and 'powerful' showing that the names are expressions of meanings, not things that force similarity.

Key Terms
الصفة الذاتية
الاسم الذاتي
التشابه
المكان
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having an attribute or name like 'knower' implies God is like a knower in creation.
Correction: Maturidi will later explain that such terms are used by necessity while negating any concept of similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents mentioned in the passage, why does having a name for God imply similarity?
A) Because names are arbitrary
B) Because names in the world indicate similarity
C) Because God's name is unknown
D) Because prophets said so

Key Takeaway
Some people erroneously think that describing God with attributes or names necessarily makes Him similar to creation, leading them to deny these descriptions altogether.

Maturidi's Principle: Names and Attributes Without Similarity
Maturidi explains how divine names and attributes are used without implying similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that God has essential names and attributes, and how he resolves the apparent similarity by using terms from the witnessed world while negating any likeness.

Core Idea
We name God using terms from creation, but we negate any similarity in meaning.

After refuting the opponents' position, Maturidi establishes the correct Sunni doctrine: God indeed has essential names (like al-Rahman) and essential attributes (like knowledge). The challenge is to use language from the created world without implying similarity. Maturidi explains that necessity permits this, and the diversity of languages shows that names are mere expressions of meanings, not indicators of likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic from 'وأما الأصل عندنا' to the end. Focus on Maturidi's affirmation of essential names and attributes.
He explicitly states that God has essential names like 'al-Rahman' and essential attributes like knowledge.
2
Understand the role of necessity
Maturidi says we use names from the witnessed world by necessity. Notice how he balances this with negating the concept of similarity.
The phrase 'الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفى المفهوم من الشاهد' is key.
3
Consider the linguistic evidence
Reflect on the example of different languages for 'knower' and 'powerful'. What does this indicate about the nature of these names?
Maturidi argues that despite different expressions, the meaning is one, showing names are expressions, not things that force similarity.
4
Conclude with divine transcendence
Maturidi ends by affirming that God is exalted above any likeness. How does this resolve the tension?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أنكر قوم أن يكون صفة الله ذاتية يوصف [بها]، أو اسم ذاتى يعرف به، وظنوا أن ذلك يوجب التشابه؛ إذ له اسم كما كان لغيره، وقالوا: وإذ لم يجز أن يكون له موافقة في شيء من جملة الخلق على الإشارة كان أدلة تحقيق الموافقة بالاسم الذى لكل شيء أحرى؛ ولهذا أنكروا إليه القول بالشيء والعاليم والقادر، وضربوا له المثل – بأن القول له وفيه بالمكان؛ إذ يوجب التشبيه والحد، فهو بكل مكان، كذلك إذ للامكنة نهاية، فالوصف بها وبالواحد منها واحد، فمثله الأول، وبالله التوفيق. وأما الأصل عندنا أن لله أسماء ذاتية يُسمّى بها نحو قوله الرحمن، لكن الوصف له وصفات ذاتية بها يوصف نحو العلم بالأشياء والقدرة عليها، والاسم إنما هو بما يحتمله وسعنا وتبلغه عبارتنا بالضرورة، إذ سبيل ذلك إنما هو عن المعروف فى الشاهد، وذلك يوجب التشابه فى القول؛ إذ عن معروف به في الشاهد قدر، ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفى المفهوم من الشاهد لينفى به الشبه، ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمى به غيرنا نسميه، لكنه إذ كان الشاهد دليله و به يجب معرفته / فمنه قدر اسمه على ما يقرب من الفهم بما يريد به، وإن كان الله يتعالى عن أن يكون له مثال أو شبه. ألا ترى أن العبارة التى بها نسميه عالما قادرا في الألسن مختلفة من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف، فيدلك أن الاسماء التي نسميه بها عبارات عما
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Some people denied that God has an essential attribute by which He is described, or an essential name by which He is known, and they thought that necessitates similarity; for He has a name just as others have. They said: Since it is not permissible for Him to have likeness in anything of creation in terms of reference, then the evidence for verifying likeness by the name that everything has is more appropriate. Hence they denied the statement about Him of 'thing', 'knower', and 'powerful', and they drew an analogy – that the statement about Him and in Him is like place; for it necessitates anthropomorphism and delimitation, so He is in every place, likewise since places have an end, describing with them and with one of them is one, so His likeness is the first. And with God is success. As for the principle with us: that God has essential names by which He is named, like His saying 'the Merciful', but the description of Him is by essential attributes by which He is described, like knowledge of things and power over them. And the name is only according to what our capacity can bear and our expression reaches by necessity, for the way of that is only from what is known in the witnessed world, and that necessitates similarity in speech; for it is from what is known in the witnessed world in some measure, but necessity has released us to negate the concept from the witnessed world in order to negate similarity, and we name Him with what I mentioned by necessity. And if our capacity could bear naming Him with what none other is named, we would name Him so, but since the witnessed world is His proof and by it He must be known, from it His name is measured to what is close to understanding by what He intends, even though God is exalted above having any likeness or similar. Do you not see that the expressions by which we name Him 'knower', 'powerful' are different in languages without there being any difference? This indicates that the names by which we name Him are expressions of what...
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation and positive exposition. Maturidi begins by presenting the view of those who deny that God has essential attributes or names, arguing that having a name like others implies similarity. They extend this to deny that God can be called 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful', and they make an analogy with place, which leads to anthropomorphism and delimitation. Maturidi then states the correct Sunni principle: God indeed has essential names (e.g., al-Rahman) and essential attributes (e.g., knowledge, power). The name is only what our capacity and expression can attain by necessity, derived from what is known in the witnessed world. This might seem to imply similarity, but necessity allows us to use these terms while negating any concept of similarity from the witnessed world. We name God by necessity, and if our capacity allowed, we would use unique names. However, because the witnessed world is the means to know God, we use terms close to understanding while affirming that God transcends any likeness. He gives the example of different languages for 'knower' and 'powerful' showing that the names are expressions of meanings, not things that force similarity.

Maturidi's Principle on Essential Names and Attributes
Maturidi affirms essential names/attributes and explains their derivation from shahid with negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah has essential names and attributes derived from the witnessed world out of necessity, and why a negation of similarity is always attached.

Core Idea
Divine names come from creation but negate all likeness.

Maturidi begins this passage by affirming that Allah has essential names and attributes, such as al-Rahman and knowledge. However, he recognizes a challenge: our language is rooted in the created world, so how can we speak of Allah without likening Him to creation? In this lesson, you will see how he resolves this by insisting on a necessary negation of similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle
Read from 'وأما الأصل عندنا' to 'والقدرة عليها'. Notice that Maturidi affirms both essential names and attributes.
2
Understand the derivation from shahid
Read the next sentences explaining that names come from the witnessed world by necessity. See how he admits this implies likeness but then introduces the negation.
The key phrase is 'ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد لينفى به الشبه'.
3
Reflect on the ideal vs. practical
Read the conditional sentences about what we would do if capacity allowed. This shows the limitation of human language.
4
Summarize the method
In your own words, describe Maturidi's solution: using names from shahid but negating similarity.
This is the core of his principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأصل عندنا أن لله أسماء ذاتية يُسمّى بها نحو قوله الرحمن، لكن الوصف له وصفات ذاتية بها يوصف نحو العلم بالأشياء والقدرة عليها. والاسم إنما هو بما يحتمله وسعنا وتبلغه عبارتنا بالضرورة، إذ سبيل ذلك إنما هو عن المعروف في الشاهد، وذلك يوجب التشابه في القول؛ إذ عن معروف به في الشاهد قدر، ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد لينفى به الشبه، ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمى به غيرنا نسميه، لكنه إذ كان الشاهد دليله وبه يجب معرفته فمنه قدر اسمه على ما يقرب من الفهم بما يريد به، وإن كان الله يتعالى عن أن يكون له مثال أو شبه. ألا ترى أن العبارة التي بها نسميه عالما قادرا في الألسن مختلفة من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف، فيدلك أن الأسماء التي نسميه بها عبارات عما يقرب إلى الأفهام، لا أنها في الحقيقة أسماؤه. ولما تأخذ القلوب منها معاني يتعالى عنها قرن بالتسمية حرف نفي، فجعل التوحيد إثبات ذات في ضمن نفي، ونفيا في ضمن إثبات على ما فسرت، وبالله التوفيق.
And as for the principle with us: that Allah has essential names by which He is called, such as His saying 'al-Rahman', but He has description and essential attributes by which He is described, such as knowledge of things and power over them. And the name is only according to what our capacity can bear and our expression can reach by necessity, for the way of that is only from what is known in the witnessed [world], and that necessitates likeness in speech; because it is from what is known in the witnessed [world] to a certain extent, but necessity has permitted us to negate the concept from the witnessed [world] in order to negate similarity by it, and we name Him by that which I have mentioned out of necessity. And if our capacity could bear naming Him by what none other is named, we would name Him, but since the witnessed [world] is His proof and by it His knowledge is necessary, then from it His name is determined in a way that brings [understanding] close to the intellect as He intends, even though Allah is exalted above having an example or likeness. Do you not see that the expression by which we name Him 'knower' and 'able' differs in tongues without there being any difference [in meaning]? This shows you that the names by which we name Him are expressions of what brings [understanding] close to minds, not that they are in reality His names. And since hearts derive from them meanings that are exalted above [Him], a particle of negation is joined with the naming, so tawhid is made an affirmation of essence within a negation, and a negation within an affirmation, as I have explained. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's foundational response to the objection. He affirms that Allah has essential names (e.g., al-Rahman) and essential attributes (e.g., knowledge, power). However, he explains that the names we use come from the witnessed world (shahid) out of necessity, because human language is derived from it. This would normally imply likeness, but Allah permits us to negate the concept of similarity (tashbih) from that witnessed meaning. So we use the same terms but with the understanding that they are not like the created counterparts. Ideally, we would use unique terms, but since knowledge of Allah comes through the witnessed world as a proof, our names are approximations for understanding. The diversity of languages for 'knower' and 'able' shows the names are expressions for understanding, not literal names. Moreover, when the heart might take meanings that are too exalted, a negation particle (like 'laysa kamithlihi shay') is attached to tawhid, making tawhid a combination of affirmation of essence and negation of likeness. This is the proper method of affirming attributes without tashbih.

Key Terms
أسماء ذاتية
صفات ذاتية
ضرورة
الشاهد
نفي التشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi reduces divine names to mere metaphors with no real meaning.
Correction: Maturidi insists that the names are used out of necessity and carry real meaning, but with a necessary negation of similarity. They are not empty metaphors.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do we need to negate the concept from the witnessed world?
A) To deny that Allah has names
B) To avoid attributing similarity (tashbih) to Allah
C) Because the witnessed world is not real
D) To affirm that Allah is absolutely unknowable

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that divine names are necessarily taken from the created world but always negated of any likeness to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah's names like al-Basīr (the All-Seeing), do you actively negate any resemblance to your own seeing? What mental habit can you form to ensure your heart affirms His perfection without likeness?

?
If you could invent a unique name for Allah that no creature shares, what would it be? But you cannot. How does this limitation move you to treasure the names He has given, while always negating any trace of anthropomorphism from your worship?

Tawhid as Affirmation and Negation
Maturidi concludes that divine names are expressions and defines tawhid as affirmation with negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how linguistic diversity shows that divine names are expressions for understanding, and why tawhid is structured as affirmation of essence within negation of likeness.

Core Idea
Linguistic diversity proves divine names are expressions, not literal labels; tawhid affirms Allah's essence while negating all resemblance.

Maturidi continues his argument by pointing to a fascinating observation: the words for 'knower' and 'able' differ across languages, yet the meaning is the same. This, he argues, shows that the names we use for Allah are expressions (عبارات) aimed at bringing understanding close to our minds, not literal names in His own language. He then concludes that tawhid must combine affirmation and negation, as a safeguard against the heart deriving inappropriate meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic argument
Read from 'ألا ترى' to 'لا أنها في الحقيقة أسماؤه'. Notice the observation about different languages having different words for the same attribute.
This is a key empirical point.
2
Understand the implication
Maturidi infers that the names are expressions (عبارات) to aid understanding, not literal names.
This counters the idea that the Arabic names are somehow the 'real' names of Allah in a direct sense.
3
See the need for negation
Read the next sentence: since hearts take meanings that are too exalted, a negation particle is joined.
The negation particle is like 'ليس كمثله شيء'.
4
Define tawhid
Maturidi defines tawhid as 'affirmation of essence within negation, and negation within affirmation'. Ensure you understand this reciprocal relationship.
This is the core definition.

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's foundational response to the objection. He affirms that Allah has essential names (e.g., al-Rahman) and essential attributes (e.g., knowledge, power). Tawhid as Negation and Affirmation
Maturidi defines tawhid as affirmation within negation and negation within affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defines tawhid as a combination of negation and affirmation in the context of divine names.

Core Idea
Tawhid is affirming God's essence by negating all similarity to creation.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid reveals the logical structure behind the Islamic declaration of oneness. Maturidi explains that divine names are not literal descriptors of God's essence but serve to bring Him close to human understanding while safeguarding transcendence. He shows how every affirmation about God is paired with a negation, forming the core of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi uses the terms 'negation' (nafy) and 'affirmation' (ithbat).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two components
Identify where Maturidi mentions negation and affirmation. Understand that tawhid is described as 'affirmation of essence within negation' and 'negation within affirmation'.
This is the core teaching.
3
Reflect on why this structure is necessary
Consider why Maturidi says a letter of negation is coupled with the name. How does this prevent misunderstanding about God's nature?
Think about anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقرب إلى الأفهام، لا أنها فى الحقيقة أسماؤه . ولما تأخذ القلوب منها معانى يتعالى عنها قرن بالتسمية حرف نفى ، فجعل التوحيد إثبات ذات في ضمن نفى ، ونفيا في ضمن إثبات على ما فسرت ، وبالله التوفيق .
It brings close to understanding, not that they are in reality His names. And when hearts take from them meanings that He is exalted above, He coupled the name with a letter of negation, so He made tawhid an affirmation of essence within negation, and a negation within affirmation, as I have explained. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of tawhid (divine oneness) as it relates to the divine names. Maturidi states that the names brought by revelation bring God close to human understanding, but they are not literally His names in a way that implies likeness. Since human hearts might take from these names meanings that are unworthy of God, He attached a letter of negation (such as the prefixed 'lā' in 'lā ilāha illā Allāh' or the word 'laysa' in 'laysa kamithlihi shay') to the act of naming. Thus, tawhid becomes an affirmation of God's essence (dhāt) enclosed within a negation, and a negation enclosed within an affirmation—meaning that one affirms God's existence while negating any similarity to creation. This is the method of the Qur'an. The passage concludes with a prayer asking for divine success.

Key Terms
التوحيد (tawḥīd)
نفى (nafy)
إثبات (ithbāt)
ذات (dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that affirming divine names implies anthropomorphism.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the names are qualified by negation, so affirmation of names always includes negation of likeness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is tawhid structured in relation to divine names?
A) Pure affirmation without negation
B) Pure negation without affirmation
C) Affirmation of essence within negation, and negation within affirmation
D) Affirmation of attributes without any negation

Key Takeaway
Tawhid requires that every affirmation about God is accompanied by a negation of any likeness to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh', do you consciously feel both the negation of everything else and the affirmation of Allah alone? How will you make this double meaning alive in your heart starting today?
?
The names of God bring Him close to your understanding, but you must not take them literally. Is there any name or attribute of Allah that you imagine in a human-like way? What step will you take to purify your mental image?
?
If someone asked you 'What is the core of your faith?', would you explain it as both a rejection of false gods and a wholehearted affirmation of the One God? How can you ensure your daily actions reflect both aspects?

The Error of Denying God's Names Out of Fear of Anthropomorphism
Maturidi refutes those who deny God's names to avoid tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's names and attributes to avoid anthropomorphism (tashbih) actually leads to complete negation (ta'til) and undermines rational knowledge of God.

Core Idea
Denying names to avoid tashbih leads to ta'til, making God unknowable.

Maturidi opens by describing the view of a group that denies God's names out of excessive caution against comparing God to creation. He shows how their position backfires, resulting in a God who cannot be proven by any evidence, reducing theology to blind imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' claim
Read the passage and note how the opponents describe the intellect and origination (ibda').
They claim everything is manifested in the intellect through ibda'.
2
Understand Maturidi's logical refutation
Maturidi argues that if God did not know, could not, or did not will what is to be manifested, the origination would be like a natural process, implying ignorance or powerlessness.
3
Recognize the consequence: ta'til
By denying names and attributes to avoid tashbih, they fall into ta'til, stripping God of all description and making it impossible to adduce evidence for Him.
4
Reflect on the irony
Their attempt to preserve transcendence results in a worse error: complete negation and blind imitation (taqlid).
Maturidi declares this position far from the truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيمد الهيولى [؟]، يقولون : كان العقل بالإبداع ، والإبداع علته ، مبروز فيه كل شيء يكون ، ومحال أن يُبرزه بالإبداع من لم يعلم ما يكون أو لم يعلم ما يكون أو من لا يقدر على أن يبرزه أو لا من يريد أن يكون مبروزا ، فيخرج الإبداع منه خروج فعل ذى طبع من حيث لا يشعر به ولا يعلمه ولا يوصف بالقدرة [عليه] ، فيكون الله تعالى عنده فى نفى الصفات عنه والأسماء كراهية التشبيه يصير في حد التعطيل ، ويصير بحيث لا شيء عليه بدليل ، ويحصل القول منه على التقليد ، وذلك بعيد ، والله الموفق . مع ما يقال : الله اسمه أو هو اسم غيره ، فيرجع في الحقيقة إلى أنه اسم العقل ، والرحمن اسم النفس ، وعلى هذا مذهبهم. وأبوا الاسم كراهة التشبيه ، ثم جعلوا المعبود باسم الإله ، والرحمن والرحيم أغيارا لا يحصى عددهم ، وأجزاء يصعب احصاؤهم ، فكأن الرسل جاءوا عندهم بعبادة العدد لا بالتوحيد ، والله المستعان . ثم يقال لهم عند قولهم ليس له اسم ، ما تعنون بقولكم ليس له ، اسم ذاتى ولا صفة ذاتية ؟ فلا يجدون السبيل إلى أن يعبروا عن أنفسهم بما قالوا ليس له اسم، ويبطل عملتهم الذي قالوا : الله الذى ليس له اسم ذاتى ، ثم زعموا أن له اسما من غيره نحو المبدع بإبداع هو علة المبدع هو العال لا المعلول ولا علة ؛ لأن كل معلول يجوز أن يصير فيقال / له إذ جعل اسمه عن غيره : أكان ما حقق له غيره ذلك إلا أو سُمّى [به] ؟ فإن قال : لا ، له أن يسميه ما شاء من الأغيار ، والعلة والمعلول ؛ إذ بغيره استحق ، لأنه كذلك يقول : كان ولا علة ولا معلول ، فإذا هو قول كان بحق المجاز لا بالحقيقة بالضرورة ، فأوجب هذا الاسم له غيره من غير أن كان منه ما استوجب. فإن قال : كان منه الإبداع ، قيل : كان منه الإبداع بعد أن لم يكن ، حتى حقق له الاسم بأن كان به من أى وجه ، حتى أوجب له الاسم ، فيلزم جعله بإبداع إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وذلك محال ، ولا يقول به ، فيجب أن يكون الإبداع بذاته ، فيكون لم يزل مبدعا ، وفي ذلك كله وجوب الاسم الذاتى له بالضرورة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
In the [?] of hyle, they say: the intellect was by origination (ibda'), and origination is its cause, everything that is [?] is manifested in it. And it is impossible that one who does not know what will be, or who cannot manifest it, or who does not will it to be manifested, should manifest it by origination. So origination issues from it like the action of something with a nature, without it perceiving or knowing or being described with power over it. Thus God, according to them, in negating attributes and names out of dislike for anthropomorphism (tashbih), falls into the limit of negation (ta'til), and becomes such that nothing can be adduced as evidence for Him, and their statement is based on imitation (taqlid), and that is far. And God is the Giver of success. Along with what is said: 'Allah is His name, or it is a name of another,' so in reality it returns to being the name of the intellect, and al-Rahman is the name of the soul, and this is their doctrine. And they refused the name out of dislike for tashbih, then they made the object of worship by the name al-Ilah, and al-Rahman and al-Rahim as others whose number cannot be counted, and parts whose enumeration is difficult, as if the messengers came to them with the worship of number, not with tawhid. And God is the Helper. Then it is said to them when they say 'He has no name': what do you mean by your statement 'He has no name'? Is it that He has no essential name nor essential attribute? Then they find no way to express themselves by what they said: 'He has no name,' and their practice by which they said 'Allah who has no essential name' is invalidated. Then they claimed that He has a name from another, like 'the Originator (al-Mubdi')' by an origination which is the cause of the originated, it is the cause not the effect nor cause; because every effect can become, so it is said to him when he made His name from another: 'Was what others actualized for Him that which [called Him] by it?' If he says: no, he has the right to name Him whatever he wishes of others, and the cause and the effect, since by another He deserves it, because he says: He was without cause and without effect. Then it is a statement that is true by metaphor (majaz) not by reality (haqiqa) necessarily. So this name obliged another for Him without there being from Him what warranted it. If he says: origination was from Him, it is said: origination was from Him after it was not, until the name was actualized for Him by His being through it from some aspect, so that it obliged the name for Him. Then it necessitates making Him by origination ad infinitum, which is impossible, and he does not hold it. So origination must be by His essence, so He is eternally originator. And in all that is the necessity of the essential name (al-ism al-dhati) for Him necessarily. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a group that denies God's names and attributes out of fear of anthropomorphism (tashbih). Maturidi begins by describing their position: they claim that the intellect (al-'aql) was brought into being by origination (ibda'), and that origination is its cause. Everything that exists is manifested in this intellect. Then he argues that it is impossible for such a manifestation to occur from one who does not know what will be, or cannot manifest it, or does not will it. This shows that their view leads to God being described as ignorant, powerless, or unwilling—which is absurd. Consequently, by negating attributes and names to avoid tashbih, they fall into the opposite extreme of ta'til (stripping God of all positive description), making it impossible to establish any proof for God, and their belief becomes mere imitation (taqlid) rather than rational evidence. Next, Maturidi discusses their claim that 'Allah' is a name for something else—specifically, the intellect—and that 'al-Rahman' is the name of the soul. This reduces worship to the worship of number and multiplicity rather than tawhid. Then he presents a direct objection: when they say that God has no name, what exactly do they mean? If they mean no essential name or attribute, they cannot even articulate their own position. They then claim that God has a name derived from another, such as 'the Originator' through an origination that is the cause. Maturidi dissects this: if the name is derived from another, then that other is what actualized the name, and the name is applied metaphorically (majaz) rather than truly (haqiqa). Moreover, if origination from God is the basis for the name, then if origination occurs after not having occurred, it would lead to an infinite regress of origins. The only consistent conclusion is that origination is by God's essence, so He is eternally an originator. This necessarily entails that God has an essential name (al-ism al-dhati). The passage ends by affirming the necessity of this essential name.

Key Terms
إبداع
تشبيه
تعطيل
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying all names and attributes preserves God's transcendence.
Correction: It actually leads to ta'til, making God unknowable and evidence impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence of denying God's names and attributes out of fear of tashbih?
A) It affirms tawhid.
B) It leads to ta'til.
C) It shows God's mercy.
D) It confirms prophecy.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's names and attributes to avoid anthropomorphism actually strips God of all description, making rational knowledge impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to describe God, do you ever hold back from using His names and attributes because you're afraid of making Him sound like a human? What would it take for you to affirm His names without reservation, while knowing He is beyond comparison?
?
This lesson says that denying names leads to ta'til—a state where no evidence can point to God. Is there any belief you hold about God that you have accepted on imitation rather than evidence? Are you willing to examine it critically, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If you cannot describe God with His names, how can you truly know Him? What is one name of God that you have avoided reflecting on because it seemed too 'human'? Commit today to studying that name until it transforms your understanding of God's perfection.

The Necessity of God's Essential Name
Maturidi argues for the necessity of God's essential name by refuting derived names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must have an essential name, and why derived names alone lead to infinite regress and contradiction.

Core Idea
God's essential name is necessary; derived names imply infinite regress, which is impossible.

In this passage, Maturidi continues his critique by examining the claim that God has only derived names, such as 'Originator' through an act of origination. He demonstrates that this leads to an infinite regress of origins, proving that God must be eternally originator and thus possess an essential name.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' shift
They say God has no essential name but a name from another, e.g., al-Mubdi' from ibda'.
Note the move from absolute denial to derived names.
2
Follow Maturidi's objection
If the name is derived from something else, that thing actualizes the name, making it metaphorical, not real.
3
Analyze the infinite regress argument
If ibda' occurs at a point, then before it God was not originator, so the name depends on a temporal act, leading to an endless chain.
4
Conclude: essential name necessary
Therefore, ibda' must be by God's essence, so He is eternally originator, proving an essential name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Divine Attributes

Absolute Names Do Not Imply Anthropomorphism
Maturidi states that absolute names do not entail tashbih, supported by examples from creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that sharing names between God and creation does not entail similarity in essence.

Core Idea
Shared names do not erase fundamental differences.

Maturidi begins by stating a foundational principle: the absolute name (al-ism al-mutlaq) does not necessitate anthropomorphism (tashbih). He supports this with examples from the witnessed world where opposites share the same name yet remain distinct. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Maturidi's main claim: that absolute names do not imply tashbih. Then read the translation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the examples of opposites
Maturidi lists pairs of opposites: life/death, light/darkness, evil/good, disbelief/faith. Notice that each pair shares a name but they are fundamentally different.
3
Understand the logical implication
If the shared name implied similarity, there would be no known opposition. Thus names indicate difference and agreement, not essential likeness.
4
Reflect on the broader argument
Maturidi extends the reasoning: if affirming a name for God implied similarity, then negating it would also imply similarity (from the opposite direction). Therefore, names do not necessitate tashbih.
This reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الأصل عندنا أن الاسم المطلق لا يحتمل تحقق التشبيه لما وجد كل متضاد في الشاهد تحت الاسم ، نحو الحياة والموت والنور والظلمة ، والشر والخير ، والكفر والإيمان ، لكل اسم على حدة ، فلو كان بالاسم المطلق تشاب لكان لا تضاد يعلم، ولا اختلاف بالاسماء ، ثبت أنها جعلت لما يراد من الاختلاف والاتفاق الذى [لا] يعلم حقيقة ذلك لو يكن له اسم . ولو كان بموافقة الاسم عند نفى المعنى الذى له المعلول من المسمى تشابه في الشاهد لكان لو لم يسم للعالم العلو والسفلى والمبدع الأول والثاني ، ولكان بين من زعموا أن له اسما وبين غيره موافقة فى نفى الاسم مع جميع الأشياء ، على أنه يجد في القول بواحد الخلق نفى التشبيه ، وإن كان من حيث اسم الآحاد اجتماع وبعد ، فإن الإبداع عنده علة ، ولا يوصف بالشيء ؛ لما كان به الأشياء ، والأعراض كلها لا توصف بعالم ولا قادر ولا نحو ذلك ، فلو كان في اثبات الاسم تشابه لكان فى نفى ذلك كذلك من الوجه الذي ذكرت ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Then the principle with us is that the absolute name does not admit the actuality of anthropomorphism (tashbih), because in the witnessed world every pair of opposites falls under a name, such as life and death, light and darkness, evil and good, disbelief and faith—each with a name individually. So if the absolute name implied similarity (tashabuh), there would be no known opposition, and no difference between names. It is established that they are made for what is intended of difference and agreement, the reality of which would not be known if there were no name for it. And if by agreement of the name, when the meaning from which the effect proceeds is negated, there were similarity in the witnessed world, then if the world had not been named 'high' and 'low' and 'first originator' and 'second', then between those who claim that He has a name and others there would be agreement in negating the name along with all things, but one finds in the statement 'one of creation' the negation of tashbih, although from the perspective of the name 'one' there is gathering. And then: indeed origination with him is a cause, and He is not described as the thing; because of what things come to be, and all accidents are not described as 'knowing' or 'powerful' and so on. So if in affirming the name there were similarity, then in negating it there would also be similarity from the aspect I mentioned. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a foundational principle by Maturidi. He asserts that the absolute name (al-ism al-mutlaq) does not necessitate anthropomorphism (tashbih). His proof is from the witnessed world (al-shahid): opposites such as life and death, light and darkness, good and evil, belief and disbelief each fall under a common name, yet they are fundamentally different. If the shared name implied similarity, there would be no distinction between opposites. Thus, names are tools to indicate both difference and agreement. He then argues that if agreement in name alone constituted similarity, then denying names would be necessary to avoid similarity, but this is not the case. He references the idea that 'origination' (ibda') is a cause (illa) and that God is not described as the thing that comes to be; similarly, accidents (a'rad) are not described as knowing or powerful. Therefore, if affirming a name for God implied similarity, then negating that name would also imply a kind of similarity (by negating a common description). So the ascription of names to God does not entail tashbih. The passage ends with a standard formula.

Key Terms
اسم مطلق
تشبيه
ضد
إبداع
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief that because God and creatures share names like 'Living' or 'Knowing', they must be similar in essence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that shared names do not erase fundamental differences, as seen in opposites like life and death which are named but opposite.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main argument that absolute names do not imply anthropomorphism (tashbih)?
A) Names are merely metaphorical and not real.
B) In the witnessed world, opposites like life and death share a name but are fundamentally different.
C) God's names are unlike any created names.
D) Names can be applied only by analogy.

Key Takeaway
Absolute names do not imply essential similarity; they indicate distinct realities, as demonstrated by opposites in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Knowing' or 'Powerful', does your mind automatically compare His knowledge to yours? What specific thought pattern can you adopt to prevent this subtle tashbih, based on the lesson's argument from opposites?
?
You call yourself a 'believer' (mu'min) and God is also 'The Believer' (al-Mu'min). Have you ever unconsciously assumed your faith resembles His in quality? How does the lesson's emphasis on 'difference and agreement' challenge you to realign your understanding?
?
Think of a recent moment when you assumed God's attributes are similar to a human attribute because the name is the same. What concrete step will you take tonight to remind yourself of the absolute distinction this passage teaches?

The Name Creator Necessitates Eternal Creation
Maturidi argues that the name 'Creator' necessitates eternal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that the name 'Creator' implies eternal creation, and why an infinite regress of creations is impossible.

Core Idea
God's name 'Creator' is essential, not acquired; otherwise infinite regress follows.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise yet powerful argument linking the divine name 'Creator' to the necessity of eternal creation. Using reason, he shows that if creation were not eternal, we would fall into an impossible infinite regress. This passage is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and the literal translation. Let the words sink in before analyzing.
Notice the key terms: إبداع, اسم ذاتي, لم يزل مبدعا.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: premise about the name, consequence of infinite regress, judgment of impossibility, conclusion of eternal creation, and final necessity of the essential name.
3
Understand the concept of 'impossible' (محال)
Why is infinite regress impossible? In kalam, an actual infinite chain of dependent events is rationally impossible because it would require an actual infinite, which is contradictory.
This is a key principle in proofs for God's existence.
4
Connect to the essential name
The argument concludes that the name 'Creator' is essential (ذاتي), meaning it has always been true of God, since He has never ceased to create.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Summarize: Because an infinite regress is impossible, God must be eternally creating by His essence. Therefore, the name 'Creator' is necessary and essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوجب له الاسم ، فيلزم جعله بإبداع إلى ما لا نهاية له ، وذلك محال ، ولا يقول به ، فيجب أن يكون الإبداع بذاته ، فيكون لم يزل مبدعا ، وفي ذلك كله وجوب الاسم الذاتى له بالضرورة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The name necessitated for Him, so it would require making Him create ad infinitum, and that is impossible, and He does not say it, so creation must be by His essence, so He has never ceased to be a creator, and in all that is the necessity of the essential name for Him necessarily, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage presents a concise proof by Imam Maturidi regarding the divine attribute of creation. The argument begins by noting that if God were to acquire the name 'Creator' only at the moment of creating, then before that He would not have been a creator. But since the name is eternal and essential, it would imply that God was not always a creator, which would then require a prior act of creation to give Him the name, leading to an infinite regress of acts of creation. This infinite regress is impossible (محال). Therefore, the act of creation must be by His very essence (بذاته), meaning God has never ceased to be a creator (لم يزل مبدعا). The conclusion is that the name 'Creator' is an essential name (الاسم الذاتي) necessarily belonging to God. The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' is a formula of reliance on God.

Key Terms
الإبداع
الاسم الذاتي
لم يزل مبدعا
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God acquired the attribute of creation in time, i.e., He was not always a creator.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this would lead to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, creation must be eternal by essence, and the name 'Creator' is essential.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must God be eternally a creator?
A) Because the name 'Creator' could be acquired later.
B) Because otherwise an infinite regress of creations would be required to bestow the name, which is impossible.
C) Because God is generous and must create.
D) Because creation is necessary for goodness.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Creator' is essential to God, meaning He has always been creating, because otherwise an infinite regress of acts would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God has never ceased to be a creator, how would that change your awareness of His presence and activity in every moment of your life? Are you living as if He is constantly creating your breath, your heartbeat, and your next thought?
?
The proof shows that the name 'Creator' is essential, not an acquired title. Does your faith treat God's attributes as mere descriptions, or do you recognize them as defining His very being? When you say 'Allah is the Creator', do you attach any condition to it, such as 'He created the world and then stopped'?
?
If the infinite regress argument exposes the impossibility of a chain of dependent creations, what does that say about your own reliance on causes other than Allah? Are you trusting in secondary causes as if they are independent, while ignoring the Eternal Creator who is the sole source of all existence?

God's Actions Are Inherently Wise: Maturidi's Proof
Maturidi proves that God's actions cannot lack wisdom due to His knowledge and self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that God's actions cannot lack wisdom, based on God's attributes of knowledge and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Only ignorance or need would cause unwise action; God has neither.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a concise rational argument for why every action of God is necessarily wise. He identifies what could cause an action to be unwise—ignorance or fear of loss—and shows that neither applies to Allah. The argument is reinforced by a Quranic verse that establishes God's sovereignty over questioning. Let's examine Maturidi's logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice the logical structure.
2
Identify the two causes of unwise action
Maturidi says an action departs from wisdom due to ignorance or fear of losing benefit. Identify these two.
3
Connect to God's attributes
God is knowing (no ignorance) and self-sufficient (no need/fear of loss). Therefore His actions must be wise.
4
Consider the Quranic reference
Maturidi cites Quran 21:16-23 to negate the idea of playfulness. Reflect on how this supports the argument.
5
Reflect on the statement of woe
The passage ends with a warning against attributing need or foolishness to God. What does this teach about adab (reverence)?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يزل عليم غنى ، فعله لا يحتمل الخروج عن الحكمة ، إذ يخرج الفعل عنها لجهل بها ، أو لما يخاف فوت نفع لو حفظ طريق الحكمة ، فإذا كان الله سبحانه عليما لا يجهل ، غنياً لا يمسه حاجة ينتفع بدفعها بطل أن يخرج فعله عن جهة الحكمة . وسؤال لم ليست فيه الحكمة ؛ ولذلك نفى الله عز وجل توهم اللعب عن فعله فقال : " وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لَاعِبِينَ " إلى قوله : " لا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْأَلُونَ " ، والحق الويل بمن يظن به الحاجة أو فى فعله السفه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
He is eternally knowing, self-sufficient. His action cannot admit departure from wisdom, for an action departs from it due to ignorance of it, or due to fear of losing benefit if the path of wisdom were preserved. So since God, glorified be He, is knowing and not ignorant, self-sufficient and not touched by need from which He would benefit by repelling it, it is invalid that His action should depart from the way of wisdom. And the question 'why' [is asked only when] wisdom is absent; therefore God, mighty and majestic, negated the suspicion of playfulness from His action, saying: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in play' up to His saying: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned.' Woe to the one who supposes need in Him or foolishness in His action. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) that God's actions are necessarily wise. Maturidi presents a conditional argument: an action only departs from wisdom due to ignorance (jahl) or fear of losing benefit. Since God is eternally knowing (ʿalīm) and self-sufficient (ghanī), neither condition applies, so His action cannot lack wisdom. He then cites Quran 21:16-23 to support that God is not questioned about His actions, implying they are inherently wise. The phrase 'wa-sawāl limma laysat fīhi al-ḥikmah' indicates that asking 'why' is only meaningful when wisdom is absent, not in God's case.

Key Terms
حكمة
علم
غنى
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions can be arbitrary or lack wisdom because He is all-powerful.
Correction: Maturidi argues that due to God's knowledge and self-sufficiency, His actions are necessarily wise; they cannot be foolish or arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can God's action never depart from wisdom?
A) Because He is powerful and can do anything.
B) Because He is knowing and self-sufficient, so He has no ignorance or need.
C) Because He created the world for a purpose.
D) Because He is just and must reward the obedient.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that God's knowledge and self-sufficiency necessarily make all His actions wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity that you cannot understand, do you truly believe that God's action is wise, or does your heart secretly accuse Him of ignorance or need? Examine your reaction to a recent trial and repent if needed.
?
The lesson says 'Woe to the one who supposes need in God.' In what ways do you, through your complaining or despair, imply that God needed to give you better or else His wisdom is flawed? Confess and seek refuge.
?
If you truly internalized that God's actions are inherently wise, how would it change your response to unanswered prayers or unjust suffering in the world? What step will you take today to align your heart with this truth?

God as Creator by Essence
Maturidi argues that God is Creator by essence, making the question 'why create?' absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the question 'why did God create?' as logically impossible.

Core Idea
God is Creator by essence; asking 'why He creates' is like asking 'why He knows.'

Maturidi presents a powerful argument: if God is Creator by His very essence, then creating is not an optional act but a necessary expression of His nature. The question 'why create?' becomes as meaningless as asking 'why does God know?' In this lesson, we unpack this argument and see how it grounds the necessity of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style: each phrase builds the argument.
2
Understand the definition
Identify the key claim: 'God is Creator by His essence.' Why is this a name of praise and greatness? What would it imply if God deserved it from another?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of God deriving benefit from anything.
3
Grasp the analogy
Maturidi compares asking 'why create?' to asking 'why decree?' and 'why know?' Reflect on why these questions are considered impossible.
If knowing and decreeing are essential, they are not caused by something else—so asking 'why' is misplaced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : إن الله خالق بذاته ؛ إذ هو اسم المدح والعظمة ، ومخال أن يكون الله سبحانه يستحقه بغيره ؛ لما فيه إيجاب النفع له ، ومن ذلك وصف فعله فهو محتاج وإذ قد ثبت أنه خالق بذاته لم يجز أن لا يكون خالقا البته ، والسؤال عن التم محال كالسؤال عن لم قدر ؟ ولم علم ؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And he said: Indeed, God is Creator by His essence; for it is a name of praise and greatness, and it is impossible that God, glorified be He, should deserve it from another; because that would necessitate benefit for Him. And from that [it follows that] describing His action as needy [is impossible]. And since it is established that He is Creator by His essence, it is not permissible that He should not be a Creator at all. And the question about the 'why' [of the act of creation] is impossible, like the question: why did He decree? and why did He know? There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage is a definitional proof. Maturidi asserts that 'Creator' (khāliq) is a name of praise (madḥ) and greatness (ʿaẓamah) that God deserves by essence, not by acquiring it from another (because that would imply benefit for Him). Therefore, if God is essentially Creator, He must create; not creating would contradict His essence. Hence, asking 'why did He create?' is as futile as asking 'why did He know?' or 'why did He decree?'—these are necessary attributes. The question is fundamentally misguided.

Key Terms
خالق بذاته
المدح
العظمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God created for a purpose we can fully understand, and asking 'why' is a valid theological question.
Correction: Maturidi argues that since God is Creator by essence, creation is necessary. Asking 'why' is like asking why He knows—it's a category mistake.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the question 'why did God create?' as impossible?
A) Because creation is not a real action.
B) Because God is Creator by essence, so creating is necessary; asking 'why' is like asking 'why did He know?'
C) Because God's creation is eternal and uncreated.
D) Because humans cannot understand God's motives.

Key Takeaway
God is Creator by essence, so creation is necessary; questioning the 'why' of creation is as futile as questioning God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When hardships strike, do you catch yourself silently asking God 'why me'? After reading this lesson, will you commit to replacing that question with 'what do You want from me through this?' recognising that your existence itself is not a choice but an outpouring of His essence?
?
If God creates by His essence, then every breath you take is not a random event but a direct manifestation of the Creator. How does that change the way you live this moment? What would it take for you to see your own life as inherently meaningful simply because it is created by Him, without needing to understand the details?
?
The passage equates asking 'why He created' with asking 'why He knows'—both are absurd. Have you ever doubted God's wisdom in your life, as if He needed to justify His actions to you? What specific step can you take today to trust that His creation of you is good, even when you cannot see the purpose?

Does God's Generosity Force Him to Create?
Maturidi presents an objection and implies its invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the claim that God's generosity and power compel Him to create.

Core Idea
God's generosity does not obligate creation; He acts freely.

This passage presents an argument from those who claim that God's attributes of generosity and power necessitate creation. Maturidi includes this viewpoint to refute it, affirming that God's actions are not forced by any external need. We'll examine the argument and its underlying assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its English translation. Notice the two premises given by the unnamed group: generosity requires outpouring, and power cannot be idle.
The passage ends with 'wa-bi-llāh al-tawfīq' which signals Maturidi's departure.
2
Identify the two premises
The group argues: (1) Since God is generous (jawād karīm), He must pour out bounty upon a recipient. (2) Since He is powerful (qādir), a power that does nothing is wasted (ḍāʾiʿah). Therefore, creation is necessary.
3
Understand Maturidi's stance
Maturidi does not explicitly rebut here, but his phrase 'wa-bi-llāh al-tawfīq' (and with Allah is success) indicates he is distancing himself from this reasoning. In earlier discussions, he insists God's actions are voluntary and not compelled by attributes.
This connects to the broader theme of God's absolute freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : إذ هو جواد كريم لزم الوصف بإفاضته الجود ، فلا بد من خلق يكون بخلقه واهبا مفيضا جوده عليه ، وهو قادر ، وقدرة لا تحقق الفعل البته ضائعة ؛ فلذلك خلق ، وبالله التوفيق .
And a group said: Since He is bountiful and generous, the description requires the outpouring of His bounty. So there must be a creation such that by His creation He is a bestower, pouring out His bounty upon it. And He is powerful, and a power that does not actualize the act at all is wasted; therefore He created. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from an unnamed group (possibly a determinist or Ash'ari position) and Maturidi's implicit critique. The group argues: God is described as generous (jawād karīm), so He must pour out bounty, which requires a recipient (creation). Moreover, His power (qudrah) that does not actualize an act would be wasted (ḍāʾiʿah). Therefore, creation is necessary. Maturidi includes this as a claim to be examined, but his tone 'wa-bi-llāh al-tawfīq' suggests he does not fully endorse it, as the necessity of creation contradicts God's absolute freedom. The refutation is implicit in earlier arguments that God's action is not contingent on external need.

Key Terms
جواد كريم
القوة
ضائعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's generosity and power force Him to create, otherwise He would not be generous or powerful.
Correction: God's attributes do not necessitate creation; He acts voluntarily. His generosity is manifest in His free bestowal, not in an obligatory act.

Checkpoint
According to the group's argument in this passage, what is the consequence of God's power if He does not create?
A) It would be incomplete.
B) It would be wasted (ḍāʾiʿah).
C) It would be hidden.
D) It would be unjust.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi rejects that God's generosity and power compel creation; God creates freely, not by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your worship or good deeds are insufficient, as if God's generosity demands a perfect response from you? How does Maturidi's rejection of necessary creation free you from that burden?
?
Are there areas in your life where you feel pressured to constantly 'produce' or 'achieve' because your talents might otherwise be wasted? How does this lesson's view of God's power—perfect without needing to be actualized—challenge that mindset?
?
When you think of God's generosity, do you subconsciously believe He 'needs' your obedience or faith to complete His perfection? What would change if you truly internalized that God's attributes are complete and self-sufficient, and that creation is a free gift?

Creation for the Tested: A Purpose in Creation
Maturidi presents the view that creation is for purposes and specifies the tested as the purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that creation is for purposes and the specific view that it is for the sake of the tested (الممتحن).

Core Idea
Creation is purposeful, and the tested are the central purpose of the universe.

Imam Maturidi reports a common rational argument: all sages agree that creation is for purposes. Then he presents the first interpretation: the world was created for the tested, through whom divine wisdom, sovereignty, and other attributes are manifested. This passage sets up a key debate in Islamic theology about the purpose of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational premise
Read the opening lines where the group argues that creation must be for purposes, citing the consensus of sages and the absurdity of an agent who does not know the consequences of his action.
Notice that this is presented as a rational necessity.
2
Identify the specific purpose
After stating the general claim, the passage specifies that creation is for the tested (الممتحن). Identify the attributes that are manifested through them and the role of other creatures.
The tested are the intended ones; others are subjected to them.
3
Reflect on God's transcendence
Note the statement that God is elevated above praise and blame. Reflect on how this view maintains God's independence while ascribing purpose to creation.
This guards against anthropopathism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : خلق العالم لعليل يكون منها وفيها وما بعدها ، وذلك هو المعقول من جميع الحكماء أنه لمقاصد يعقب الصنيع ، وكذا كل فاعل لا يعلم عواقب فعله أنه لماذا يفعله فهو غير حكيم . ثم اختلف في المعني الذي له خلق : من يقول : خلق جل العالم للممتحن فيه ؛ إذ ظهور الحكمة فيهم فيهم يظهر العلو والسلطان والجلال والرفعة ، وبهم تظهر الحكمة والسفه ، المقصودون من الخلق ، وغيرهم من الخلائق خلقوا لهم ، لمنافع لهم ، وللامتحان بها ، وللدلالة ، وسخر وا لهم ، والممتحنون خلقوا للعبادة ، أو لأنفسهم ليسعوا العواقب يحمدون عليها ويذمون ، إليهم يقع ذلك ، وضرورة جل خالقهم عن الوجهين ؛ الذين خلقوا محتاجين ، ركب فيهم ما عرفوا به حوائجهم وما يقومون في إذ هم قضائها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وقال قوم : لم يخلق الكل العيلة ؛ لأنه ليس وراء الكل شيء يكون ذلك وذلك كما لم يخلق الكل في مكان ؛ لأن المكان علة ، وخلق البعض العلمة في الكل ، وخلق بعضا لبعض ، وعلى هذا الأمر التوالد ثم الجزاء والمحنة وبالله التوفيق
And a group said: The world was created for causes that are from it and in it and after it, and that is the rational view from all the sages that it is for purposes that follow the making. Likewise every agent who does not know the consequences of his action — that is, why he does it — is not wise. Then they differed concerning the meaning for which He created. Some say: He created the world — may He be exalted — for the tested in it, because the manifestation of wisdom in them shows supremacy, sovereignty, majesty and loftiness, and through them wisdom and foolishness appear. They are the intended ones from creation, and others of the creatures were created for them, for benefits for them, for testing by them, for indication, and they were subjected to them. And the tested were created for worship, or for themselves to pursue the consequences, praised or blamed upon them; that falls to them, and necessity elevates their Creator above both aspects. Those who were created needy, He instilled in them that by which they know their needs and what they rely on in fulfilling them. And there is no power except with God. And a group said: He did not create the whole for a cause, because there is nothing beyond the whole such that this and that would be. Just as He did not create the whole in a place, because place is a cause. And He created some as a cause within the whole, and created some for others, and upon this is the matter of generation, then requital and trial. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage presents two related positions on the purpose of creation. The first group asserts that creation is for purposes (عليل) — a view common to all sages. They then disagree on the specific purpose. Some say the world was created for the sake of the tested (الممتحن), i.e., humans, as through them God's wisdom, sovereignty, and other attributes appear. Other creatures were created for their benefit and as means of testing. The tested themselves were created for worship, or for themselves to seek consequences. The author notes that God is beyond both aspects (of praise and blame). The second group (وقال قوم : لم يخلق الكل العيلة) denies that the whole universe was created for a single cause, arguing that just as the whole is not in a place (because place would be a cause), so also the whole is not for a cause. Instead, parts are created as causes within the whole, and some parts for others, establishing a system of generation, requital, and trial.

Key Terms
عليل
مقاصد
الممتحن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God created the world because He needs something from creation.
Correction: The passage states that God is above both aspects (praise and blame) and that creation is for the tested, not for God's need.

Checkpoint
According to the first group, what is the primary purpose of creating the world?
A) For God's own benefit
B) For the tested
C) For no purpose
D) For angels

Key Takeaway
A major theological position holds that creation is purposeful and centers on the tested, manifesting divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire universe was created with you as the tested at its center, how does that change the weight you give to your choices today? Will you live this day as if your actions are the whole point of creation?
?
The passage says other creatures are subjected to you for benefits and testing. When you use the resources around you—food, technology, nature—do you do so as one who will be held accountable, or as one who simply consumes? What would it mean to treat every convenience as a test?
?
You were created either for worship or to pursue consequences that will be praised or blamed. If you examined your last hour, which description fits better? And what change can you make right now to ensure worship is your primary aim?

Parts as Causes: An Alternative View on Creation's Purpose
Maturidi presents the view that the whole is not caused, but parts cause parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second group's view that the whole universe was not created for a single cause, but rather parts are causes within the whole.

Core Idea
The whole is not caused, but parts within it exist as causes for other parts.

After presenting the first view, Imam Maturidi reports a contrasting position: some argued that the entire universe cannot have a single purpose, just as it is not located in a place. Instead, causation operates within the universe, with some parts created as causes for others, leading to a system of generation, requital, and trial.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial and reason
Read the opening sentence where the group denies that the whole was created for a cause, and note the reason: there is nothing beyond the whole.
The denial is based on the impossibility of an external cause for the totality.
2
Understand the analogy
The passage uses the analogy of the whole not being created in a place, because place would be a cause. Reflect on how this clarifies the denial.
Just as place is a cause, a purpose external to the whole would be a cause.
3
Identify the positive claim
The passage then states that He created some as a cause within the whole, and some for others. Identify this as the alternative model.
This is internal causation, not external.
4
Recognize the resulting system
The passage concludes with 'generation, requital, trial.' Consider how these concepts relate to internal causality.
This system explains the order without a single overarching purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : خلق العالم لعليل يكون منها وفيها وما بعدها ، وذلك هو المعقول من جميع الحكماء أنه لمقاصد يعقب الصنيع ، وكذا كل فاعل لا يعلم عواقب فعله أنه لماذا يفعله فهو غير حكيم . ثم اختلف في المعني الذي له خلق : من يقول : خلق جل العالم للممتحن فيه ؛ إذ ظهور الحكمة فيهم فيهم يظهر العلو والسلطان والجلال والرفعة ، وبهم تظهر الحكمة والسفه ، المقصودون من الخلق ، وغيرهم من الخلائق خلقوا لهم ، لمنافع لهم ، وللامتحان بها ، وللدلالة ، وسخر وا لهم ، والممتحنون خلقوا للعبادة ، أو لأنفسهم ليسعوا العواقب يحمدون عليها ويذمون ، إليهم يقع ذلك ، وضرورة جل خالقهم عن الوجهين ؛ الذين خلقوا محتاجين ، ركب فيهم ما عرفوا به حوائجهم وما يقومون في إذ هم قضائها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وقال قوم : لم يخلق الكل العيلة ؛ لأنه ليس وراء الكل شيء يكون ذلك وذلك كما لم يخلق الكل في مكان ؛ لأن المكان علة ، وخلق البعض العلمة في الكل ، وخلق بعضا لبعض ، وعلى هذا الأمر التوالد ثم الجزاء والمحنة وبالله التوفيق
And a group said: The world was created for causes that are from it and in it and after it, and that is the rational view from all the sages that it is for purposes that follow the making. Likewise every agent who does not know the consequences of his action — that is, why he does it — is not wise. Then they differed concerning the meaning for which He created. Some say: He created the world — may He be exalted — for the tested in it, because the manifestation of wisdom in them shows supremacy, sovereignty, majesty and loftiness, and through them wisdom and foolishness appear. They are the intended ones from creation, and others of the creatures were created for them, for benefits for them, for testing by them, for indication, and they were subjected to them. And the tested were created for worship, or for themselves to pursue the consequences, praised or blamed upon them; that falls to them, and necessity elevates their Creator above both aspects. Those who were created needy, He instilled in them that by which they know their needs and what they rely on in fulfilling them. And there is no power except with God. And a group said: He did not create the whole for a cause, because there is nothing beyond the whole such that this and that would be. Just as He did not create the whole in a place, because place is a cause. And He created some as a cause within the whole, and created some for others, and upon this is the matter of generation, then requital and trial. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage presents two related positions on the purpose of creation. The first group asserts that creation is for purposes (عليل) — a view common to all sages. They then disagree on the specific purpose. Some say the world was created for the sake of the tested (الممتحن), i.e., humans, as through them God's wisdom, sovereignty, and other attributes appear. Other creatures were created for their benefit and as means of testing. The tested themselves were created for worship, or for themselves to seek consequences. The author notes that God is beyond both aspects (of praise and blame). The second group (وقال قوم : لم يخلق الكل العيلة) denies that the whole universe was created for a single cause, arguing that just as the whole is not in a place (because place would be a cause), so also the whole is not for a cause. Instead, parts are created as causes within the whole, and some parts for others, establishing a system of generation, requital, and trial.

Key Terms
الكل
العيلة
علة
التوالد
الجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This group denies any purpose in creation.
Correction: They deny a single purpose for the whole, but affirm purposes within the whole through parts causing parts.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the second group use to support their view?
A) Creation is like a building with a blueprint.
B) The whole is not created in a place, just as it is not created for a cause.
C) Parts are independent and unconnected.
D) God is beyond all causality.

Key Takeaway
An alternative theological view holds that the whole universe is not created for a single cause, but internal causality operates through parts created as causes for other parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe is not created for one overarching purpose, but rather you are a cause for others, what specific way have you been a cause for someone’s good today? If you haven’t, what stops you from being one?
?
The passage ends with 'And with God is success'—a reminder that even our causal actions depend on God. In what area of your life are you relying on your own power rather than seeking God’s help? Will you turn to Him now?
?
You will face requital and trial. Are you living today as if every action is a cause that will return to you? What is one habit you must change knowing that it will be brought to account?
