Maturidi's Refutation of Divine Constraint
Maturidi refutes a claim restricting God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that God cannot do other than what He did, by exposing its implications of injustice, lack of free choice, and neediness.

Core Idea
Denying God the ability to do otherwise leads to absurd implications of need and imperfection.

In this dense passage, Imam al-Maturidi (referred to as al-faqih) dismantles a theological claim that restricts God's power to only what He actually did. He argues that such a view would force us to attribute injustice, compulsion, and even need to God. Let's trace his logical steps as he turns the claim into a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Notice the flow: Maturidi first summarizes the claim, then draws two major consequences. The language is technical.
Pay attention to the terms 'الجور' (injustice) and 'الحاجة' (need).
2
Identify the two major implications
The passage presents two sets of consequences if God could only do what He did. First, it leads to no preference among actions, the attribute of injustice, and lack of free choice. Second, it implies God benefits from His action and is in need of it for praise.
These are the core of Maturidi's refutation.
3
Reflect on why these implications are problematic
Ask yourself: why would these consequences be unacceptable for God? Maturidi assumes that God must be free, just, and self-sufficient. The claim forces the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله: وجملة هذا الفصل أنه على قولهم إذ لم يكن له غير الذي فعل لم يكن شيء من فعله مفضلا؛ إذ هو أبقى بكل فعله صفة الجور، ولا كان لما يفعله مختارا له، إذ لو كان منه غير ذلك كان مفسدا، وكان عن جعل الإصلاح في غيره عاجزا، وذلك هو النهاية من صفة الدم، والله الموفق. ولو كان لا يجوز له غير الذي فعل لكان بفعله منتفعا، ويفسير هو إليه محتاجا ليجمد به ويثني عليه، إذ من لا يستحق حمدا ولا مدحا إلا بغيره فهو إليه محتاج فى أن يحق له الثناء، وبه منتفع، إذ من قولهم: إن فعله غيره، ولم يكن له تركه، ولا غير الذي فعله، إذ غيره يحط رتبته ويسفهه، فثبت بما فعل النفع، وهو غيره عندهم، وهذه صفة الحاجة في عرف العقول، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The summary of this chapter is that according to their statement, since there is no other [action] for Him aside from what He did, no action of His would be preferable; for He would have perpetuated the attribute of injustice in all His actions, and He would not be choosing what He does, for if something other than that were from Him, it would be corrupting, and He would be incapable of establishing reform in others, and that is the utmost of the attribute of [blood?], and Allah is the Grantor of success. And if it were not permissible for Him to do other than what He did, He would be benefiting by His action, and it would be interpreted that He is in need of it to gain through it and to be praised for it, for whoever deserves praise and commendation only through others is in need of that other for praise to be due to him, and he benefits from it, for from their statement: His action is other than Him, and He could not abandon it, nor [do] other than what He did, for other than it would lower His rank and make Him foolish. So it is established that He benefits by what He did, and it is other than Him according to them, and this is the description of need according to the intellects' custom. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation by Maturidi (referred to as al-faqih) of the claim that God cannot do other than what He did. Maturidi argues that if God had no alternative actions, then none of His actions would be preferable or superior; He would be stuck with an attribute of injustice (الجور) in all His actions, and He would not be truly choosing (مختار) them—since any other action would be corrupting, implying He is incapable of reform. This leads to attributing imperfection to God. Furthermore, if God could not do other than what He did, then He would be benefiting by His own action and would be in need of that benefit—in need of something else to entitle Him to praise. This is a description of need (الحاجة). Maturidi points out that the claim that God's action is other than God, yet He cannot abandon it or do otherwise, implies that the action benefits Him and is other than Him, which is a sign of need according to sound reason. Thus, the original claim leads to absurdities.

Key Terms
الفقيه
الجور
مختار
الحاجة
الخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God cannot do wrong, then He cannot do otherwise than what He did.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this would make God's actions unjust, unfree, and needy, which are incompatible with divine perfection.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what does the claim that God cannot do other than what He did imply about God?
A) It implies God is all-powerful.
B) It implies God is in need and lacks freedom.
C) It implies God is just.
D) It implies God is wise.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the claim that God cannot do otherwise by showing it leads to the absurd consequences of injustice, lack of choice, and neediness in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God as if He must answer your prayers in a certain way, forgetting that He is absolutely free and not constrained by your expectations? What would change in your dua if you fully believed He can do otherwise but chooses what is best?
?
When you worship, do you sometimes feel that God needs your praise, or that your worship adds to His glory? Maturidi says even the possibility of needing something contradicts God. Examine your heart: do you worship out of gratitude or out of a sense that God requires it?
?
If you believed that God had to create the world and had no choice, would your trust in His wisdom and justice be shaken? Or would you accept a God whose actions are necessary? This lesson demands you reflect: does your faith rest on a God who is free or on a God who is compelled?

Rational Necessity of Divine Commands and Prohibitions
Maturidi establishes the rational basis for divine commands and prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that human nature and reason necessitate that God commands knowledge and prohibits ignorance, and that gratitude is rationally required.

Core Idea
Human nature demands divine obligation; otherwise, lying and evil would be permitted.

Imam al-Maturidi opens this section by grounding the reality of divine commands and prohibitions in the very nature of human creation. He argues that since humans are created with the capacity for discipline and rational inference, it is not permissible for God to leave them without obligation. Allowing ignorance would open the door to every blameworthy act. Gratitude for God's favors is also a rational necessity. This passage establishes the rational foundation for religious law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human nature and discipline
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting that God created humans with a nature that can be disciplined and with the ability to discern benefit and harm.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مذلّلا بالتأديب' (humbled by discipline).
2
Understand the argument for necessity of obligation
Notice that because humans can infer the unseen from the witnessed, it is not permissible for God to leave them without knowledge obligation. Allowing ignorance would sanction lying and all blameworthy actions.
3
Recognize gratitude as rationally necessary
The passage adds that gratitude for God's favors is a rational requirement. This further supports the necessity of divine commands.
The word 'فاستاداه' indicates that God requires gratitude from humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالأمر والنهي والترغيب والترهيب ، مع ما يقدم منه الكافي من ذلك الذي ذكره الحسين : أن الله خلق خلقا مذلّلا بالتأديب ، عارفا بالنفع والضر ، خلقه ، مستدلا بالذي شهد من الحجة على الذي غاب ، لم يجز أن لا يفرض المعرفة ولا يحضر عليه الجهل ، فيكون فيه إباحة الكذب وكل ذميم . مع ما كان لمن نعم عليه في الخلقة ، وشكر النعمة لازم فى العقل ، فاستاداه . ثم الوعد والوعيد فى الترغيب بتعظيمه ، والترحيب عن الاستخفاف به ، ثم إذ كرمه بفنون كل الكرم فعلى ذلك ثوابه لا أمد له ، وإذ كان الكفر غاية في العصيان فكذلك عقوبته . وأيضا أن الايمان تصديق بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاذ ، والكفر تكذيب بما لا نهاية له ولا نفاذ ، فعلى ذلك جزاؤهما ، ولهذا يجوز العفو عما دون الكفر لأنه ليس يجحد لما لا نهاية له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the statement about command and prohibition, and encouragement and intimidation, along with what suffices from what al-Husayn mentioned: that God created a creation humbled by discipline, knowing benefit and harm, His creation, inferring from the witnessed evidence to the unseen; it is not permissible that knowledge not be obligated and ignorance be brought upon him, for that would permit lying and every blameworthy thing. Along with what is for one upon whom He has bestowed favor in creation, and gratitude for the favor is necessary in reason, so He [sought?] it. Then promise and threat in encouragement by magnifying Him, and deterrence from belittling Him. Then when He honored him with all kinds of honor, accordingly his reward has no limit; and since disbelief is the utmost in disobedience, so too is its punishment. Also, faith is affirmation of that which has no end and no cessation, and disbelief is denial of that which has no end and no cessation; accordingly their recompense [is eternal], and for this reason forgiveness is permissible for what is less than disbelief, for it is not denial of that which has no end. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage begins by establishing that God created humans with a nature that can be disciplined and can discern benefit and harm, and that they are capable of inferring the unseen from the witnessed. Therefore, it is necessary that knowledge of God be obligated upon them, and ignorance should not be allowed, as that would sanction lying and all evil. Gratitude for God's favors is rationally necessary. Then the passage argues that promise and threat are used to encourage reverence and deter belittlement. Since God honored humans with every kind of honor, reward must be eternal; and since disbelief is the utmost disobedience, its punishment is also eternal. The reason is that faith affirms what is infinite, and disbelief denies what is infinite, so the recompense matches the object. Thus, forgiveness is possible for sins less than disbelief, because those do not involve denial of the infinite.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
الترغيب والترهيب
المعرفة
الجهل
شكر النعمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could have left humans without any guidance or obligation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that because humans have the capacity to know and infer, leaving them without obligation would permit evil and lying, which contradicts divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it necessary for God to obligate knowledge and forbid ignorance?
A) Because humans are inherently evil
B) Because humans can be disciplined and infer the unseen from the witnessed, allowing ignorance would permit lying and evil
C) Because ignorance is a sin in itself
D) Because God wants to test humans

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that divine commands and prohibitions are rationally necessitated by human nature and the need to prevent evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage says that ignorance would permit lying and every blameworthy act. In what areas of your life do you deliberately avoid knowing what God commands, and what excuses do you use? Will you commit to seeking that knowledge today, no matter how uncomfortable?
?
Gratitude for God's favor is rationally necessary. Look at the blessings you have—health, intellect, faith. Have you truly thanked God for them, or do you take them for granted? What specific act of gratitude will you perform today as a duty of your reason?
?
Maturidi argues that God cannot leave humans without the obligation of knowledge. If you know a truth from God but ignore it, you are effectively choosing ignorance. Is there a divine command you are disobeying that you need to submit to right now, before you face the consequence of permitting blameworthy acts in your life?

Eternity of Reward and Punishment Based on the Infinite Object of Faith
Maturidi explains the eternity of reward and punishment and the permissibility of forgiveness for lesser sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reward for faith and punishment for disbelief are eternal because they are directed toward an infinite object (God), and why forgiveness is possible for lesser sins.

Core Idea
Faith and disbelief engage the infinite, so their recompense must be eternal.

After discussing the role of promise and threat in encouraging reverence and deterring belittlement, Maturidi explains the eternity of reward and punishment. He offers two complementary arguments: first, that God's honor of humans demands eternal reward for obedience, and second, that the very nature of faith as affirmation of the infinite and disbelief as denial of the infinite necessitates eternal recompense. This also explains why forgiveness is possible for sins less than disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on promise and threat
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting that promise and threat serve to encourage reverence for God and deter belittlement.
The words 'الترغيب' and 'الترحيب' indicate encouragement and deterrence.
2
Understand the first argument: honor and disobedience
Notice that because God honored humans with every kind of honor, reward must have no limit. Conversely, disbelief as the utmost disobedience deserves eternal punishment.
3
Grasp the second argument: infinite object
Faith affirms the infinite, disbelief denies the infinite. Therefore the recompense must match the infinite nature of the object.
This is a deeper theological principle.
4
Apply the principle to forgiveness
Since sins less than disbelief do not involve denial of the infinite, forgiveness is permissible for them.
This shows God's mercy without compromising justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What would it take for you to take even your 'small' sins seriously, without losing hope in His mercy?
?

What would change if you consistently acted as if God truly has no limits—not in your theology, but in your daily trust and obedience?

Reward as Grace and the Doubling of Reward
Maturidi explains reward as divine grace and cites Quranic verse on doubling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi views all reward as divine grace (faḍl) while punishment is equal to the deed as required by wisdom, as evidenced by the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Reward is grace; punishment is justice.

Maturidi begins by establishing that all reward from God is ultimately a favor, since God has already given countless blessings that obligate gratitude. He then cites the Quranic verse that multiplies good deeds tenfold but restricts punishment to an equal measure, showing that reward is grace while punishment is justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's argument about reward being grace.
Notice the contrast between reward and punishment.
2
Identify the distinction between grace and justice
Maturidi states that reward can be considered entirely as grace because of prior favors. He then cites the verse to show that punishment is equal (justice) while reward is multiplied (grace).
3
Understand the Quranic proof
The verse (6:160) is used as evidence: good deeds get tenfold, evil deeds only equal. This asymmetry is based on wisdom for justice and grace for reward.
This is not contradictory to God's mercy; it reflects His wisdom.
4
Reflect on the principle
Maturidi concludes that this principle (reward as grace, punishment as justice) is what intellect can grasp regarding command and prohibition.
This sets the stage for discussing the limits of intellect in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أمكن أن نجعل الثواب كله فضلا إذ قد سبق من الله من النعم ما استحق الشكر عليه بعامة ما احتمل الوسع ، فيكون الثواب فصلا من الله . ثم المضاعفة في ذلك كقوله تعالى : مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِها وَمَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ، فذكر في السيئة توجبه الحكمة من الجزاء وضاعف فى الثواب على ما تحتمله الأفضال ، إذ ذلك أصله، ولا قوة إلا بالله . فهذا فيما احتمله عقولنا مما يلزم الأمر والنهي، ما كان فيما جاء بهما الرسل عن الله دليل كاف يلزم القبول بعظم الحكمة فهما لو قصرت عقولنا عن ما فى العقل إذا ترك استعماله كسائر الجوارح لم يعتدل الوقوف على ذلك ، مع تعطيلها عن المنافع التي هي سببها ، فمثله العقل ، مع ما كان الذي ذكرت في سائر الجوارح هو حق الفعل أيضا. واشارته ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And it is possible that we consider all reward as grace (faḍl), since there have preceded from God such favors that require gratitude for them in general as much as capacity allows, so reward becomes a grace from God. Then the multiplication in that is like His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed shall have ten the like of it, and whoever comes with an evil deed shall not be recompensed except with the like of it' (Quran 6:160). So He mentioned for the evil deed what wisdom necessitates in recompense, and multiplied reward according to what graces allow, for that is its origin. And there is no power except with God. This is what our intellects can grasp of what is necessitated by command and prohibition. As for what the messengers brought from God, sufficient evidence is necessary for acceptance due to the greatness of wisdom; for if our intellects fall short of [understanding] that, and if the intellect is left unused like other limbs, it would not be properly grounded in that, along with depriving it of the benefits for which it is the cause. So the intellect is similar to the other limbs in that what I mentioned is also the right action for it and its indication. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage begins by considering that all reward can be viewed as divine grace (faḍl), because God has already given favors that obligate gratitude. Thus reward is an additional grace. Then Maturidi cites the Quranic verse (6:160) to show that punishment is only equal to the sin (as wisdom requires), while reward is multiplied tenfold (as grace allows). This is the principle. He then concludes that this is what our intellects can grasp regarding what is necessitated by command and prohibition. However, regarding matters that the messengers brought from God, there is sufficient evidence (dalīl kāf) that demands acceptance due to the greatness of wisdom. If our intellects fall short of understanding those matters, and if we leave the intellect unused (like other limbs), it would not be properly grounded in them, and would be deprived of the benefits for which it exists. So the intellect must be used like other limbs; its proper function is to seek understanding through revelation. The passage ends with 'wa ishāratihi' (and its indication).

Key Terms
الثواب فضلا
المضاعفة
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward is purely earned and not a gift; that punishment is arbitrary.
Correction: Maturidi shows that reward is an act of grace beyond what is owed, while punishment is exact justice based on wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is reward multiplied but punishment is not?
A) Because God is more merciful than just
B) Because wisdom requires equal punishment, but grace allows multiplication of reward
C) Because sins are less serious
D) Because the verse says so, but reason alone cannot know

Key Takeaway
Reward is a divine grace beyond mere justice, while punishment is exactly what wisdom necessitates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you subconsciously feel that Allah owes you a reward? How does the lesson that reward is pure grace—not a right—challenge that assumption? What practical step can you take today to cultivate gratitude instead of entitlement?
?
Consider a sin you have committed that you think is particularly heavy. How does knowing that Allah's justice means He will only punish you exactly as wisdom requires—no more, no less—affect your hope in His mercy? Are you willing to trust that His measure is perfectly fair, even if it means accepting the full weight of the punishment?
?
The Quran promises ten times the reward for a single good deed, but only an equal punishment for a sin. Does this asymmetry make you more motivated to increase your good deeds or more complacent? What specific change in your daily habits would reflect a genuine belief that even a small good deed can be multiplied beyond measure?

The Role of Intellect and Revelation
Maturidi discusses the scope of intellect and the necessity of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that while intellect can grasp certain moral principles, matters brought by messengers require acceptance through revelation, and intellect must be actively used like other faculties.

Core Idea
Intellect must be used to accept revelation; both are necessary.

After discussing reward and punishment, Maturidi turns to the distinction between what intellect can grasp and what requires prophetic revelation. He compares the intellect to a limb that must be exercised, otherwise it fails to achieve its purpose. This passage underscores the complementary roles of reason and revelation in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand Maturidi's discussion on the scope of intellect and the necessity of revelation.
Note the analogy of intellect to other limbs.
2
Identify the two domains
Maturidi distinguishes between what intellect can grasp (like the justice of punishment) and what messengers bring (detailed commands and wisdom) that requires acceptance through revelation.
3
Understand the analogy of the intellect as a limb
If intellect is not used, like other limbs, it cannot function properly. It must be employed to gain benefits, especially to understand revelation.
This emphasizes the active role of reason in faith.
4
Reflect on the necessity of revelation
Even if intellect falls short in some matters, revelation provides sufficient evidence (dalīl kāf) that demands acceptance due to the greatness of wisdom.
Intellect is not discarded but guided by revelation.

Source Text

6 min
Allah is a Thing: Affirmation without Limitation
Maturidi defines 'thing' as pure affirmation and links it to knowledge of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi defines 'thing' (shay') as pure affirmation and how this concept leads to knowledge of Allah without attributing limitations.

Core Idea
'Thing' is pure affirmation of existence, not a description of physicality.

Imam Maturidi begins by addressing a fundamental question: what does it mean to say that Allah is a 'thing'? This term often carries physical connotations in everyday language, but in theological discourse it requires precise definition. Maturidi explains that 'thing' is nothing more than affirmation of being, and this affirmation is the path to recognizing the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the repetition of 'affirmation' and 'negation'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of 'thing'
Maturidi defines 'thing' as pure affirmation (ithbat) and affirmation of identity (huwiyya). Contrast this with 'no thing' which is negation. Consider why this distinction is crucial for theology.
The term 'thing' here does not imply physical composition.
3
See how 'thingness' leads to knowledge of Allah
Maturidi argues that Allah does not negate His own thingness; rather, He negates general conditions while retaining thingness. This thingness is what guides the knower to the Lord. Reflect on how affirmation of being, without added attributes, is sufficient for knowledge of Allah.
This avoids anthropomorphism while affirming real existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة معنى القول بأن الله شيء. ثم الشيء إثبات لا غير، وإثبات عن الهوية؛ إذ لا شيء هو النفي، فيعلم بأن الله سبحانه شيء، لا نفى عن نفسه أنه شيء، إذ ينفى عامة أحوال نفسه، ويعلمها من غير أن ينفى شيئيتها، فصار يعرف ربه لا الذي يعرف أنه شيء، لذلك لم تمنع معرفته بشيئية نفسه المعرفة أنه إذ لا شيئية دلته على الرب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأما الجسم فهو اسم لكل محدود، والشيء إثبات لا غير، وفي وجود العالم على ما عليه دليل الإثبات؛ لذلك قيل بالشيء، وفيه إذ حيث الشيئية بل من حيث الحد دليل نفى الحد عن الله جل ثناؤه. إلا أن يراد بالحد الوحدانية والربوبية، فهو كذلك، وحرف الحد ساقط لأنه يغلب في الدلالة على نهاية الشيء من طريق العرض ونحو ذلك مما يتعالى عن ذلك، وذلك معنى الجسم في الشاهد. وفيه أيضا إيجاب الجهات المحتمل كل جهة أن يكون أطول منها وأعرض وأقصر، فلذلك بطل القول بذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Question: The meaning of the statement that God is a thing. Then 'thing' is affirmation only, and affirmation of identity; for 'no thing' is negation. So it is known that God, exalted, is a thing. He does not negate from Himself that He is a thing, for He negates [only] the general conditions of Himself and knows them without negating their being-things. Thus one knows his Lord not [merely] as one who knows that He is a thing; therefore, his knowledge that he is a thing did not prevent [true] knowledge, for it is through being-a-thing that He led him to the Lord. And no power except through God. As for the body, it is a name for everything limited. 'Thing' is affirmation only, and in the existence of the world as it is there is evidence of affirmation; therefore it is said by 'thing'. And in it, with respect to being-a-thing, but from the aspect of limit, there is evidence for negating limit from God, may He be glorified. Unless by 'limit' is meant oneness and lordship, then it is so; but the word 'limit' is dropped because it predominantly indicates the end of a thing in terms of dimension and the like, which He is exalted above. That is the meaning of body in the witnessed world. And in it also is the affirmation of directions, each direction possibly being longer and wider and shorter [than the other]; therefore the statement [that God is a body] is invalid. And no power except through God.
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer definition and refutation. Maturidi begins by defining the term 'thing' (shay') as pure affirmation (ithbat) and affirmation of identity (huwiyya). He explains that calling something 'no thing' is negation, so to say 'Allah is a thing' is to affirm His existence and reality. Allah does not negate His own being-a-thing; rather, He negates other attributes from Himself without negating thingness itself. This thingness is what leads the knower to recognize the Lord. Maturidi then turns to the term 'body' (jism). He defines body as 'every limited thing' (kullu mahdud). Since 'thing' is pure affirmation, it does not imply limitation, whereas 'body' implies limit. The existence of the world provides evidence for affirmation, so affirming Allah as a thing is appropriate. However, from the aspect of limit, one can negate limit from Allah. If 'limit' is interpreted as oneness and lordship, then it is acceptable, but the word 'limit' is avoided because it primarily signifies spatial or dimensional ends, which are not fitting for Allah. Furthermore, calling Allah a body would necessitate direction (jiha), and each direction could be longer, wider, or shorter, implying comparison and composition, which is impossible for Allah. Therefore, the statement that Allah is a body is invalid. The passage concludes with a reliance on God's power.

Key Terms
الشيء (shay')
الإثبات (ithbat)
النفي (nafy)
الهوية (huwiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Allah a 'thing' implies He is a physical object.
Correction: Maturidi explains that 'thing' is pure affirmation of existence, not a description of physical attributes. It is the most basic category of existence without limitations.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the term 'thing' (shay') signify when applied to Allah?
A) A physical object
B) Pure affirmation of existence
C) A limited entity
D) Negation of attributes

Key Takeaway
Calling Allah a 'thing' affirms His existence without attributing any physical or limiting characteristics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you feel the weight of that affirmation in your heart, or is it just a mental concept? How would your life change if you truly lived as if His existence is the only reality?
?
The passage says that thingness leads to knowing the Lord. Are you content with merely knowing that God is, or are you actively seeking to know Him through that affirmation? What concrete steps can you take today to move from intellectual knowledge to heart knowledge?
?
Maturidi says that Allah does not negate His own thingness. Do you ever find yourself unconsciously limiting Allah by imagining Him in human terms? How can you train yourself to affirm His reality without reducing Him to your mental images?

Why Allah is Not a Body: Refuting Limit and Direction
Maturidi refutes describing Allah as a body, citing limit and direction as reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments against describing Allah as a body, focusing on the implications of limit and direction.

Core Idea
Body implies limitation and direction, both impossible for Allah.

After establishing that Allah is a 'thing' in the sense of pure affirmation, Maturidi turns to refute the notion that Allah could be described as a body (jism). The term 'body' in everyday language refers to something limited and extended. Maturidi argues that applying this term to Allah would imply limit, direction, and comparability, all of which are incompatible with divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on body
Read the second part of the Arabic text and its translation. Note how Maturidi defines 'body' as 'everything limited' (kullu mahdud) and contrasts it with 'thing' as affirmation.
2
Understand the argument from limit
Maturidi argues that since 'thing' is pure affirmation, it does not imply limit. But 'body' necessarily implies limit. The existence of the world provides evidence for affirmation, so it is appropriate to call Allah a 'thing' but not a 'body' because limit must be negated from Allah. Consider the nuance about 'limit' possibly meaning oneness and lordship.
Maturidi acknowledges that if 'limit' is reinterpreted, it could be acceptable, but the word is avoided due to its primary meaning of spatial end.
3
Understand the argument from direction
Maturidi adds that calling Allah a body would necessitate direction (jiha), and each direction could be longer, wider, or shorter, implying comparison and composition. This is impossible for Allah, so the statement is invalid.
This reinforces the impossibility of Allah having physical attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة معنى القول بأن الله شيء. ثم الشيء إثبات لا غير، وإثبات عن الهوية؛ إذ لا شيء هو النفي، فيعلم بأن الله سبحانه شيء، لا نفى عن نفسه أنه شيء، إذ ينفى عامة أحوال نفسه، ويعلمها من غير أن ينفى شيئيتها، فصار يعرف ربه لا الذي يعرف أنه شيء، لذلك لم تمنع معرفته بشيئية نفسه المعرفة أنه إذ لا شيئية دلته على الرب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأما الجسم فهو اسم لكل محدود، والشيء إثبات لا غير، وفي وجود العالم على ما عليه دليل الإثبات؛ لذلك قيل بالشيء، وفيه إذ حيث الشيئية بل من حيث الحد دليل نفى الحد عن الله جل ثناؤه. إلا أن يراد بالحد الوحدانية والربوبية، فهو كذلك، وحرف الحد ساقط لأنه يغلب في الدلالة على نهاية الشيء من طريق العرض ونحو ذلك مما يتعالى عن ذلك، وذلك معنى الجسم في الشاهد. وفيه أيضا إيجاب الجهات المحتمل كل جهة أن يكون أطول منها وأعرض وأقصر، فلذلك بطل القول بذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Question: The meaning of the statement that God is a thing. Then 'thing' is affirmation only, and affirmation of identity; for 'no thing' is negation. So it is known that God, exalted, is a thing. He does not negate from Himself that He is a thing, for He negates [only] the general conditions of Himself and knows them without negating their being-things. 'Thing' is affirmation only, and in the existence of the world as it is there is evidence of affirmation; therefore it is said by 'thing'. And in it, with respect to being-a-thing, but from the aspect of limit, there is evidence for negating limit from God, may He be glorified. Commentary

He explains that calling something 'no thing' is negation, so to say 'Allah is a thing' is to affirm His existence and reality. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Describing Allah as a body is acceptable if understood metaphorically.
Correction: Maturidi argues that even metaphorically, 'body' implies limit and direction, which are incompatible with Allah's transcendence. The term should not be used at all.

Why does Maturidi reject describing Allah as a body?
A) Because body implies limitation
B) Because body implies direction
C) Both A and B
D) Because body is a synonym for thing

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your mind, do you unconsciously assign Him a direction (like up or down) or a form? This lesson shows that even the thought of direction implies limitation. Will you now actively guard your thoughts against such mental images, and affirm absolute transcendence in your heart?
?
Maturidi says that calling Allah a 'body' would make Him comparable to creation, implying dimensions longer or shorter. Have you ever described Allah in human-like terms to make Him more 'relatable' in your duas? How will you now express closeness to Allah without reducing His majesty?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'thing' (affirmation) and 'body' (limitation). Are you comfortable with affirming that Allah exists without being able to picture or locate Him? In your worship, do you truly feel His presence without spatial constraints? What change in your awareness does this demand?

Maturidi on Istiwa: Highness, Not Place
Maturidi interprets 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa' as highness, not spatial location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that the Qur'anic phrase 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa' refers to God's essential highness and majesty, not spatial location.

Core Idea
God's 'istiwa' over the Throne means His majestic highness, not sitting in a place.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key theological question: what does it mean when the Qur'an says the Most Merciful 'istawa' over the Throne? Does it imply God sits somewhere? In this passage, he argues that such an interpretation is impossible because God existed before creation and place. Instead, 'istiwa' expresses God's essential highness and majesty. Let's read his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of Maturidi's argument.
The passage begins with a foundational premise about God's existence before place.
2
Identify the key argument
Look for why Maturidi rejects a spatial interpretation. Notice how he uses verses about God's nearness as analogies.
He argues that just as 'nearness' does not mean physical closeness, 'istiwa' does not mean sitting.
3
Analyze the conclusion
Observe how Maturidi concludes that 'istiwa' must mean highness and majesty (al-'uluww wa al-jalal), which are essential attributes of God, not acquired through place.
The phrase 'bikhatimihi' (by His seal) is unclear but likely refers to God's own essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ قد ثبت أن كان ولا مكان، وليس في الإضافة إلى أنه على العرش استوى تثبيت مكان، كما لم يكن في قوله: وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ الْوَرِيدِ، وقوله: مَا يَكُونُ مِنْ نَجْوَى ثَلَاثَةٍ إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ، وقوله: وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْكُمْ... ذلك على أن القول بالمكان ليس من نوع التعظيم والتبجيل، بل الأمكنة إنما شرفت به وتفاوتت أقدارها بتفضيله مكانا على مكان يجعله مخصوصا لأخيار خلقه أو لما جعل لعبادته وتعظيمه فيه. فأما أن يكون أحد تعلو رتبته بالمكان من ملوك الأرض أو الأخيار فليس به، فكيف بالملك الجبار الذي ما ارتفع قدر مكان ولاجل خطره إلا به، وإذا كان كذلك بطل أن يكون في الإضافة تعظيمه، ثم يكون فما بعد ذلك للحاجة، وهو يتعالى عنها، ولذلك لم يجب بقوله: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى معنى الكون في المكان؛ إذ ذلك الحرف يعبر به عن العلو والجلال، ومحال مثله له بخاتمه، فثبت أن ذلك من الذي يستحقه بذاته من العلو والرفعة، وما هو بذاته عليه، فهو كان كذلك ولا خلق، لم يجز الوصف له بالخلق، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
For it has been established that He was while there was no place, and in the attribution that He istawa (established/ascended) over the Throne there is no establishing of a place, just as it was not in His saying: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein', and His saying: 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them', and His saying: 'And We are nearer to him than you are'... That is because speech about place is not a type of glorification and veneration; rather, places are only honored by Him, and their values differ by His preferring one place over another, making it specific for the best of His creation or for what He has made for His worship and veneration therein. As for one whose rank is elevated by place among the kings of the earth or the righteous, it is not by it; so how about the King, the Compeller, whose power of place and glory are only by Him? And if that is so, it becomes invalid that there is glorification in the attribution. Then whatever is after that is for need, and He is exalted above it. Therefore, it is not necessitated by His saying: 'The Most Merciful over the Throne istawa' the meaning of being in a place; because that word expresses highness and majesty, and it is impossible for the like of that to be from His seal. So it is established that that is from what He deserves by His essence of highness and elevation, and what He is by His essence upon, He was thus without creation, so it is not permissible to describe Him with coming into being, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
Maturidi asserts that God existed before place, so interpreting 'istiwa' over the Throne as spatial location is invalid. He draws analogy with Qur'anic verses about nearness (qurb) that do not imply physical proximity. Place is not inherently glorious; rather, it is God who gives honor to places. Therefore, attributing 'istiwa' to God must mean highness and majesty (al-'uluww wa al-jalal), which are essential attributes of God, not indicating need or change. The phrase 'bikhatimihi' (by His seal) is unclear but likely means God's own mark or essence.

Key Terms
الاستواء
العرش
العلو
الجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'istiwa' implies God sitting on the Throne like a physical being.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this by showing that 'istiwa' denotes essential highness and majesty, not spatial location.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue that 'istiwa' over the Throne means?
A) God sits on the Throne
B) God controls the Throne
C) Highness and majesty
D) God is everywhere

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that 'istiwa' over the Throne refers to God's inherent highness and majesty, not a physical location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa' in your prayers, do you ever catch yourself imagining a throne or a direction for God? How can you train your mind to focus instead on His infinite majesty, which has no location? Make a specific du'a this week asking Allah to purify your conception of Him.
?
Maturidi says that places are honored by God, not that God is honored by place. Do you sometimes feel more spiritual in a masjid or a particular spot, as if God is 'more there'? How can you carry the same reverence outside, knowing His nearness is not bound to any place? Write a concrete action you will take to feel His closeness in a non-sacred space.
?
God's attribute of highness is eternal—He was high before creation. Does that thought humble you when you seek status or recognition from others? How does this truth shape your ambitions and your relationship with pride? Commit to one way you will redirect your desire for elevation to seeking nearness to the Most High.

Asking About the Name of Allah
Maturidi presents the first interpretation of 'what is He?' as asking about the name, with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'what is He?' can be a request for the name of Allah, as shown in Quranic examples.

Core Idea
The question 'what is He?' may simply ask for His name, not His essence.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by clarifying that the question 'what is He?' (ما هو) regarding Allah is not always about definition or essence. Sometimes it is a metonymy for asking about His name. The Quran provides examples where such a question is answered by stating the name or description of the entity. Let's examine the passage and the evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the examples from the Quran: Pharaoh's question and the staff of Moses.
2
Identify the first meaning
Maturidi states that 'what is He?' may be a metonymy for the name of Allah. Understand that in this context, the answer is the name itself.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the two Quranic examples: Pharaoh asked 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and was answered 'Lord of the heavens and the earth.' Similarly, Allah asked Moses 'What is that in your right hand?' and he answered 'It is my staff.' Both answers give the name or identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الرجل : قد يكنى به عن اسمه كقول فرعون : وما رب العالمين ؟ قال : رَبَّ السَّمَاوَاتِ والأَرْضِ ، وقول الله لموسى : " وَمَا تِلْكَ بِيَمِينِكَ يَا مُوسى ، قَالَ هِيَ عَصَايَ ، فجواب الأول أن يُقال : المثال . وقد يكون ما هو ؟ ما صفته ؟ ، فجوابه سميع بصير وما هو ؟ أي مما يعرف له مائية فى الخلق ، فهو يتعالى عن [؟] وما هو ؟ يحتمل ما فعله ، فجوابه خلق الخلق ، ووضع كل شيء موضعه ، وذلك حكمته وقد يحتمل ما هو ؟ أى ممن هو ؟ فهو يتعالى من أن يكون من شيء ، بل هو مكوّن الأشياء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the man's saying: 'what is He?' may be metonymy for His name, like the saying of Pharaoh: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' He said: 'Lord of the heavens and the earth', and Allah's saying to Moses: 'And what is that in your right hand, O Moses?' He said: 'It is my staff.' So the first answer is to say: the example. And it may be 'what is He?' meaning 'what is His attribute?' So its answer is 'Hearing, Seeing'. And 'what is He?' meaning 'from which [something] is known to have a quiddity in creation?' So He is exalted above [that]. And 'what is He?' may mean 'what did He do?' So its answer is 'He created the creation and placed everything in its place, and that is His wisdom'. And it may be 'what is He?' meaning 'from whom is He?' So He is exalted from being from something, but He is the Maker of things. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage discusses the various meanings of the question 'what is He?' (ما هو) when asked about Allah. The author (Imam al-Maturidi) states that this question can be asked in different senses. First, it may be a metonymy for asking about His name, citing examples from the Quran: Pharaoh's question 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and Allah's question to Moses 'What is that in your right hand?' These were answered by describing the entity (the Lord of the heavens and the earth, a staff). Second, it may be asking about His attributes, so the answer is that He is All-Hearing, All-Seeing. Third, it may be asking about a quiddity (māhiyya) known in creation, but Allah is exalted above having a quiddity. Fourth, it may be asking about His action, so the answer is that He created the creation and placed everything in its place—that is His wisdom. Fifth, it may be asking about His origin (from whom?), and He is exalted from being from something; rather He is the Maker of all things. The passage concludes with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
ما هو
اسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that asking 'what is He?' always seeks a definition in terms of genus and species.
Correction: Maturidi shows that it can simply be a metonymy for asking about the name, as demonstrated in the Quran.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, when someone asks 'what is He?' about Allah, what is one possible meaning?
A) Asking about His size
B) Asking about His name
C) Asking about His location
D) Asking about His number

Key Takeaway
The question 'what is He?' can be a request for Allah's name, answered by stating His names or descriptions as shown in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask yourself 'what is Allah?' do you instinctively try to imagine His essence? This lesson teaches that such a question can simply be answered by His names. Which of Allah's names do you truly live by, and which have you neglected to reflect upon? Commit to studying one name this week.
?
The Quranic dialogues show that even the simplest inquiry—'what is that?'—is met with an answer that identifies and clarifies. How often do you seek to know Allah through His revealed names and descriptions versus your own speculations? This week, when you read a name of Allah, pause and ask yourself: does this name define how I should live right now?

Asking About the Attributes of Allah
Maturidi presents the second interpretation: asking about Allah's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'what is He?' can be about Allah's attributes, and the answer is to mention His attributes like Hearing and Seeing.

Core Idea
When asked 'what is He?', you may be asking about His attributes, so answer with His attributes.

After explaining the first meaning related to the name, Imam al-Maturidi presents a second interpretation. The question 'what is He?' could be seeking knowledge of Allah's attributes. The appropriate response is to mention attributes such as All-Hearing and All-Seeing. This passage clarifies that the question is not about definition but about description through attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second interpretation. Notice that it directly states the meaning and answer.
2
Understand the meaning
Realize that 'what is His attribute?' is a valid interpretation of 'what is He?'
3
Reflect on the answer
The answer 'Hearing, Seeing' are attributes that do not imply a quiddity or limitation.

?

?

Asking About the Quiddity of Allah
Maturidi rejects the idea that 'what is He?' asks about a quiddity in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that asking 'what is He?' about Allah cannot be about a quiddity (māhiyya), because Allah is transcendent above having a quiddity like created things.

Core Idea
Allah has no quiddity; He is unlike any created thing.

Imam al-Maturidi now addresses an interpretation that must be rejected. The question 'what is He?' could be mistakenly taken as asking about a quiddity (مائية), i.e., a 'whatness' or essence that defines something by genus and species. But Allah is exalted from having such a quiddity, which belongs only to created things. This passage reinforces divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the key point: Allah is exalted above having a quiddity.
2
Understand quiddity
A quiddity (مائية) is the 'whatness' that makes a thing what it is, like a species definition. Created things have quiddities, but Allah does not.
3
Reflect on transcendence
Consider why Allah cannot have a quiddity: it would imply He is composed of genus and differentia, which limits Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الرجل : قد يكنى به عن اسمه كقول فرعون : وما رب العالمين ؟ قال : رَبَّ السَّمَاوَاتِ والأَرْضِ ، وقول الله لموسى : " وَمَا تِلْكَ بِيَمِينِكَ يَا مُوسى ، قَالَ هِيَ عَصَايَ ، فجواب الأول أن يُقال : المثال . وقد يكون ما هو ؟ ما صفته ؟ ، فجوابه سميع بصير وما هو ؟ أي مما يعرف له مائية فى الخلق ، فهو يتعالى عن [؟] وما هو ؟ يحتمل ما فعله ، فجوابه خلق الخلق ، ووضع كل شيء موضعه ، وذلك حكمته وقد يحتمل ما هو ؟ أى ممن هو ؟ فهو يتعالى من أن يكون من شيء ، بل هو مكوّن الأشياء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the man's saying: 'what is He?' may be metonymy for His name, like the saying of Pharaoh: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' He said: 'Lord of the heavens and the earth', and Allah's saying to Moses: 'And what is that in your right hand, O Moses?' He said: 'It is my staff.' So the first answer is to say: the example. And it may be 'what is He?' meaning 'what is His attribute?' So its answer is 'Hearing, Seeing'. And 'what is He?' meaning 'from which [something] is known to have a quiddity in creation?' So He is exalted above [that]. And 'what is He?' may mean 'what did He do?' So its answer is 'He created the creation and placed everything in its place, and that is His wisdom'. And it may be 'what is He?' meaning 'from whom is He?' So He is exalted from being from something, but He is the Maker of things. And there is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage discusses the various meanings of the question 'what is He?' (ما هو) when asked about Allah. The author (Imam al-Maturidi) states that this question can be asked in different senses. First, it may be a metonymy for asking about His name, citing examples from the Quran: Pharaoh's question 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and Allah's question to Moses 'What is that in your right hand?' These were answered by describing the entity (the Lord of the heavens and the earth, a staff). Second, it may be asking about His attributes, so the answer is that He is All-Hearing, All-Seeing. Third, it may be asking about a quiddity (māhiyya) known in creation, but Allah is exalted above having a quiddity. Fourth, it may be asking about His action, so the answer is that He created the creation and placed everything in its place—that is His wisdom. Fifth, it may be asking about His origin (from whom?), and He is exalted from being from something; rather He is the Maker of all things. The passage concludes with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Allah has a quiddity or essence that can be defined by genus and species.
Correction: Maturidi asserts that Allah is exalted above having a quiddity; He is unlike any created thing.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does it mean when 'what is He?' is interpreted as asking about a quiddity?
A) It is valid, and the answer is a definition
B) It is invalid because Allah is exalted above having a quiddity
C) It is only valid for prophets
D) It means asking about His actions

Key Takeaway
Allah is transcendent and has no quiddity; asking 'what is He?' in the sense of a quiddity is inappropriate for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever try to picture Him in your mind? This lesson says Allah has no quiddity. How does that challenge your instinct to imagine what He is like? What will you do when you catch yourself trying to define Him?
?
If Allah is beyond any quiddity, then your knowledge of Him is limited to what He reveals. Are you content with that, or do you secretly wish you could know more of His essence? How does this affect your reliance on revelation?
?
The source says quiddity is only for creation. Reflect on a time when you attempted to comprehend Allah's nature through your own reason. Did it lead to confusion or doubt? How can you train yourself to accept His transcendence without trying to 'wrap your mind' around Him?

Asking About the Actions of Allah
Maturidi explains that 'what is He?' can ask about Allah's action, answered by His creation and wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'what is He?' can be asking about Allah's actions, and the answer is to mention His creation and wisdom.

Core Idea
When asked about Allah's doing, answer with His creative act and wisdom.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a fourth interpretation: the question 'what is He?' may be inquiring about what Allah has done or does. In this case, the answer is to mention His act of creation and His placing everything in its proper place, which is a manifestation of His wisdom. This passage shows another legitimate way to respond to the question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth interpretation. Note that it connects action to wisdom.
2
Understand the meaning
The question 'what did He do?' is a valid interpretation of 'what is He?'.
3
Reflect on the answer
The answer includes creating the creation and placing everything in its place, which is His wisdom (حكمة).

Source Text

This passage discusses the various meanings of the question 'what is He?' (ما هو) when asked about Allah. The author (Imam al-Maturidi) states that this question can be asked in different senses. First, it may be a metonymy for asking about His name, citing examples from the Quran: Pharaoh's question 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and Allah's question to Moses 'What is that in your right hand?' These were answered by describing the entity (the Lord of the heavens and the earth, a staff). Second, it may be asking about His attributes, so the answer is that He is All-Hearing, All-Seeing. Third, it may be asking about a quiddity (māhiyya) known in creation, but Allah is exalted above having a quiddity. Fourth, it may be asking about His action, so the answer is that He created the creation and placed everything in its place—that is His wisdom. Fifth, it may be asking about His origin (from whom?), and He is exalted from being from something; rather He is the Maker of all things. The passage concludes with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms

فعل
حكمة

Checkpoint

A) He is All-Knowing
B) He created the creation and placed everything in its place
C) He is the First and Last
D) He has no partner

Key Takeaway
If the question is about Allah's action, answer with His creation and wise order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Reflect on a current situation where you struggle to see the wisdom in His placement of circumstances or people around you. What would it take for you to surrender to the fact that He has placed everything in its proper place?

Asking About the Origin of Allah
Maturidi rejects the question regarding origin, affirming Allah is the Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that asking 'what is He?' about Allah cannot be about His origin, because Allah is not from anything; He is the Maker of all things.

Core Idea
Allah has no origin; He alone is the Creator, not created.

Imam al-Maturidi concludes his analysis with a fifth interpretation: the question 'what is He?' might be taken as asking 'from whom is He?' i.e., about His origin or lineage. This is completely invalid for Allah, as He is transcendent from being derived from anything. Instead, He is the Maker (مكوّن) of all things. The passage ends with a common supplication: 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the strong negation: Allah is exalted from being from something.
2
Understand the meaning
The question 'from whom is He?' is inappropriate for Allah; He has no creator or source.
3
Reflect on transcendence
Allah is the Maker (مكوّن) of all things, meaning He brings things into existence without any material or antecedent cause.

Two Meanings of 'How Is He?' Regarding Allah
Maturidi distinguishes two meanings of 'how' about Allah and answers each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'how is He?' about Allah is invalid when seeking a likeness, and how it can be properly understood as asking about His attributes as described in revelation.

Core Idea
Asking 'how?' about Allah either seeks a likeness (impossible) or asks about attributes (known only from revelation).

When people ask 'how is Allah?', the question can be ambiguous. Maturidi clarifies that it either demands a likeness for Allah—which is impossible given His absolute uniqueness—or it inquires about His attributes. The proper answer is that Allah is described only by what He has revealed about Himself, and His attributes have no modality like creatures. Let's examine this passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the technical terms like كيفية and شبه.
2
Identify the two meanings
Maturidi presents two aspects of the question 'how?' (كيف). Find both and understand the difference: one seeks a likeness, the other asks about the attribute.
The second meaning could also be about 'how' of the attribute itself, not the essence.
3
Understand the resolution
See how Maturidi answers each: the first is impossible, the second also has no 'how' unless it is actually asking 'Is He described?' — then answer with His self-revelation (mercy, knowledge, power).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسؤال عن الكيفية يحتمل وجهين : أحدهما طلب المثال له أن يكون مثلا لشيء من الأشياء ، والله واحد يجل عن الأشباه ، ويحتمل كيف صفته ؟ فجوابه مثل الأول أن ليس لصفته كيف ، إذ هو طلب المثال ، وهو يتعالى عن الشبه بالذات والصفة ، إلا أن يريد [؟] يه : أيوصف هو ؟ قيل : بلى ، بما وصف به نفسه من الرحمة والعلم والقدرة .
And the question about 'how-ness' (al-kayfiyya) has two aspects: one is seeking a likeness (mithāl) for Him to be similar to something, but Allah is One, exalted above likenesses. And it may be 'how is His attribute?' So its answer is like the first, that there is no 'how' for His attribute, since that is seeking a likeness, and He is exalted above similarity in essence and attribute. Unless what is meant by it is: Is He described? It is said: Yes, with what He described Himself with: mercy, knowledge, and power.
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage addresses the question 'how is He?' (كيف هو). Maturidi explains it has two possible meanings. First, it may be asking for a likeness (mithāl) of Allah, i.e., something comparable to Him. This is impossible because Allah is unique and exalted above any likenesses. Second, it may be asking about His attribute (صفة), meaning 'how is His attribute?' The answer is that His attribute has no 'how' because that would again be seeking a likeness. However, if the question really means 'Is He described?', then the answer is yes: He is described with the attributes He has described Himself with, such as mercy, knowledge, and power.

Correction: Maturidi clarifies that 'how' either demands a likeness (impossible) or asks about attributes, which are known only from revelation.

Key Takeaway
The question 'how is He?' about Allah either seeks a likeness (impossible) or asks about attributes, which are answered only by divine self-description.

Relational vs Essential Attributes
Maturidi distinguishes relational from essential divine descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi permits describing Allah with relational attributes like nearness and mercy but denies that these describe His essence.

Core Idea
Allah’s mercy and nearness are actions, not essential descriptions.

Maturidi clarifies a subtle but crucial point: we can say Allah is merciful to His friends, but we cannot say mercy is an essential attribute like knowledge or power. This passage explains the reasoning, grounding it in Allah’s absolute independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once through, then the English translation. Note the list of permissible descriptions and the final argument against essential description.
The passage has two clear parts: what is allowed and what is not.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice that Maturidi first lists relational attributes (nearness, aid, etc.) and says it is permissible to describe Allah with them in a specific way. Then he argues why describing His essence with them is corrupt.
The key phrase is 'لأن وصف هذا كله وصف ذاتي جائز' – describing all this is a self-description permissible.
3
Understand the reasoning
The corruption comes from the implication that Allah would need His creation to be praised or benefited. Since He is independent (غني), such descriptions cannot apply to His essence.
This connects the doctrine of divine independence to the theory of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويوصف بالقرب من طريق العون والنصر ، ومن جهة التشريف والتخصيص ومن جهتة الرحمة والإحسان ، ومن جهة التوفيق والإرشاد وهذا النوع ؛ لأن وصف هذا كله وصف ذاتي جائز أن يقال : لم يزل رحيما بأوليائه محبا لهم لوقت كونهم له أولياء ، مبغضا لأعدائه على ذلك ، وأما الوجوه هى حقيقة تلك الصفات يحققها غيره ، لا أنه بذاته يوصف يوصف ، فإنه فاسد ؛ لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون له مدح وتمجيد وتعظيم فيكون له ذلك بغيره ، فيصير بخلقه الخلق ممدوحا منتفعا ، وهو الغنى بنفسه يتعالى أن يكون له بأحد مدح أو نفع ، فلذلك لا يوصف بذلك جل جلاله
And He is described by nearness (qurb) from the way of aid and victory, and from the aspect of honor and specification, and from the aspect of mercy and beneficence, and from the aspect of success and guidance. And this type; because describing all of this is a self-description, it is permissible to say: He has always been merciful to His friends, loving them at the time they are His friends, hating His enemies for that. As for the aspects, they are the reality of those attributes, verified by other than Him, not that He is described in His essence—being described—that is corrupt; because it cannot be free from that He has praise, glorification, and exaltation, so that would be for Him through other than Him, so He would become praised and benefited by His creation, while He is independent in essence, exalted above having any praise or benefit from anyone. Therefore He is not described by that, may His majesty be exalted.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage discusses how Allah is described with certain relational attributes such as nearness (qurb), aid (awn), victory (nasr), honor (tashrīf), mercy (rahma), beneficence (ihsān), success (tawfīq), and guidance (irshād). The author (Maturidi) states that it is permissible to say that Allah has always been merciful to His friends and loving to them, and hating His enemies, but these are descriptions of His actions (or relational attributes) rather than essential attributes. The 'aspects' (وجوه) are the reality of those attributes, but they are verified by other than Him (i.e., they depend on the existence of the objects of these attributes). It would be corrupt to say that He is described in His essence by such attributes because that would imply that He receives praise and benefit from His creation, but He is independent (ghani) and exalted above needing any praise or benefit from anyone. Therefore, He is not described by such attributes (in an essential sense).

Key Terms

عون
نصر
رحمة
محبة
بغض
صفة ذاتية
غنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that describing Allah as 'near' or 'merciful' implies a physical or emotional state in His essence.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies these are relational attributes tied to His actions, not descriptions of His essence.

Checkpoint

A) Because He is not aware of His creation
B) Because it would imply He needs praise from others
C) Because He does not have attributes
D) Because He is not near to anyone

Key Takeaway
Allah's relational attributes (like mercy and nearness) describe His actions toward creation, not His essence, preserving His absolute independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Maturidi says describing Allah's mercy as an essential attribute would imply He is praised by creation—a thought he calls 'corrupt.' Yet when you pray, do you aim to praise Allah as if He needs it? What would it change in your prayer to remember that He is completely independent of your praise?

Why Allah Cannot Be Described by His Actions
Maturidi argues against describing Allah by His actions in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that describing Allah by His actions in pre-eternity is impermissible, as it would imply dependence and change.

Core Idea
Describing Allah by His creation would imply need, which is impossible.

Maturidi addresses a subtle theological issue: can we say that Allah is described by His actions, like 'creating', in pre-eternity? He argues that this is not permissible because describing Him by what He brings into existence would imply that He depends on His creation. This passage presents three arguments against such a description, rooted in the impossibility of change and need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical connectors ('because') that introduce each argument.
The passage is dense, so read slowly.
2
Identify the main claim and three proofs
The main claim is that Allah cannot be described by His effect (maf‘ūl). Find the three reasons: (1) not known in the witnessed world, (2) describing by another implies need, (3) would lead to absurdity.
The reasons are introduced by 'li-mā' (because).
3
Reflect on the implications for tawhid
Consider how this argument protects Allah's independence and eternal attributes. The key is that Allah's attributes are not dependent on creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التقول بفعله أنه لا يجوز أن يكون مفعوله ؛ لما لا يعرف ذلك في الشاهد ، ولا يوصف به ولا يوصف بغيره ، ولما بينا أن الوصف بغيره يوجب الحاجة إليه ويوصف به في الأزل ؛ لما بينا من إحالة التغير والزوال ، ولما لو جاز الوصف بما حال في غيره الجاز الوصف بكل شيء من خلقه ، وذلك ممتنع ، وقد بينا هذا في تقديم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the statement about His action that it is not permissible that He be described by His effect (maf‘ūl); because that is not known in the witnessed world, and He is not described by it nor described by other than it. And because we have explained that describing Him by other than Him necessitates need for it, and He is described by it in pre-eternity; because of what we have explained of the impossibility of change and passing away. And because if describing Him by what inheres in other than Him were permissible, then describing Him by everything of His creation would be permissible, and that is impossible. And we have explained this in the preceding. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of describing Allah by His actions (such as creating). The author (Maturidi) argues that it is not permissible to describe Allah by His effect (maf‘ūl), i.e., by what He produces, because that is not found in the witnessed world (i.e., we do not describe a doer by the done thing in the same sense). He also states that Allah is not described by His action nor by something other than Him. Describing Him by something other than Him would imply need (ḥāja) for that thing. He further argues that if describing Allah by something that inheres in other than Him were allowed, then He could be described by everything He creates, which is impossible. The key point is that Allah's attributes are not dependent on His creation; He is described by eternal attributes, not temporal ones. This was already explained in the preceding discussion.

Key Terms
فعل
مفعول
وصف
حاجة
تغير
زوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since Allah is the Creator, He can be described as 'Creator' in pre-eternity, just as we say 'Allah is Creator'.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between the eternal attribute of creating (qādir, khāliq as attribute of power) and describing Him by the actual created thing (maf‘ūl). The latter would imply temporal dependence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible to describe Allah by His action (like creating) in pre-eternity?
A) Because actions are not attributes
B) Because it would imply need for the creation
C) Because He does not act
D) Because actions are only known through revelation

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes are eternal and independent; He cannot be described by what He creates, as that would imply need and change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If describing Allah by His creation implies need, then how much of your heart's reliance is on created things rather than the Creator? Will you today identify one created thing you depend on excessively and turn your trust to Allah alone?
?
The argument forbids describing Allah by what is temporal. Yet you often describe Him by His actions (e.g., 'He provided for me'). Is your description truly of His eternal attribute, or are you restricting Him to your experience? Repent of confining Allah to your moments.
?
Maturidi warns that allowing one description leads to absurdity. Is there any description of Allah you hold that might be based on your own limited perspective rather than revelation? Ask Allah to correct your understanding and submit to His transcendence.

God's Eternal Intrinsic Attributes
Maturidi affirms eternal intrinsic attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi affirms that God's attributes like mercy and aid are intrinsic (waṣf dhātī) and eternal, not dependent on creation.

Core Idea
God's mercy is eternal, not created at the moment He shows mercy.

Maturidi begins by clarifying that certain descriptions of God, such as closeness through aid, honor, mercy, and guidance, are intrinsic descriptions that have always been true. He emphasizes that God has always been merciful to His friends and hateful to His enemies from eternity. This sets the stage for understanding that God's attributes are not reactions to creation but are inherent to His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on intrinsic attributes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi lists different aspects of closeness and then states they are intrinsic descriptions.
Focus on the phrase 'لم يزل رحيما بأوليائه'.
2
Identify the concept of waṣf dhātī
Maturidi says this description is 'waṣf dhātī' (intrinsic description). Understand that this means the attribute is essential to God, not acquired.
3
Consider the eternal nature
Maturidi uses 'lam yazal' (has always been) to affirm that God's mercy and hatred are eternal, not contingent on the existence of friends or enemies in time.
This counters the idea that God's attributes change with creation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this matters for tawhid: it upholds God's independence and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويوصف بالقرب من طريق العون والنصر، ومن جهة التشريف والتخصيص ومن جهة الرحمة والإحسان، ومن جهة التوفيق والإرشاد وهذا النوع؛ لأن وصف هذا كله وصف ذاتى جائز أن يقال: لم يزل رحيما بأوليائه محبا لهم لوقت كونهم له أولياء، مبغضا لأعدائه على ذلك، وأما الوجوه هي حقيقة تلك الصفات يحققها غيره، لا أنه بذاته يوصف، فإنه فاسد؛ لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون له مدح وتمجيد وتعظيم فيكون له ذلك بغيره، فيصير بخلقه الخلق ممدوحا منتفعا، وهو الغنى بنفسه يتعالى أن يكون له بأحد مدح أو نفع، فلذلك لا يوصف بذلك جل جلاله. ثم التقول بفعله أنه لا يجوز أن يكون مفعوله؛ لما لا يعرف ذلك في الشاهد، ولا يوصف به ولا يوصف بغيره، ولما بينا أن الوصف بغيره يوجب الحاجة إليه ويوصف به فى الأزل؛ لما بينا من إحالة التغير والزوال، ولما لو جاز الوصف بما حال في غيره لجاز الوصف بكل شيء من خلقه، وذلك ممتنع، وقد بينا هذا في تقديم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And He is described by closeness in the way of aid and victory, and from the aspect of honor and specification, and from the aspect of mercy and beneficence, and from the aspect of divine success and guidance—and this type; because describing all of this as an intrinsic description is permissible to say: He has always been merciful to His friends, loving them at the time they are His friends, hating His enemies for that. As for the aspects, they are the reality of those attributes realized by other than Him, not that He is described by His essence—that is corrupt; because it cannot be free from having praise, glorification, and exaltation, so that would be through other than Him, thus He would become praised through His creation, benefiting, while He is self-sufficient, exalted above having praise or benefit from anyone, therefore He is not described by that, exalted be His majesty. Then the saying regarding His action that it is not permissible for it to be His effect; because that is not known in the witnessed world, and He is not described by it nor by other than it, and because we have explained that description by other than Him necessitates need for it, and He is described by it in eternity; because of what we explained of the impossibility of change and passing away, and because if describing by what inheres in another were permissible, then describing by everything of His creation would be permissible, and that is impossible, and we have explained this in the earlier presentation, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument about the nature of God's attributes. Maturidi begins by acknowledging that God can be described with certain relational attributes such as closeness via aid, honor, mercy, and guidance, but clarifies that these are intrinsic descriptions (waṣf dhātī) because God has always been merciful to His friends and hateful to His enemies from eternity. However, he argues that the 'aspects' (wujūh) or specific manifestations of these attributes are realized by other than God (i.e., by creation), not that God is described by His essence in those terms. He deems the latter view corrupt because it would imply that God receives praise or benefit from His creation, which contradicts His self-sufficiency. Then he addresses the claim that God's action cannot be His effect (maf‘ūl) because such a relationship is not observed in the witnessed world. He reiterates that describing God by something other than Himself implies need, while God is described by His attributes in eternity due to the impossibility of change. Finally, he warns that if describing God by what inheres in another were allowed, it would lead to describing Him by every created thing, which is impossible. The argument emphasizes God's transcendence and the unique nature of His attributes.

Key Terms
وصف ذاتى
لم يزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's mercy and love are only present when He interacts with creation.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that these attributes are intrinsic and eternal; God was always merciful, loving, and hating from eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does it mean for God's description to be 'waṣf dhātī'?
A) It is an attribute that changes over time
B) It is an intrinsic and eternal attribute of God
C) It is an attribute derived from creation
D) It is an attribute that only applies in the afterlife

Key Takeaway
God's attributes like mercy and hatred are intrinsic to His essence and eternal, not dependent on creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's mercy towards you is eternal and not dependent on your actions, does that make you feel secure or careless? How does this truth challenge your daily relationship with Him?
?
God has always hated His enemies—those who persist in disbelief. Knowing this, what specific sin or attitude in your life do you now realize places you in dangerous proximity to that hatred? What will you do about it today?
?
The passage says God's closeness includes 'aid and victory.' In what area of your life are you currently striving alone, forgetting that His eternal help is already there for you? What would change if you truly believed that?
