Refuting Attributes Derived from Creation
Maturidi refutes the idea that attributes are realized by creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that attributing God with aspects realized by creation is corrupt because it implies need, change, and contradicts His self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Attributing God with aspects from creation would make Him dependent and changeable.

Maturidi then critiques the notion that God's attributes are realized by other than Him. He argues that if God's praise, glorification, and exaltation were through creation, then God would be praised by His creation and would benefit, which is impossible for the Self-Sufficient. He also argues that describing God by what inheres in another would lead to describing Him by every created thing, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage criticizing extrinsic attributes
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the phrase ' وأما الوجوه هي حقيقة تلك الصفات يحققها غيره'—this introduces the refutation.
2
Understand the first argument: need for creation
Maturidi says if attributes are realized by creation, then God would receive praise and benefit from them, contradicting His self-sufficiency.
3
Understand the second argument: impossibility of change
Maturidi argues that describing God by what inheres in another would imply change (taghayyur) and passing away (zawāl), which are impossible for God.
4
See the reductio ad absurdum
If describing God by something in creation were allowed, then He could be described by every created thing, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويوصف بالقرب من طريق العون والنصر، ومن جهة التشريف والتخصيص ومن جهة الرحمة والإحسان، ومن جهة التوفيق والإرشاد وهذا النوع؛ لأن وصف هذا كله وصف ذاتى جائز أن يقال: لم يزل رحيما بأوليائه محبا لهم لوقت كونهم له أولياء، مبغضا لأعدائه على ذلك، وأما الوجوه هي حقيقة تلك الصفات يحققها غيره، لا أنه بذاته يوصف، فإنه فاسد؛ لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون له مدح وتمجيد وتعظيم فيكون له ذلك بغيره، فيصير بخلقه الخلق ممدوحا منتفعا، وهو الغنى بنفسه يتعالى أن يكون له بأحد مدح أو نفع، فلذلك لا يوصف بذلك جل جلاله. ثم التقول بفعله أنه لا يجوز أن يكون مفعوله؛ لما لا يعرف ذلك في الشاهد، ولا يوصف به ولا يوصف بغيره، ولما بينا أن الوصف بغيره يوجب الحاجة إليه ويوصف به فى الأزل؛ لما بينا من إحالة التغير والزوال، ولما لو جاز الوصف بما حال في غيره لجاز الوصف بكل شيء من خلقه، وذلك ممتنع، وقد بينا هذا في تقديم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

?

?

?

How does the truth of His independence free you from that burden? Determine to worship without expecting any benefit to Him, only out of love and obedience.

Harmful Substances Reveal God's Self-Sufficiency and Power
Maturidi states that harmful things show God's self-sufficiency and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how contemplating harmful substances like fire and water reveals God's self-sufficiency, transcendence above needs, and His absolute power.

Core Idea
Even harmful things contain hidden benefits, proving God's self-sufficiency and power.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument from the natural world: even things that cause harm contain hidden benefits. This observation leads us to recognize God's self-sufficiency (al-ghanā) and His absolute power. Let us examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Focus on how Maturidi connects harmful substances to divine attributes.
Notice the words 'الغنى' (self-sufficiency) and 'القدرة' (power).
2
Identify the two main arguments
Maturidi makes two points: first, harm reveals God's self-sufficiency (He acts only beneficially); second, harm reveals His power (hidden benefits in harmful things). Identify where each appears in the text.
The first is in the first half, the second in the second half.
3
Reflect on the examples
Fire and water are given as examples. Think about how fire both burns and improves food, how water gives life and causes death. Why does this duality point to God's power?
Only God can combine opposites in one substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليعلم من تأمل خلقه على جوهر الضرر والنفع على غناه وتعاليه عن تمسه الحاجات؛ لأن من ذلك وصفه فإنما يخرج فعله على وجوه تنفع ولا تضر وليعلم قدرته على ما يشاء مع ما لا يشاهد من الجواهر الضارة إلا وفيه منافع تعجز الخلائق عن الإحاطة بكنهها، من ذلك النار، مع ما فيها من الإحراق ففيها من إصلاح الأغذية، والماء يجوز أن يكون به حياة كل ذي روح وهلاكه
And let him who contemplates His creation on the substance of harm and benefit know His self-sufficiency and His exaltedness above being touched by needs; because from that is His description: His action only emerges in ways that benefit and do not harm. And let him know His power over what He wills, along with what is not witnessed of harmful substances except that in them are benefits that creatures are incapable of encompassing their essence, such as fire, along with what is in it of burning, yet in it is the improvement of foods, and water: it is possible that by it is the life of every soul and its destruction.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage presents yet another wisdom: contemplation of harmful and beneficial substances reveals God's self-sufficiency (ghanā') and transcendence above needs. God's actions are always beneficial and never harmful in essence, though they may appear harmful. Moreover, it shows God's power over what He wills, because even harmful substances contain hidden benefits that creatures cannot fully comprehend. Maturidi gives examples: fire burns but also improves food; water can give life or cause death. This duality highlights God's power and wisdom.

Key Terms

التعالى
الحاجات
القدرة
النار
الماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harmful things prove God's cruelty or lack of care.
Correction: Maturidi shows that harmful things contain hidden benefits, demonstrating God's wisdom and power, not cruelty.

Checkpoint

B) God's self-sufficiency and power, because they contain hidden benefits
C) That evil is from another source
D) That God is not all-powerful

Key Takeaway
The dual nature of harmful substances demonstrates God's self-sufficiency and absolute power over creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When harmful things happen to you—such as illness, financial loss, or broken relationships—do you instinctively see only the pain, or can you actively search for the hidden benefits God has placed in it? What is one difficult situation you are facing right now where you can thank Him for the benefits you cannot yet see?
?
This lesson says God's actions never harm, only benefit. Have you ever considered a trial in your life to be 'pure evil' or 'meaningless suffering'? Knowing this truth, will you commit tonight to reviewing that trial and asking God to show you its hidden mercy?
?
If every harmful substance contains benefits beyond human grasp, then no tragedy is truly absolute. What tragedy have you buried in resentment that you now need to bring before God, asking for forgiveness for your lack of trust? Write down one hurt you will release to His wisdom this week.

Training Humans Through Harm and Benefit
Maturidi explains the pedagogical wisdom of combining harm and benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains that the combination of harm and benefit in creation trains humans to deal with enemies and friends, and prepares children for religious obligations.

Core Idea
Harm and benefit are divine teachers for human conduct.

Maturidi begins by noting how engagement with harmful and beneficial things distracts from pleasures and teaches difficult lessons. He then explains that God created these as likenesses of enemies and friends to train humans in caution and preparedness, and that children are trained early to ease the path of obligation. This passage lays the foundation for understanding the pedagogical wisdom in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human training
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi describes the effect of harm and benefit on human occupation and relationships.
2
Identify the purpose of creation as training
Maturidi says God created harm as likeness of enemies and benefit as likeness of friends. Ask: why does this analogy matter?
It teaches how to interact with real enemies and friends.
3
Consider the application to children
The passage mentions that children are trained in this from a young age. Reflect on how this prepares them for the age of obligation.
This shows the educational purpose of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهما الشغل عن اللذات والشهوات، وتحمل مثله على البدن عسير، وفي التقصير فيها اختلاف وتفرق، وذلك يعقب المعاداة والمجادلة، وفي الموافقة مولاة ومسالمة، فجعل الله تعالى لهم فيها خلق لهم شبيه الأعداء بما فيها من المضار، ومثال الأولياء بما فيها من المنافع، ليكون بشرهم زاجرا لهم على اعتياد كيفية معاملات الأعداء والأولياء، حتى إذا بلغوا بمثلهم في جوهرهم عرفوا كيفيته من الحذر والتأهب والمعونة والنصر. وعلى ذلك يؤمر الصبيان عند احتمال وسعهم للاعتياد ليسهل سبيل ذلك عليهم وقت التكليف، فمثله في خلق ما ذكر، والله أعلم.
العبادات والأخلاق المحمودة
وأيضا أن الخلق على اختلاف جوهر هم في المضار والمنافع جعلهم الله في الدلالة على مدير لهم حكيم عليم، وعلى وحدانيته كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق من جهة الدلالة والشهادة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
حكمته أن جمع بين الضار والنافع، والخير والشر على تناقضهما في الدلالة على وحدانيته والشهادة بربوبيته واحدا.
وأيضا إنه خلق ذلك ليذل به الجبابرة والملوك، فيعلو بذلك ضعفهم - ولئلا يغتروا بكثرة الحواشى والجنود فيتعدوا حدود الله بما يرون من سلطان في قدرته تسليط من يشاء على من يشاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وليعلم من تأمل خلقه على جوهر الضرر والنفع على غناه وتعاليه عن تمسه الحاجات؛ لأن من ذلك وصفه فإنما يخرج فعله على وجوه تنفع ولا تضر وليعلم قدرته على ما يشاء مع ما لا يشاهد من الجواهر الضارة إلا وفيه منافع تعجز الخلائق عن الإحاطة بكنهها، من ذلك النار، مع ما فيها من الإحراق ففيها من إصلاح الأغذية، والماء يجوز أن يكون به حياة كل ذي روح وهلاكه.
وكذلك [كل] جوهر مر أو إلا فيه دواء للداء المعضل، ليعلم الناظر أن القول بالشر بالجوهر والخير خطأ باطل، بل كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع، فيكون في ذلك أعظم آيات التوحيد. مع ما فيه وجهان: أحدهما القدرة التامة على ملك ما يضر وينفع ليرجى ويخاف، ومن لا يكون كذلك لا به، لأنه لا يتم الأمر يرهب منه ولا يرغب فما عنده، وقد يغلبه من له الأمران أيضا. والثاني العبر وليصبح الأمر والنهي فيكون للنظر والشكر مجال في الأمرين، عظة وعبرة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And by them [the harmful and beneficial] there is occupation from pleasures and desires, and bearing their like on the body is difficult, and in deficiency therein there is disagreement and division, and that leads to enmity and argument, and in agreement there is friendship and peace. So God Almighty created for them in them a likeness of enemies because of the harms in them, and an example of friends because of the benefits in them, so that their good news might be a deterrent to them to accustom themselves to the manner of dealing with enemies and friends, until when they attain their like in their substance they know its nature of caution, preparedness, aid, and victory. And upon that children are commanded when their capacity for habituation is probable, so that the way of that becomes easy for them at the time of obligation. So the like is in the creation of what was mentioned, and God knows best.
Worships and Praiseworthy Morals
And also that the creation, despite the difference of their substances in harms and benefits, God has placed them as indicators of a manager for them who is wise, knowing, and of His oneness, like a single substance in agreement from the aspect of indication and testimony, and there is no power except with God.
His wisdom is that He combined the harmful and the beneficial, and the good and the evil despite their contradiction in indicating His oneness and testifying to His lordship as one.
And also that He created that to humble the tyrants and kings, so that their weakness prevails thereby—and lest they be deluded by the multitude of attendants and soldiers and transgress the limits of God by what they see of authority in His power to empower whom He wills over whom He wills, and there is no power except with God.
And so that one who contemplates His creation on the substance of harm and benefit may know His self-sufficiency and transcendence above being touched by needs; because from that is His description, for His action only issues in ways that benefit and do not harm, and so that one may know His power over what He wills, along with the fact that no harmful substance is witnessed except that there are benefits in it that the creatures are incapable of encompassing their inner reality. Of that is fire, with what is in it of burning, yet in it is the correction of foods, and water can be with it the life of every living soul and its destruction.
And likewise every bitter substance or other contains a remedy for the intractable disease, so that the observer may know that the saying that evil belongs to substance and good is an erroneous falsehood. Rather, every substance from it is harm and benefit, and in that are the greatest signs of tawhid, along with two aspects: one is complete power over what harms and benefits, so that He may be hoped and feared, and whoever is not like that is not [worthy] with Him, because the matter is not complete—one does not fear Him nor desire what is with Him—and he who has both matters may also overcome him. And the second is lesson, so that command and prohibition may be established, and so that there is room for reflection and gratitude in both matters, an admonition and a lesson, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage is a series of arguments (each introduced by 'And also' or a similar phrase) explaining the divine wisdom in creating things with both harm and benefit, and how this testifies to God's oneness. The author begins by stating that engagement with these beneficial and harmful things diverts from pleasures, and that bearing them is difficult, yet they teach humans how to deal with enemies and friends. God created likenesses of enemies (harmful things) and friends (beneficial things) so that humans may learn caution and preparedness. Children are trained in this from a young age to ease the path of obligation. Next, the author points out that despite the difference in substances regarding harms and benefits, they all point to a single wise manager, just as a single substance indicates agreement in testimony. Then he states that God combined harmful and beneficial, good and evil, despite their contradiction, to indicate His oneness. Another argument is that God created these to humble tyrants and kings, so they do not become arrogant. He then highlights that reflecting on creation shows God's self-sufficiency and that His acts only benefit. Moreover, every harmful substance contains benefits beyond human comprehension (e.g., fire's cooking, water's life and death). Finally, the author concludes that every substance has both harm and benefit, refuting those who attribute evil to substance and good elsewhere. This is a great sign of tawhid. He then mentions two aspects: first, God's complete power over what harms and benefits inspires hope and fear; second, these serve as lessons for command and prohibition, reflection and gratitude.

Key Terms
ضرر (darar)
نفع (nafʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of creating harm as a likeness of enemies?
A) To make humans suffer
B) To train humans in caution and preparedness for dealing with enemies
C) To show that enemies are evil
D) To distract from pleasures

Key Takeaway
God created harm and benefit as training tools for human conduct, from childhood to adulthood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a harmful situation, do you instinctively complain or do you consciously ask: 'What is this training me for? What caution or preparedness is God teaching me?' From this moment on, choose to see every difficulty as a lesson in dealing with the enemies of your faith and character.
?
Think of a relationship where you are a source of benefit to someone else. Are you modeling the conduct of a true friend (وليّ) by offering aid and seeking victory for them in righteousness? Repent of any selfishness and intentionally become a training ground for others to learn friendship through you.
?
The passage says children are trained in this from a young age. If you are a parent or mentor, are you actively using everyday harms and benefits to teach your children how to deal with real enemies and friends? Or are you sheltering them from the very tools God created for their growth? Commit today to let one small harm be a teaching moment this week.

Indication of Tawhid Through Opposites and Humility
Maturidi presents three arguments for tawhid from the diversity and combination of harm and benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that the existence of harm and benefit in various substances, despite their differences, points to a single wise manager, and that the combination of opposites also indicates God's oneness, and that this creation humbles tyrants.

Core Idea
Diverse harms and benefits all point to one wise Creator.

Maturidi presents two additional wisdoms: first, that despite the diversity of substances in terms of harm and benefit, they all agree in indicating a single wise manager and His oneness. Second, the very combination of opposites (harm/benefit, good/evil) testifies to His lordship. Third, God created these to humble tyrants and prevent their arrogance. This passage strengthens the argument for tawhid from the order and purposefulness of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify each argument introduced by 'وأيضا' or similar.
There are three clear points.
2
Analyze the first argument: unified indication
Notice that Maturidi says despite differences in substances regarding harm and benefit, they all point to a single manager. How does that support oneness?
A single source of wisdom behind diversity.
3
Analyze the second argument: combination of opposites
The combination of harmful and beneficial, good and evil despite their contradiction indicates oneness. Ask: why does contradiction in creation point to a single creator?
Opposites coming from one source shows His power.
4
Analyze the third argument: humbling tyrants
God created harm and benefit to humble tyrants and kings. Reflect on how this prevents transgression.
It reminds them of their weakness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهما الشغل عن اللذات والشهوات، وتحمل مثله على البدن عسير، وفي التقصير فيها اختلاف وتفرق، وذلك يعقب المعاداة والمجادلة، وفي الموافقة مولاة ومسالمة، فجعل الله تعالى لهم فيها خلق لهم شبيه الأعداء بما فيها من المضار، ومثال الأولياء بما فيها من المنافع، ليكون بشرهم زاجرا لهم على اعتياد كيفية معاملات الأعداء والأولياء، حتى إذا بلغوا بمثلهم في جوهرهم عرفوا كيفيته من الحذر والتأهب والمعونة والنصر. وعلى ذلك يؤمر الصبيان عند احتمال وسعهم للاعتياد ليسهل سبيل ذلك عليهم وقت التكليف، فمثله في خلق ما ذكر، والله أعلم.
العبادات والأخلاق المحمودة
وأيضا أن الخلق على اختلاف جوهر هم في المضار والمنافع جعلهم الله في الدلالة على مدير لهم حكيم عليم، وعلى وحدانيته كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق من جهة الدلالة والشهادة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
حكمته أن جمع بين الضار والنافع، والخير والشر على تناقضهما في الدلالة على وحدانيته والشهادة بربوبيته واحدا.
وأيضا إنه خلق ذلك ليذل به الجبابرة والملوك، فيعلو بذلك ضعفهم - ولئلا يغتروا بكثرة الحواشى والجنود فيتعدوا حدود الله بما يرون من سلطان في قدرته تسليط من يشاء على من يشاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وليعلم من تأمل خلقه على جوهر الضرر والنفع على غناه وتعاليه عن تمسه الحاجات؛ لأن من ذلك وصفه فإنما يخرج فعله على وجوه تنفع ولا تضر وليعلم قدرته على ما يشاء مع ما لا يشاهد من الجواهر الضارة إلا وفيه منافع تعجز الخلائق عن الإحاطة بكنهها، من ذلك النار، مع ما فيها من الإحراق ففيها من إصلاح الأغذية، والماء يجوز أن يكون به حياة كل ذي روح وهلاكه.
وكذلك [كل] جوهر مر أو إلا فيه دواء للداء المعضل، ليعلم الناظر أن القول بالشر بالجوهر والخير خطأ باطل، بل كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع، فيكون في ذلك أعظم آيات التوحيد. مع ما فيه وجهان: أحدهما القدرة التامة على ملك ما يضر وينفع ليرجى ويخاف، ومن لا يكون كذلك لا به، لأنه لا يتم الأمر يرهب منه ولا يرغب فما عنده، وقد يغلبه من له الأمران أيضا. والثاني العبر وليصبح الأمر والنهي فيكون للنظر والشكر مجال في الأمرين، عظة وعبرة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And by them [the harmful and beneficial] there is occupation from pleasures and desires, and bearing their like on the body is difficult, and in deficiency therein there is disagreement and division, and that leads to enmity and argument, and in agreement there is friendship and peace. So God Almighty created for them in them a likeness of enemies because of the harms in them, and an example of friends because of the benefits in them, so that their good news might be a deterrent to them to accustom themselves to the manner of dealing with enemies and friends, until when they attain their like in their substance they know its nature of caution, preparedness, aid, and victory. And upon that children are commanded when their capacity for habituation is probable, so that the way of that becomes easy for them at the time of obligation. So the like is in the creation of what was mentioned, and God knows best.
Worships and Praiseworthy Morals
And also that the creation, despite the difference of their substances in harms and benefits, God has placed them as indicators of a manager for them who is wise, knowing, and of His oneness, like a single substance in agreement from the aspect of indication and testimony, and there is no power except with God.
His wisdom is that He combined the harmful and the beneficial, and the good and the evil despite their contradiction in indicating His oneness and testifying to His lordship as one.
And also that He created that to humble the tyrants and kings, so that their weakness prevails thereby—and lest they be deluded by the multitude of attendants and soldiers and transgress the limits of God by what they see of authority in His power to empower whom He wills over whom He wills, and there is no power except with God.
And so that one who contemplates His creation on the substance of harm and benefit may know His self-sufficiency and transcendence above being touched by needs; because from that is His description, for His action only issues in ways that benefit and do not harm, and so that one may know His power over what He wills, along with the fact that no harmful substance is witnessed except that there are benefits in it that the creatures are incapable of encompassing their inner reality. Of that is fire, with what is in it of burning, yet in it is the correction of foods, and water can be with it the life of every living soul and its destruction.
And likewise every bitter substance or other contains a remedy for the intractable disease, so that the observer may know that the saying that evil belongs to substance and good is an erroneous falsehood. Rather, every substance from it is harm and benefit, and in that are the greatest signs of tawhid, along with two aspects: one is complete power over what harms and benefits, so that He may be hoped and feared, and whoever is not like that is not [worthy] with Him, because the matter is not complete—one does not fear Him nor desire what is with Him—and he who has both matters may also overcome him. And the second is lesson, so that command and prohibition may be established, and so that there is room for reflection and gratitude in both matters, an admonition and a lesson, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage is a series of arguments (each introduced by 'And also' or a similar phrase) explaining the divine wisdom in creating things with both harm and benefit, and how this testifies to God's oneness. The author begins by stating that engagement with these beneficial and harmful things diverts from pleasures, and that bearing them is difficult, yet they teach humans how to deal with enemies and friends. God created likenesses of enemies (harmful things) and friends (beneficial things) so that humans may learn caution and preparedness. Children are trained in this from a young age to ease the path of obligation. Next, the author points out that despite the difference in substances regarding harms and benefits, they all point to a single wise manager, just as a single substance indicates agreement in testimony. Then he states that God combined harmful and beneficial, good and evil, despite their contradiction, to indicate His oneness. Another argument is that God created these to humble tyrants and kings, so they do not become arrogant. He then highlights that reflecting on creation shows God's self-sufficiency and that His acts only benefit. Moreover, every harmful substance contains benefits beyond human comprehension (e.g., fire's cooking, water's life and death). Finally, the author concludes that every substance has both harm and benefit, refuting those who attribute evil to substance and good elsewhere. This is a great sign of tawhid. He then mentions two aspects: first, God's complete power over what harms and benefits inspires hope and fear; second, these serve as lessons for command and prohibition, reflection and gratitude.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
توحيد (tawḥīd)
ربوبية (rubūbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the combination of harmful and beneficial, good and evil indicate?
A) That these come from different sources
B) That God's oneness is testified by opposites
C) That evil is from substances
D) That humans should avoid harm

Key Takeaway
The diversity and contradiction in creation all point to one wise and powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you experienced both harm and benefit from the same event. Did you recognize that both came from one wise Manager, or did you attribute them to different sources? How would your trust in God grow if you saw every contradictory experience as a unified sign from Him?
?
The passage says God created opposites to humble tyrants. Where in your own heart do you find pride or a sense of self-sufficiency? What 'opposites' has God sent your way to remind you of your weakness and His sole power? Will you let those moments break your arrogance or will you resist?
?
When you see good and evil coexisting in the world, do you ever doubt that they come from the same Lord? Maturidi says their very contradiction points to oneness. How can you train your heart to see the hand of the one Creator in every circumstance, especially the difficult ones? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen that conviction?

Hidden Benefits and the Two Aspects of Tawhid
Maturidi reveals hidden benefits in harmful substances and explains two aspects of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that every harmful substance contains hidden benefits beyond human knowledge, refuting the idea that evil comes from substance, and that this points to two aspects of tawhid: God's complete power inspiring hope and fear, and the establishment of moral lessons.

Core Idea
Every harmful thing holds a hidden benefit, proving God's sole power and wisdom.

Maturidi deepens the discussion by pointing to the hidden benefits in seemingly harmful substances, using fire and water as examples. He then refutes the notion that evil is inherent in substance, insisting that every substance contains both harm and benefit. This leads to two key aspects of tawhid: God's complete power over what harms and benefits, inspiring hope and fear, and the moral lessons that enable command and prohibition, reflection and gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on hidden benefits
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the examples of fire and water, and the general statement about every bitter substance containing a remedy.
This shows God's wisdom is beyond human comprehension.
2
Understand the refutation of dualism
Maturidi explicitly refutes the view that evil belongs to substance and good elsewhere. He says every substance has both harm and benefit. This is key to tawhid.
This counters any dualistic tendencies.
3
Analyze the two aspects
The passage ends with two aspects: God's complete power over harm and benefit (so He alone is hoped and feared), and the lesson for command and prohibition (so reflection and gratitude are possible).
These aspects link creation to worship and ethics.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil and good come from separate sources, e.g., substance is evil and God is good.
Correction: Maturidi says every substance contains both harm and benefit, and both come from God, indicating His oneness.

What does Maturidi say about the claim 'evil belongs to substance and good is elsewhere'?
A) It is correct for some substances
B) It is an erroneous falsehood
C) It is partially true
D) It applies only to fire and water

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful or harmful situation, do you instinctively search for the hidden benefit that God has placed in it, or do you complain and despair? Will you today consciously look for that wisdom and thank Him for it?
?
You claim to hope and fear only God, but examine your heart: when you need something, do you first turn to people or means, or do you truly rely on the One who alone has power over harm and benefit? What practical step will you take to strengthen that reliance?
?
If every substance contains both harm and benefit from God alone, then your trials are not random. How will you change your reaction to your current struggle, knowing that it is a direct lesson and admonition from your Lord to draw you closer to Him? What specific act of gratitude will you offer today?

Divine Attributes

God's Attributes and the Perfection of Creation
Maturidi argues from divine attributes to the perfection of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses God's essential attributes to argue that creation contains no real flaw, and why apparent evils are due to the limitations of human intellect.

Core Idea
God's perfect attributes guarantee the perfection of His creation.

This passage from Imam Maturidi lays a foundation for theodicy from a Sunni perspective. By reflecting on God's essential attributes—power, self-sufficiency, knowledge, and wisdom—Maturidi deduces that creation cannot contain true discrepancy or evil. He then uses an analogy with the senses and intellect to explain why we may not always perceive the wisdom behind creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very condensed style here.
2
Identify the four attributes
Find the four attributes of God listed: powerful, self-sufficient, all-knowing, and wise. Note that each is described as being 'by His essence' (بذاته). Ask yourself why Maturidi emphasizes this.
These attributes are essential, not accidental.
3
Understand the logical inference
After listing the attributes, Maturidi concludes that there can be no discrepancy or contradiction in creation. Reflect on how these attributes together rule out any flaw.
This is a deductive argument from God's nature to the state of creation.
4
Examine the analogy of senses and intellect
Maturidi compares the intellect to the senses: each sense has a limited range, and if it falls short, another sense may grasp the object. Similarly, the intellect is limited and may not grasp the full wisdom of divine actions.
This analogy explains why we perceive apparent evil; it is due to our limitation, not a real flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله سبحانه إذ هو القادر عليه بذاته لا يعجزه شي. الغنى بنفسه لا يحوجه شيء ، العليم بذاته لا يجوز أن يجهل شيئا ، الحكيم بذاته لا يجوز الخطأ منه في الفعل ، بطل أن يكون فى خلقه تفاوت تتناقض لديه الشهادة ويتضاد فيه التدبير ، ولزم القول بكل ما لا تبلغه عقولنا بدرك الحكمة بعد أن ثبت أنه أن نعلم أن فيه حكمة بليغة لم يبلغها ، على ما لا يعلم أن كل جانسة من حواسنا جعلت لدرك ما تقع هي عليه ، وإن كانت تقصر ربما عن الإحاطة ، وتجئ خاسة أخرى فتحيط به ، فمثله العقل إذ هو مخلوق محدود لا يجاوز الحد الذي جعل له ، مع ما كان موجودا فيه قبح كل شيء يظهر حسنه وفساد شيء يظهر صلاحه ، فثبت أنه ربما يعتريه ما يمنع عن كنه ما يقع عليه من الحكمة والسفه من فعل لا عن شي.
Allah is exalted, for He is the powerful over it by His essence, nothing incapacitates Him. Self-sufficient by His essence, nothing needs Him. All-knowing by His essence, it is not possible that He is ignorant of anything. Wise by His essence, it is not possible that error occurs from Him in action. So it is invalid that there is in His creation discrepancy that would contradict testimony and oppose governance. And it is necessary to affirm everything that our minds cannot attain of wisdom, after it is established that we know that in it there is profound wisdom that we have not attained. Just as we know that each of our senses is made to perceive what it is suited for, though they may sometimes fall short of full comprehension, and another sense comes and comprehends it. Similarly, the intellect is created and limited, it does not exceed the limit assigned to it, along with what is present in it that [؟] every thing whose goodness appears and the corruption of a thing whose soundness appears. So it is established that perhaps there affects it something that prevents the essence of what it falls upon of wisdom and folly from an action not from something.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument for the perfection of God's creation based on His attributes. Maturidi states that God is powerful by His essence (nothing incapacitates Him), self-sufficient (nothing needs Him), all-knowing (cannot be ignorant), and wise (cannot err in action). From these attributes it follows that there cannot be any discrepancy or contradiction in His creation. Even if we do not perceive the wisdom behind certain things, we must affirm that there is profound wisdom beyond our grasp. He draws an analogy with the senses: each sense is designed for specific perceptions, and if one sense falls short, another may apprehend it. Similarly, the intellect is limited and may be prevented from grasping the full wisdom behind divine actions. Thus apparent evils or flaws in creation are not real flaws but beyond our understanding.

Key Terms
القادر
الغنى
العليم
الحكيم
التفاوت
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this teaching implies that everything in creation is good by human moral standards, or that evil does not exist.
Correction: Maturidi's point is that apparent evils are part of a wise plan beyond human comprehension; they are not real flaws in the sense of contradicting God's wisdom.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from God's attributes of power, self-sufficiency, knowledge, and wisdom?
A) That creation contains real flaws to teach humans.
B) That it is invalid for there to be any discrepancy or contradiction in creation.
C) That humans can fully comprehend divine wisdom.
D) That God is not responsible for evil.

Key Takeaway
God's essential attributes of power, self-sufficiency, knowledge, and wisdom entail that creation is perfectly wise, and any perceived evil arises from the limitations of human intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe suffering in the world, does your heart immediately question God's wisdom? How does Maturidi's argument confront that doubt? Are you willing to trust that God's attributes guarantee perfect wisdom even when you cannot see it? What would change in your life if you truly believed that?
?
You have likely assumed that your intellect can judge good and evil in the world. After reading this lesson, are you ready to admit that your mind is limited and that what seems evil may actually be part of a wise plan? Will you let go of your own judgment and submit to God's wisdom?
?
God describes Himself as powerful, self-sufficient, knowing, and wise. Do you live as if these attributes are true? When you face injustice or confusion, do you respond with trust in His attributes, or do you fall into despair or rebellion? Commit to one concrete way you will remind yourself of these attributes this week.

Divine Attributes

Refuting Dualism and Divine Compulsion
Maturidi refutes dualism and divine compulsion through divine attributes and human limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualism and the notion of divine compulsion by establishing God's perfect attributes and the limitations of human intellect and perception.

Core Idea
God's perfect wisdom and power deny any defect in creation; human judgment is tainted by need and custom.

Maturidi addresses two dangerous ideas: that evil comes from an external dark principle, and that God acts under compulsion. He argues that God's essence is all-powerful, all-knowing, and wise, so His creation cannot have inherent contradictions. Moreover, our limited senses and intellects cannot fully grasp divine wisdom. He then exposes the flaws in judging God by human standards.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the full English translation. This dense argument unfolds in several stages.
Notice the logical flow from divine attributes to human limitation to a direct challenge to dualists.
2
Identify God's attributes listed by Maturidi
Look for the attributes: powerful by essence, independent, all-knowing, wise. How do these negate any deficiency or discrepancy in creation?
Consider why error in action would be impossible for God.
3
Follow the argument about human limitation
Maturidi compares the intellect to the senses: each has a limited scope. Then he critiques how human perception is colored by need, custom, and desire. Finally, see the dilemma posed to the dualist.
The dilemma exposes the inconsistency in the dualist position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم منشته ومحدثه الله سبحانه إذ هو القادر عليه بذاته لا يعجزه شي. الغنى بنفسه لا يحوجه شيء، العليم بذاته لا يجوز أن يجهل شيئا، الحكيم بذاته لا يجوز الخطأ منه في الفعل، بطل أن يكون فى خلقه تفاوت تتناقض لديه الشهادة ويتضاد فيه التدبير، ولزم القول بكل ما لا تبلغه عقولنا بدرك الحكمة بعد أن ثبت أنه أن نعلم أن فيه حكمة بليغة لم يبلغها، على ما لا يعلم أن كل جانسة من حواسنا جعلت لدرك ما تقع هي عليه، وإن كانت تقصر ربما عن الإحاطة، وتجئ خاسة أخرى فتحيط به، فمثله العقل إذ هو مخلوق محدود لا يجاوز الحد الذي جعل له، مع ما كان موجودا فيه قبح كل شيء يظهر حسنه وفساد شيء يظهر صلاحه، فثبت أنه ربما يعتريه ما يمنع عن كنه ما يقع عليه من الحكمة والسفه من فعل لا عن شيء. وبعد، فإن تقدير جهة الحكمة ممن هو محتاج فقير يحبب إليه حاجته ويزين في عينيه فقره ويحسن اشياء قبيحة بالعادة والإلف، وكذلك أضدادها، فإن هذا وصفه من الإحاطة بحكمة الربوبية، ولتلك الآفات أيضا عجز عن إنشاء شيء؛ إذ هو يتقلب بالجوارح ويستعمل الآلات، فأنى يكون لمن ذلك محله في فعله بعد علمه أنه يعمل بقوة أحدثت وعلم أفيد هو التحكم بالعجز والجهل على من هو بذاته قادر عالم بالعجز عن مثله والجهل، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم عليهم في الفصل الأول أن يقال: أيا من النور والظلمة إذا آذته بالانتهاء عنه وينهاه عن ذلك؟ فإن قال: لا أقرّ بسفهه؛ إذ مثله فعل السفيه في الشاهد، كلفه ما لا يحتمل جوهره عنده، فهو سفيه أيضا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإن قال: نعم
Then its Originator and Maker is God exalted, for He is the powerful over it by His essence, nothing escapes Him. Independent by Himself, nothing needs Him. Knowing by His essence, it is impossible that He be ignorant of anything. Wise by His essence, it is impossible that error occur from Him in action. It became invalid that in His creation there be discrepancy, contradictory testimony regarding Him, and conflicting management. And it is necessary to affirm everything that our intellects do not attain by perceiving wisdom, after it is established that we know that therein is profound wisdom we have not attained. For just as it is known that each sense among our senses is made to perceive what it falls upon, even though it may sometimes fall short of encompassment, and another sense comes and encompasses it, likewise the intellect, for it is created limited and does not exceed the limit set for it, given that there exists in it the ugliness of everything whose goodness appears and the corruption of everything whose soundness appears. So it is established that perhaps there befalls it something that prevents it from the core of what it falls upon of wisdom and foolishness from an act not from something. And then, indeed estimating the aspect of wisdom from one who is needy and poor—his need endears itself to him and his poverty is adorned in his eyes, and he makes ugly things beautiful by custom and familiarity, and likewise their opposites. For this is his description of encompassing the wisdom of lordship, and due to those defects also he is incapable of originating something, since he changes with limbs and uses instruments. So how can he who has such a status in his action, after knowing that he acts with a power that is originated and a knowledge that is acquired—be arrogant with incapacity and ignorance over Him who by His essence is powerful, knowing, over incapacity like his and ignorance? There is no power except with God. Then, it is to be said to them in this first chapter: 'O you of light and darkness, when it harms you, do you desist from it and forbid it?' If he says: 'No'—I would affirm his foolishness, for like this is the act of the fool in this world, he burdened it with what its essence does not tolerate, so he too is a fool, and there is no power except with God. And if he says: 'Yes'.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage refutes two related positions: the dualist claim that evil originates from a dark principle, and the idea that God is compelled by external factors. Maturidi first establishes the nature of God as self-sufficient, all-powerful, all-knowing, and wise, which negates any contradiction or deficiency in His creation. He argues that our limited intellects, like our senses, may not fully grasp divine wisdom, but this does not mean wisdom is absent. He then criticizes the human condition—our perceptions are colored by need, custom, and desire—so we are not qualified to judge divine acts. Finally, he presents a logical dilemma to the dualist: if they claim that darkness causes harm but they do not forbid it, they are foolish; if they claim they do, they admit that even their own principle (darkness) is subject to a higher authority. Thus, the notion of compulsion is refuted.

Key Terms
منشئ (Originator)
حكمة (wisdom)
سفه (foolishness)
طبيعة (nature)
إكراه (compulsion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies the existence of evil altogether.
Correction: He does not deny evil; he refutes that it originates from an independent principle or that God is compelled to create it. Evil is part of a wise creation, even if we cannot always grasp the wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it invalid to say that there is 'discrepancy' or 'contradictory testimony' in God's creation?
A) Because such claims are based on sensory illusions
B) Because God is wise by His essence and cannot commit error
C) Because the intellect is limited and cannot judge divine wisdom
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism and divine compulsion by affirming God's perfect attributes and the inherent limitations of human perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust or meaningless, do you instinctively assume God is indifferent or mistaken? How does Maturidi's teaching about human limitation and the distortion of need and custom challenge you to pause before judging His wisdom? This week, commit to asking God for patience and insight before reaching a conclusion.
?
In what specific areas of your life do you trust your own sense of what is 'good' or 'wise' more than you trust God's decree? Are there actions or events you have labeled as 'evil' that might actually be part of a wisdom you cannot grasp? Write down one such event and, as an act of faith, declare that God's wisdom in it is profound and beyond your current understanding.
?
Maturidi says that our perception of ugliness in something that is truly good comes from custom and need. What custom or attachment in your life might be blinding you to the wisdom in God's commands or decrees? How will you actively seek to break that attachment this week to see God's hand more clearly?

Rejecting 'Why Create?' and the Doctrine of al-Aslah
Maturidi rejects the question 'why create?' and the doctrine of al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the question 'Why create?' and the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah, and how he grounds creation in divine wisdom rather than obligation.

Core Idea
God's creation flows from wisdom, not obligation or benefit for creatures.

This passage tackles two related challenges: the very question of why God created, and the Mu'tazili claim that God must always do what is most beneficial. Maturidi argues that both assumptions impose constraints on God that are contrary to His nature. Read carefully as he establishes the foundation for a free, wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: al-aslah, al-hikmah, haqq al-fi'l.
Note the variant spelling 'ليم' for 'لِمَ'.
2
Identify the two targets
Maturidi targets two ideas: the question 'why create?' and the doctrine of al-aslah. Find where he addresses each.
3
Understand the argument against al-aslah
Maturidi argues that claiming God must do al-aslah imposes a right (haqq) on God, making Him liable to blame. Instead, God is Wise (hakim) and His action never leaves wisdom.
Contrast this with the Mu'tazili view that God is obliged to benefit creatures.
4
Grasp the final point about creation
Maturidi asserts that creation is not for the benefit of creatures since non-existence causes them no harm. So creation serves a different purpose—presumably worship and test, as mentioned elsewhere.
This reinforces that God is not under obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا نحن القول بالخلق ، وإحالة السؤال عن ليم خلق ؟ ، وليس لنا أن نزعم أنه لا يفعل إلا الأصلح فيلزمه حق الفعل حتى يلحقه وصف ذم إن أخر أو قدم ، بل الله تعالى إذ هو حكيم لا يخرج فعله الحكمة ، وأما اعتبار الأصلح لغيره إنما تقدير الحق عليه لا تقدير الفعل بذاته، وشال كون الحق إغيره عليه ولا غير بل السؤال عن جملة الخلق ، فالقول فى أنه يناق النفع لهم أو صلاح لهم لا معنى له ؛ إذ ليس عليهم فيما لا يخلقهم ضرر ولا فساد ، فيكون الخلق لما ذكر ، والله أعلم
And we have already stated the statement about creation, and the impossibility of the question 'Why create?'. And it is not for us to claim that He does only what is most beneficial (al-aslah), such that the right of action is incumbent upon Him, leading to a description of blame if He delays or advances. Rather, Allah the Exalted, since He is Wise, His action does not depart from wisdom. As for considering al-aslah for others, it is an estimation of the right upon Him, not an estimation of the action itself. And [؟] the existence of the right upon Him is not otherwise. But the question is about the entirety of creation. So the statement that it is for benefit or gain for them has no meaning, since there is no harm or corruption upon them in not creating them. Thus creation is for what was mentioned, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage addresses the theological question of why God created and refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah (that God must do what is most beneficial for creatures). Maturidi begins by reiterating that the question 'Why create?' is impossible (iḥālat al-su'āl) because it implies a need in God. He then states that we cannot claim God only does al-aslah, because that would impose a right upon God, leading to blame if He delays or advances. Rather, God is Wise (ḥakīm), and His actions are always in accordance with wisdom. As for considering what is beneficial for creatures, that would be imposing a right on God, not evaluating the action itself. The questioner may ask about the creation as a whole, but since not creating would cause no harm or corruption to non-existent beings, creation cannot be for their benefit. Therefore, creation is for reasons mentioned earlier (such as worship and test).

Key Terms
الأصلح
الحكمة
حق الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures (al-aslah).
Correction: Maturidi shows that this imposes an obligation on God, while true Islamic teaching is that God acts from wisdom, not obligation.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the statement that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah)?
A) Because God can do evil
B) Because it would impose a right on God, implying blame if He does otherwise
C) Because God is not wise
D) Because creation has no benefit

Key Takeaway
God's creation is an act of wisdom, not an obligation to benefit creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself questioning why God allows certain hardships in your life, as if He owes you an explanation? How does this lesson's affirmation that God is Wise and not obligated to benefit you change how you should respond to trials?
?
If you truly believe that God created you out of wisdom, not because you deserved existence or because it was the best for you, how does that reshape your sense of gratitude and purpose? Will you live today as if you are here by wise design, or by chance?
?
When you pray, do you ever feel entitled to have your requests granted because you think God 'should' do what is best for you? How does the rejection of al-Aslah challenge that entitlement, and what does it mean to submit to a God whose wisdom may not align with your concept of benefit?

The Two Ways of Divine Wisdom: Justice and Bounty
Maturidi enumerates the two ways of wisdom and explains their nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi classifies divine wisdom into justice and bounty, and why both are encompassed by wisdom.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom has two faces: justice and bounty.

Imam Maturidi begins by explaining that God's wisdom (hikma) is not monolithic; it manifests in two fundamental ways: justice and bounty. This classification helps us understand that God's actions, whether just or bountiful, are always wise and fitting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines the two ways.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two ways of wisdom: justice and bounty. Notice that God's bounties have no end, and actions can be described as either beneficence or justice.
3
Understand the general names
The passage says 'they are two general names for everything the agent does.' Reflect on how justice and bounty are both aspects of wisdom.
Justice is specifically when God does something He could have left undone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فى الحكمة طريقان : أحدهما العدل والثانى الفضل وليس لما يقدر الله من الأفضال نهاية ، فيتكلم في الشىء بأفضل ما يبلغه قوته من الفعل مع ما ليس عليه الأفضال يختص به من شاء . وغير جائز خروج فعله عن وضع كل شيء موضعه ، لكن له درجات : يوصف فعل بعضها إحسانا وأفضالا ، وفعل بعضها عدلا وحكمة ؛ إذ هما اسمان عامان لكل ما للفاعل فعله ، والأول الحكمة لما ذكرت ، وكذلك معنى العدل : إنه خاص من حيث كان له تركه فيفعله منعها محسنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وسؤال القدرة على خلق شيء قبل هذا الخلق يخرج على ما بيناه في الوقت والله على كل شيء قدير ثم عورض بما لم لا كان لم يزل يحدث الأشياء ؟ فأجاب بالذي تقدم ذكره من فساد كون شيء قبل شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وجواب هذا عندنا أن يقال : لو أردت بقولك لم يزل يحدث الأشياء ليكون هي لم يزل ، فذلك محال ؛ لما فيه إثبات قدمها ، وفي قدمها فساد إحداثها . وإن أردت به الإحداث ليكون كل شيء من ذلك لوقت كونه فذلك حق ؛ إذ هو بذاته خالق ، لا بغيره .
Then in wisdom (hikma) there are two ways: one is justice (al-'adl) and the second is bounty (al-fadl). There is no end to what God can bestow of bounties, so one speaks about a thing in the best way his power reaches from action, while what is not upon him? Bounty is specific to whom He wills. And it is not permissible for His action to depart from placing everything in its proper place, but it has degrees: some actions are described as beneficence (ihsan) and bounty, and some actions are described as justice and wisdom, for they are two general names for everything the agent does. And the first is wisdom as mentioned, and likewise the meaning of justice: it is specific in that He could leave it but He does it, [؟] beneficent (muhsin). And there is no power except with God. And the question of power to create something before this creation comes out based on what we explained about time, and God is powerful over everything. Then it was objected: why has He not been creating things eternally? He answered with what was previously mentioned about the corruption of one thing before another ad infinitum. The shaykh (God have mercy on him) said: The answer for this according to us is that it is said: if you mean by your saying 'He has not ceased creating things' that they should be eternal (lam yazal), that is impossible (muhal), because it entails establishing their pre-eternity (qidam), and in their pre-eternity is the corruption of their creation (ihdath). And if you mean by it the creation (ihdath) such that everything of that is for the time of its existence (waqt kawnihi), then that is true (haqq), for He is Creator (Khaliq) by His essence (bi-dhatihi), not by another.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage first classifies wisdom into two categories: justice and bounty. God's actions are either just or bountiful, but both are encompassed by wisdom. Justice means He could leave something but does it, and it is described as beneficence. Then the author addresses the objection that God could have been creating things eternally. He distinguishes two interpretations: (1) If by 'eternally creating' one means that the created things themselves are eternal, that is impossible because it would imply their pre-eternity, which contradicts the concept of creation (something created cannot be pre-eternal). (2) If one means that God creates each thing at its proper time, then that is true because God is essentially the Creator; He does not acquire the attribute of creation from another.

Key Terms
حكمة
عدل
فضل
إحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many ways are there in wisdom?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Infinite

Key Takeaway
Divine wisdom includes both justice and bounty, and both are essential to understanding God's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you immediately see it as part of God's justice, or do you ever consider that it might also contain hidden bounty? How can you train yourself to look for both?
?
If God's bounty has no limit, what is stopping you from expecting more from Him in your daily life? Do you limit your expectations of God's goodness because you feel unworthy?
?
The passage says that every action of God is placed appropriately. When you look at your own life, do you trust that even the difficult parts are placed exactly where they should be? What would change in your attitude if you truly believed that?

Refuting Eternal Creation: The Impossibility of Pre-Eternal Created Things
Maturidi answers the objection of eternal creation by distinguishing two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea that God has been creating things from eternity (lam yazal yuhdith) cannot mean the things themselves are eternal, and how Maturidi distinguishes two interpretations.

Core Idea
Creation cannot make originated things pre-eternal.

Having established the categories of wisdom, Maturidi now addresses a potential objection: if God is all-powerful, why didn't He create things from eternity? The answer distinguishes between the act of creating being eternal and the products being eternal. The latter is impossible, the former is true.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: an objection followed by a two-part answer.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author explains two possible meanings of 'He has not ceased creating things.' Find them and note the key difference.
One interpretation leads to impossibility, the other to truth.
3
Understand the impossibility
Why is the first interpretation impossible? Because it would make created things eternal, which contradicts their being created.
The passage says: 'in their pre-eternity is the corruption of their creation.'
4
Affirm the true interpretation
The second interpretation is that God creates each thing at its proper time. This is true because God is Creator by essence.
This does not imply that created things are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فى الحكمة طريقان : أحدهما العدل والثانى الفضل وليس لما يقدر الله من الأفضال نهاية ، فيتكلم في الشىء بأفضل ما يبلغه قوته من الفعل مع ما ليس عليه الأفضال يختص به من شاء . وغير جائز خروج فعله عن وضع كل شيء موضعه ، لكن له درجات : يوصف فعل بعضها إحسانا وأفضالا ، وفعل بعضها عدلا وحكمة ؛ إذ هما اسمان عامان لكل ما للفاعل فعله ، والأول الحكمة لما ذكرت ، وكذلك معنى العدل : إنه خاص من حيث كان له تركه فيفعله منعها محسنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وسؤال القدرة على خلق شيء قبل هذا الخلق يخرج على ما بيناه في الوقت والله على كل شيء قدير ثم عورض بما لم لا كان لم يزل يحدث الأشياء ؟ فأجاب بالذي تقدم ذكره من فساد كون شيء قبل شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وجواب هذا عندنا أن يقال : لو أردت بقولك لم يزل يحدث الأشياء ليكون هي لم يزل ، فذلك محال ؛ لما فيه إثبات قدمها ، وفي قدمها فساد إحداثها . وإن أردت به الإحداث ليكون كل شيء من ذلك لوقت كونه فذلك حق ؛ إذ هو بذاته خالق ، لا بغيره .
Then in wisdom (hikma) there are two ways: one is justice (al-'adl) and the second is bounty (al-fadl). There is no end to what God can bestow of bounties, so one speaks about a thing in the best way his power reaches from action, while what is not upon him? Bounty is specific to whom He wills. And it is not permissible for His action to depart from placing everything in its proper place, but it has degrees: some actions are described as beneficence (ihsan) and bounty, and some actions are described as justice and wisdom, for they are two general names for everything the agent does. And the first is wisdom as mentioned, and likewise the meaning of justice: it is specific in that He could leave it but He does it, [؟] beneficent (muhsin). And there is no power except with God. And the question of power to create something before this creation comes out based on what we explained about time, and God is powerful over everything. Then it was objected: why has He not been creating things eternally? He answered with what was previously mentioned about the corruption of one thing before another ad infinitum. The shaykh (God have mercy on him) said: The answer for this according to us is that it is said: if you mean by your saying 'He has not ceased creating things' that they should be eternal (lam yazal), that is impossible (muhal), because it entails establishing their pre-eternity (qidam), and in their pre-eternity is the corruption of their creation (ihdath). And if you mean by it the creation (ihdath) such that everything of that is for the time of its existence (waqt kawnihi), then that is true (haqq), for He is Creator (Khaliq) by His essence (bi-dhatihi), not by another.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage first classifies wisdom into two categories: justice and bounty. God's actions are either just or bountiful, but both are encompassed by wisdom. Justice means He could leave something but does it, and it is described as beneficence. Then the author addresses the objection that God could have been creating things eternally. He distinguishes two interpretations: (1) If by 'eternally creating' one means that the created things themselves are eternal, that is impossible because it would imply their pre-eternity, which contradicts the concept of creation (something created cannot be pre-eternal). (2) If one means that God creates each thing at its proper time, then that is true because God is essentially the Creator; He does not acquire the attribute of creation from another.

Key Terms
قدم
إحداث
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'God has not ceased creating things' means the created things themselves are eternal.
Correction: It could mean either the things are eternal (impossible) or that God's act of creating is eternal in the sense that He is always the Creator (true).

Checkpoint
What is impossible according to the passage?
A) God creating from eternity
B) That created things be pre-eternal
C) That God is Creator by essence
D) That everything has a time of existence

Key Takeaway
The claim that God has been creating eternally can only be true if it refers to God's eternal creatorship, not to the things themselves being eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say God is the Creator, but do you live as if creation happened long ago and is now independent of Him? How would your daily reliance on Him change if you truly believed He is creating every moment, including your next breath?
?
What created thing—a person, a possession, a status—are you treating as if it has permanence, as if it could never be taken away? How does this passage correct your heart's attachment?
?
If God's creating is eternal but each thing is created in its time, are you willing to trust His timing for what you desire most? What specific thing are you demanding now that you must wait for?

Answering the Question 'How Did God Create?'
Maturidi answers and repudiates the question of how God created, then discusses two views on creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the question 'how' regarding God's creation is invalid and the two theological views on the relationship between creation and the created thing.

Core Idea
The 'how' of creation is invalid; creation is either the thing itself or an attribute.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: how did God create the universe? Maturidi first clarifies possible meanings of the question, then argues that 'how' cannot apply to God's act because it implies comparison. He then presents two contrasting views on whether the act of creation is identical to the created thing or a distinct attribute. Understanding this helps clarify the uniqueness of divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let Maturidi's reasoning unfold without interruption.
The passage contains multiple layers; do not worry about details yet.
2
Identify the three interpretations of 'how'
Maturidi considers three possible meanings of the question 'how did God create?': manipulation, identity of created thing, and purpose. Notice how he refutes the first and accepts the third.
The second interpretation is more ambiguous; he says if 'created' refers to the thing itself, then the creation is that thing.
3
Understand the repudiation of 'how'
The Faqih's statement directly denies that God's act has a 'how', because having a 'how' implies having likenesses. This is a key theological principle.
4
Analyze the two views on creation
After the repudiation, Maturidi notes a disagreement: some say creation is the same as the created thing, others say it is an eternal attribute. See how each view affects the validity of the question.
The first view makes the question invalid because there is no separate 'act' to compare; the second shifts the question to modality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب لسؤال : كيف خلق الله الخلق ؟ إنه لو أراد به المعالجة في الفعل فهو غير جائز ، بل ابتدعه وأحدث عينه بلا علاج ، ولو أراد أى شيء خلق ، يشار إلى الجواهر من نحو السماء وغيرها إذ خلق الشيء زعم هو ذلك الشي ذلك الشيء. ولو أراد به : لم خلق ؟ فلمنافع الخلق في وما هو أصلح لهم فيما كافهم . وقال الفقيه رحمه الله: جواب هذا السؤال دفعه أن ليس لفعله كيف ؟ إذ كل ذى كيف هو ذو أمثال . ثم القول فى كون خلق الشيء أنه هو أو غيره اختلاف : فمنهم من يقول : هو هو ، وبه يقول . والسؤال على مذهبه فاسد ؛ لأنه لا غير لخلقه فيمثل هو به . ومنهم من يقول : خلق من يقول : خلق الشيء فهو صفته التي وصف بها فى الأزل ، فالسؤال عن كيفيته اهو السؤال عن كيفية ذاته وعلمه وقدرته ، وذلك فاسد .
Then he answered the question: 'How did Allah create the creation?' [He said:] 'If he intended by it manipulation in the act, that is not permissible. Rather, He initiated it and brought forth its very essence without manipulation. And if He intended 'any thing created', pointing to substances like the heaven and others, for when He created a thing, he claimed that it is that thing itself. And if He intended by it 'why did He create?', it is for the benefits of creation and what is most beneficial for them in what suffices them.' And the Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'The answer to this question is to repudiate it: that there is no 'how' to His act, since everything with a 'how' has likenesses.' Then there is disagreement concerning the act of creation: whether the created thing is the same as the creation or other. Some say it is the same, and that is his view. The question according to his school is corrupt because there is no other to His creation to liken Him to. And some say: 'created'—some say the creation of a thing is His attribute which He described in eternity, so asking about its modality is like asking about the modality of His essence, knowledge, and power, and that is corrupt.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage addresses the question 'How did Allah create?' Maturidi explains that the question can be interpreted in different ways. If it asks about manipulation or process, that is impermissible because Allah creates without tools or effort. If it asks about the identity of the created thing, it is that thing itself. If it asks 'why', it is for the benefit of creation. The Faqih (likely another authority) says the question itself should be repudiated because 'how' implies measurement and likeness, which does not apply to Allah. Then Maturidi notes a theological disagreement: some say the act of creation is identical to the created thing, others say creation is an attribute. The first view makes the question invalid because there is no separate 'act' to compare. The second view makes the question about the modality of an attribute, which is like asking about the modality of God's essence—invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's creation involves a process or tools, similar to human making.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that God creates without any manipulation or instruments; He simply brings things into existence by His command.

Checkpoint
What is one reason Maturidi gives that the question 'how' is invalid?
A) Because Allah uses tools
B) Because everything with a 'how' has likenesses
C) Because the question is about the essence
D) Because creation is an attribute

Key Takeaway
The question of 'how' God creates is invalid because it implies likeness; creation is either identical to the created thing or an eternal attribute, both of which defy modal inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial you don't understand, do you find yourself asking 'Why me?' or 'How could this happen?' — and is that question actually a demand for God to justify Himself to you? What would it cost you to simply say 'I do not know, but I trust'?
?
The lesson says that asking about God's act is like asking about His essence—impossible and arrogant. Have you ever caught yourself trying to mentally picture or measure God's attributes? What does that reveal about your heart's need to control the uncontrollable? Will you stop today?
?
If someone asked you 'How does God answer prayers?' and you remembered this lesson, would you be able to refuse to give an explanation and instead point to the One who acts without cause? Practice that refusal now in your heart.

The Problem with Identifying Creation with the Thing Created
Al-Faqih examines and refutes the claim that creation is identical to the thing created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying creation with the created object leads to logical difficulties regarding the meaning of 'creator'.

Core Idea
If creation is the thing itself, then 'creator' becomes meaningless.

This passage from al-Faqih presents a logical objection against the idea that creation is identical to the thing created. He argues that such a view collapses the distinction between God and His creation, and even raises the absurd possibility that the created thing could be called 'creator'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once without analyzing. Note the key claim being refuted: 'creation is that thing'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical problem
If creation is identical to the thing, then nothing proceeds from God except the thing itself. This makes it unclear how God is distinct from His act.
Al-Faqih asks: how then can God be called 'creator'?
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Faqih argues that if creation is the thing, then the thing itself could be considered 'creator' more deserving than God, since nothing from God to it except itself.
This is an absurd consequence.
4
Examine the reply about 'how'
Al-Faqih dismisses the answer that God did not create by manipulation, because the question was about modality, not about what it is not.
The answer avoids the real issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وقوله خلق هو ذلك الشيء ، فاذا الشيء بذات الله أو بذات نفسه ، إذ لم يكن من الله إلا ذاته ، ولا إلى الخلق منه سوى الخلق بذاته ، فكيف صار هو خالقا ولم يكن منه غير الخلق دون أن كان الخلق بلا غيره ؟ ولم لا كان الخلق في أن يكون خالقا أحق منه ؛ إذ لم يكن منه إليه سوى أن كان هو ، وقدم الشيء لا يوجب كون آخر به إذا لم يكن منه إليه ما به يكون في الشاهد كيف أوجب ذلك في الغائب ؟ له وقواه لكيف خلق ؟ لم يخلق بالمعالجة ، وما ذكر كلام لا معنى له ؛ لأنه لم يسأل عما لم يكن ، بل سئل عن كيفية فعله ، فقوله لم يعالج لا معنى وإذا كان عنده أن خلق الشيء هو ذلك الشيء فليذكر إذا في جوابه ذلك الشيء دون أن يقسم السؤال ثم يزيل عنه المفهوم من الكيف ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Al-Faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: And his statement: 'creation is that thing.' So then the thing is by God's essence or by His own essence, since nothing is from God except His essence, and nothing goes from Him to the creation except creation by His essence. So how did He become 'creator' while nothing came from Him other than creation, without creation being other than Him? And why is not creation more deserving of being 'creator' than Him, since nothing comes from it to Him except that it is itself? And the priority of a thing does not necessitate that another thing come from it when nothing comes from it to Him by which it might be, in the witnessed world. How then did it necessitate that in the unseen? And his saying: 'How was He created? He was not created by manipulation.' And what he mentioned is meaningless speech, because he was not asked about what was not, but rather he was asked about the modality of His act. So his saying 'He did not manipulate' is meaningless. And if he holds that creation of a thing is that thing, then let him mention in his answer that thing, without dividing the question and then removing from it the understood meaning of modality. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 727

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the nature of creation and the meaning of the term 'creator.' Al-Faqih begins by examining a statement: 'creation is that thing' (i.e., creation is identical to the created object). He then poses a logical problem: if creation is nothing other than the thing itself, and God only brings forth the thing, then the thing is related to God's essence. But how does that make God a 'creator'? The problem is that nothing proceeds from God except the thing, and the thing is not other than God's act? Actually, the argument is that if creation is identical to the thing, then the thing's existence is by God's essence. But then the term 'creator' seems arbitrary. He then considers an objection: why not say that the creation (the thing) is more deserving of being called 'creator' because it is the source? He then discusses the question 'how was He created?' and criticizes the answer 'He did not create by manipulation,' arguing that the question was about the modality of the act, not about what it is not. He concludes that if creation is the thing itself, the answer should be the thing itself, not a redefinition of the question. The passage is dense and uses logical reasoning to refute a particular understanding of divine creation.

معالجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that creation is identical to the created object.
Correction: Al-Faqih shows this leads to logical absurdities, such as the thing itself being called 'creator'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with saying 'creation is that thing'?
A) It implies God created Himself.
B) It makes it unclear how God is distinct from the creation.
C) It denies the concept of power altogether.
D) It leads to the conclusion that the creation itself could be called 'creator'.

Key Takeaway
Al-Faqih refutes the identity of creation and created by showing it undermines the distinction between God and His act, leading to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that God's act of creation is identical to the created thing, how would that affect your understanding of God's transcendence? Do you treat God's actions as just the world itself, or do you see a distinction that demands humility before the Creator?
?
Al-Faqih criticizes giving an answer that avoids the real question. When you face difficult questions about God, do you tend to give evasive answers that miss the point? Ask yourself: Are you truly seeking to understand the how, or are you satisfied with comfortable phrases?
?
The argument shows that if creation is the thing, then the thing could be considered creator. Does your life reflect a confusion between the gift and the Giver? Are you treating God's blessings (the things) as if they are from themselves, forgetting the distinction that makes Him worthy of worship?
